Divine Attributes

The View that Attributes are Necessary by Essence: Presentation and Critique
Taftazani presents and critiques the claim that all qadim are wajib by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's attributes are necessary in themselves and Taftazani's critique that this violates tawḥīd.

Core Idea
Claiming attributes are necessary by essence conflicts with the oneness of God.

This passage from Taftazani's commentary presents a controversial view from later scholars like Ḥamīd al-Dīn al-Ḍarīr, who argued that both God and His attributes are necessary in themselves. However, Taftazani finds this claim deeply problematic and critiques it on the grounds of tawḥīd and logical consistency. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the flow from claim to objection to critique.
2
Identify the core argument and objection
Identify the claim that both God and attributes are necessary in themselves. Then find the proof given, and the subsequent objection and response.
The proof is that if not necessary, they would be contingent and need a cause.
3
Understand Taftazani's critique
Taftazani points out two problems: multiplicity of necessary beings contradicts tawhid, and the alternative (attributes as possible) conflicts with their own principle that every possible is created.
Consider how this critique defends orthodox Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كلام بعض المتأخرين كالإمام حميد الدين الضرير - رحمه الله - ومن تبعه، تصريح بأن واجب الوجود لذاته هو الله تعالى وصفاته. واستدلوا على أن كل ما هو قديم فهو واجب لذاته بأنه لو لم يكن واجبا لذاته لكان جائز العدم في نفسه، فيحتاج في وجوده إلى مخصص، فيكون محدثا إذ لا نعنى بالمحدث إلا ما يتعلق وجوده بإيجاد شيء آخر. ثم اعترضوا بأن الصفات لو كانت واجبة لذاتها لكانت باقية والبقاء معنى فيلزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى . فأجابوا بأن كل صفة فهى باقية ببقاء هو نفس تلك الصفة . وهذا كلام فى غاية الصعوبة فإن القول بتعدد الواجب لذاته، مناف للتوحيد . والقول بإمكان الصفات ينافي قولهم بأن كل ممكن فهو حادث. فإن زعموا بأنها قديمة بالزمان بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالعدم - وهذا لا ينافي الحدوث الذاتي بمعنى الاحتياج إلى ذات الواجب -
And in the speech of some later scholars like Imam Ḥamīd al-Dīn al-Ḍarīr - may Allah have mercy on him - and those who follow him, there is an explicit statement that the Necessary Existence in itself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī) is Allah the Exalted and His attributes. And they argued that everything that is eternal (qadīm) is necessary in itself because if it were not necessary in itself, it would be contingent (jāʾiz al-ʿadam) in itself, so it would need a specific cause (mukhaṣṣiṣ) in its existence, and thus would be created (muḥdath), for we mean by 'created' nothing other than that whose existence depends on the bringing into existence by something else. Then they objected: if the attributes were necessary in themselves, they would be enduring (bāqiya), and endurance is a meaning (maʿnā), so this entails the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning. So they answered: every attribute endures by an endurance that is itself that attribute. And this speech is extremely difficult, for the statement of multiplicity of the necessary in itself is contrary to tawḥīd. And the statement of the possibility (imkān) of the attributes contradicts their statement that every possible is created (ḥādith). If they claim that they (the attributes) are eternal in time (qadīma bi-l-zamān) meaning not preceded by non-existence, this does not contradict essential createdness (al-ḥudūth al-dhātī) in the sense of need for the essence of the Necessary.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques a controversial view. The author reports that some later scholars, including Imam Ḥamīd al-Dīn al-Ḍarīr, explicitly state that both God and His attributes are necessary in themselves (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī). They argue that everything eternal is necessary in itself, otherwise it would be contingent and need a cause, making it created. Then an objection is raised: if attributes are necessary, they would be enduring (bāqiya), and endurance is a meaning (an attribute) subsisting in another meaning, which is problematic. Their answer is that each attribute endures by an endurance that is identical to itself. The author finds this extremely problematic because claiming multiple necessary beings is against tawḥīd. Also, if they say attributes are possible (contingent), that contradicts their claim that every possible is created (which would make attributes created). Finally, the author mentions an alternative: if they claim attributes are eternal in time (qadīma bi-l-zamān) meaning not preceded by non-existence, this does not contradict essential createdness (meaning they depend on God's essence). This last part is cut off but implies a potential solution.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
القديم بالزمان
الحدوث الذاتي
التوحيد
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes are necessarily existent in the same way God's essence is, implying multiple necessary beings.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that attributing necessary existence to the attributes leads to shirk (multiplicity of necessary beings) or logical inconsistency, so attributes must be seen as dependent on God's essence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main criticism of the view that attributes are 'wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī'?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It leads to multiplicity of necessary beings, which violates tawḥīd.
C) It makes attributes impossible.
D) It is an innovation not found in earlier scholars.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani demonstrates that the attempt to assert the necessity of attributes by essence leads to either polytheism or self-contradiction, reinforcing the orthodox position that attributes are not independent of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered an attribute of God—like His power or mercy—as if it were an independent reality, forgetting that it is inherently dependent on His essence? How might this subtle shift affect your worship and understanding of tawḥīd? What can you do today to correct that in your heart?
?
When you reflect on the fact that claiming attributes are necessary by essence leads to shirk, does it make you more cautious about how you speak about God's attributes? What specific statement about God's attributes that you have heard or made might need correction to align with pure tawḥīd?
?
This lesson warns that a theological slip, even if unintentional, can undermine the very foundation of faith. Is there any area of your belief about God where you have been careless or followed a popular but imprecise statement? What step will you take to ensure your words and convictions align with the tawḥīd of the Salaf?

Al-Qadim and Al-Wajib: Distinction and Extension
Al-Nasafi defines qadim and distinguishes it from wajib in concept and scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between al-qadim and al-wajib in both concept and extension, and why al-qadim is more inclusive by applying to the divine attributes.

Core Idea
Al-qadim is broader than al-wajib—it applies to Allah's attributes, while al-wajib does not.

In this passage, al-Nasafi clarifies the relationship between two central kalam terms: al-qadim (the eternal) and al-wajib (the necessary). While some treat them as synonyms, the author shows they differ in meaning and scope. Understanding this distinction is essential for later discussions on the divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the definition of qadim
Al-Nasafi defines qadim implicitly from wajib: no beginning of existence. Note the conditional statement: if it were originated, it would need a cause.
3
Understand the synonymy claim and its rejection
Some said wajib and qadim are synonyms. Al-Nasafi rejects this because the concepts are distinct. Reflect on why concepts can differ even if they apply to the same subject.
4
Grasp the extension difference
Al-Nasafi explains that in terms of truth-value, qadim is more general: it applies to the attributes, while wajib does not. Multiple eternal attributes are possible, but not multiple eternal essences.
This is a key distinction for the later arguments about attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القديم هذا تصريح بما علم التزاما إذ الواجب لا يكون إلا قديما أى لا ابتداء لوجوده إذ لو كان حادثا مسبوقا بالعدم، لكان وجوده من غيره ضرورة . حتى وقع في كلام بعضهم : أن الواجب والقديم مترادفان . لكنه ليس بمستقيم، للقطع بتغاير المفهومين. وإنما الكلام في النساوى بحسب الصدق . فإن بعضهم على أن القديم أعم من الواجب لصدقه على صفات الواجب، بخلاف الواجب، فإنه لا يصدق عليها، ولا استحالة في تعدد الصفات القديمة، وإنما المستحيل تعدد الذوات القديمة ..
وفي كلام بعض المتأخرين كالإمام حميد الدين الضرير - رحمه الله - ومن تبعه، تصريح بأن واجب الوجود لذاته هو الله تعالى وصفاته. واستدلوا على أن كل ما هو قديم فهو واجب لذاته بأنه لو لم يكن واجبا لذاته لكان جائز العدم في نفسه، فيحتاج في وجوده إلى مخصص، فيكون محدثا إذ لا نعنى بالمحدث إلا ما يتعلق وجوده بإيجاد شيء آخر. ثم اعترضوا بأن الصفات لو كانت واجبة لذاتها لكانت باقية والبقاء معنى فيلزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى .
فأجابوا بأن كل صفة فهى باقية ببقاء هو نفس تلك الصفة . وهذا كلام فى غاية الصعوبة فإن القول بتعدد الواجب لذاته، مناف للتوحيد . والقول بإمكان الصفات ينافي قولهم بأن كل ممكن فهو حادث.
فإن زعموا بأنها قديمة بالزمان بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالعدم -
وهذا لا ينافي الحدوث الذاتي بمعنى الاحتياج إلى ذات الواجب -
فهو قول بما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من انقسام كل من القدم والحدوث إلى الذاتي والزماني. وفيه رفض لكثير من القواعد . وسيأتي لهذا زيادة تحقيق.
Al-qadim (the eternal) is explicit knowledge of what is known implicitly, for the necessary being (al-wajib) is only eternal, i.e., no beginning to its existence, because if it were originated (hadith) preceded by non-existence, its existence would necessarily be from another. Until it occurred in the speech of some of them: that al-wajib and al-qadim are synonyms. But it is not correct, because the two concepts are certainly different. Rather, the discussion among the Nasafis is regarding truth-value. For some of them hold that al-qadim is more general than al-wajib because it is true of the attributes of the Necessary Being, unlike al-wajib, which is not true of them, and there is no impossibility in multiple eternal attributes; rather, the impossible is multiple eternal essences. And in the speech of some later scholars, like Imam Hamid al-Din al-Darir - may Allah have mercy on him - and those who follow him, there is an explicit statement that the Necessary of Existence by essence (wajib al-wujud li dhatihi) is Allah and His attributes. And they argued that everything that is eternal is necessary by essence, because if it were not necessary by essence, it would be possible of non-existence in itself, so it would need a specifier (mukhassis) in its existence, thus it would be originated, for we mean by originated nothing but that whose existence is dependent on the bringing into existence by something else. Then they objected that if the attributes were necessary by themselves, they would be everlasting (baqiya), and everlastingness is a meaning (ma'na), so it would necessitate the subsistence of a meaning by a meaning. So they answered that each attribute is everlasting by an everlastingness that is identical to that attribute. And this speech is extremely difficult, because the claim of multiple necessary beings by essence is contrary to tawhid. And the claim of the possibility of the attributes contradicts their statement that everything possible is originated. If they claim that they are eternal in time (qadimat bil-zaman) meaning not preceded by non-existence - and this does not contradict essential origination (al-huduth al-dhati) meaning the need for the essence of the Necessary Being - then this is an assertion of what the philosophers held, namely the division of both eternity and origination into essential and temporal. And in it is a rejection of many principles. And further investigation of this will come.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qadim' (eternal) and discusses its relationship with 'wajib' (necessary). It begins by stating that 'qadim' is explicit knowledge of what is known implicitly, because the Necessary Being (wajib) can only be eternal — if it were originated (hadith), it would need a cause. Some scholars claimed that 'wajib' and 'qadim' are synonymous, but the author rejects this because the two concepts are distinct. In terms of truth-value, some hold that 'qadim' is more general because it applies to the attributes of the Necessary Being, whereas 'wajib' does not apply to attributes. Multiple eternal attributes are possible, but multiple eternal essences are impossible. The passage then presents the view of later scholars like Imam Hamid al-Din al-Darir, who assert that both God and His attributes are necessary by essence (wajib li dhatihi). Their argument: everything eternal is necessary by essence, otherwise it would be contingent and need a specifier, making it originated. An objection is raised: if attributes are necessary by themselves, they would be everlasting (baqiya), and everlastingness is a meaning (ma'na) subsisting in them, leading to a meaning subsisting in a meaning. Their answer: each attribute is everlasting by an everlastingness identical to itself. The author critiques this as extremely difficult and contrary to tawhid because it implies multiple necessary beings. Furthermore, if they claim the attributes are eternal in time but not in essence, that is the philosophers' view dividing eternity and origination into essential and temporal, which rejects many principles. The author promises further investigation.

Key Terms
القديم
الواجب
الحدوث
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-wajib and al-qadim are synonyms.
Correction: While they share the same subject (God), the concepts differ: qadim means eternal, wajib means necessary. Moreover, qadim applies to attributes, wajib does not.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the relationship between al-qadim and al-wajib in terms of extension?
A) They are identical.
B) Al-qadim is more general.
C) Al-wajib is more general.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Al-qadim is broader than al-wajib, applying to both God's essence and attributes, while al-wajib applies only to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nasafi insists that multiple eternal attributes are possible, but multiple eternal essences are impossible. In your heart, do you ever treat God's attributes as if they were separate beings? How does this lesson correct your concept of tawḥīd?
?
If al-wajib and al-qadim are not synonyms, what does that mean for the way you think about God's eternity? Is there any part of your faith that assumes anything eternal must be God? How would you respond to someone who calls the Quran 'eternal' but denies it is created?
?
The passage says 'there is no impossibility in multiple eternal attributes.' How does that make you feel about the richness of God's nature? Do you ever reduce God to a single attribute in your worship? Commit to reflecting on all His attributes as truly multiple yet one.

The Later View: Attributes as Necessary by Essence
Al-Nasafi presents and critiques the view that attributes are necessary by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the divine attributes are necessary by essence, the objection that this leads to a meaning subsisting in a meaning, and the response to that objection.

Core Idea
Later scholars argued that all eternal beings are necessary by essence, including the attributes, but this raises the problem of meanings subsisting in meanings.

Some later scholars, like Imam Hamid al-Din al-Darir, took a strong position: not only God but also His attributes are necessary by essence. They based this on the principle that whatever is eternal cannot be contingent. This lesson presents their argument, an objection about everlastingness, and their attempted response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: claim, argument, objection, response, author's critique.
2
Identify the claim and its basis
The claim: attributes are necessary by essence. The argument: if eternal, then necessary; otherwise contingent and originated.
The key premise is that everything eternal must be necessary.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection: if attributes are necessary, they are everlasting (baqiya). Everlastingness is a meaning (ma'na). This would lead to meaning subsisting in meaning.
4
Examine the response
The response: each attribute's everlastingness is identical to that attribute itself.
5
Note the author's critique
Al-Nasafi criticizes this as extremely difficult and contrary to tawhid because it implies multiple necessary beings. Also, if attributes were possible, that would contradict the principle that every possible is originated.
This shows the tension in the later view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القديم هذا تصريح بما علم التزاما إذ الواجب لا يكون إلا قديما أى لا ابتداء لوجوده إذ لو كان حادثا مسبوقا بالعدم، لكان وجوده من غيره ضرورة . حتى وقع في كلام بعضهم : أن الواجب والقديم مترادفان . لكنه ليس بمستقيم، للقطع بتغاير المفهومين. وإنما الكلام في النساوى بحسب الصدق . فإن بعضهم على أن القديم أعم من الواجب لصدقه على صفات الواجب، بخلاف الواجب، فإنه لا يصدق عليها، ولا استحالة في تعدد الصفات القديمة، وإنما المستحيل تعدد الذوات القديمة ..
وفي كلام بعض المتأخرين كالإمام حميد الدين الضرير - رحمه الله - ومن تبعه، تصريح بأن واجب الوجود لذاته هو الله تعالى وصفاته. واستدلوا على أن كل ما هو قديم فهو واجب لذاته بأنه لو لم يكن واجبا لذاته لكان جائز العدم في نفسه، فيحتاج في وجوده إلى مخصص، فيكون محدثا إذ لا نعنى بالمحدث إلا ما يتعلق وجوده بإيجاد شيء آخر. ثم اعترضوا بأن الصفات لو كانت واجبة لذاتها لكانت باقية والبقاء معنى فيلزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى .

The author promises further investigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if attributes are eternal, they must be necessary by essence.
Correction: The author argues this leads to multiple necessary beings, which violates the unity of God. Instead, attributes are eternal but not necessary in the same sense as God's essence.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the view that attributes are necessary by essence?
A) It makes attributes created.
B) It implies attributes are everlasting and everlastingness is a meaning, leading to meaning subsisting in meaning.
C) It denies the attributes.
D) It is against revelation.

Key Takeaway
The claim that attributes are necessary by essence leads to the problem of multiple necessary beings and a meaning subsisting in a meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God’s attributes like ‘knowledge’ or ‘power,’ do you ever subconsciously picture them as separate ‘things’ rather than essential aspects of His being? How does this lesson’s warning about multiple necessary beings challenge you to purify your mental image of God’s oneness?
?
If someone today argued that God’s attributes are ‘necessary by essence’ in the same way God is, would you be able to explain why this might lead to a violation of tawhid? What specific words from Al-Nasafi would you use to correct them?
?
This debate shows that even great scholars struggled to avoid errors in discussing God’s attributes. Have you ever held a belief about Allah that, upon deeper reflection, might have subtly compromised His uniqueness? What concrete step will you take this week to ensure your understanding of God’s essence and attributes aligns with the teachings of the Salaf?

The Philosophers' Division of Eternity and Origination
Al-Nasafi critiques the philosophers' division of eternity and origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' distinction between essential and temporal eternity and origination, and why it is problematic for kalam.

Core Idea
The philosophers divided eternity and origination into essential and temporal, a view that undermines key kalam principles.

Finally, al-Nasafi addresses a possible retort: what if the attributes are called eternal only in the sense of being without temporal beginning, while being originated in essence (needing God)? This, he explains, is exactly the philosophers' division of eternity and origination, which rejects many established kalam principles. He promises further investigation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a short but dense passage.
2
Understand the hypothetical claim
Some might say the attributes are eternal in time (qadimat bil-zaman) but not in essence (i.e., they have no temporal beginning but are intrinsically dependent on God).
3
Recognize the philosophical origin
Al-Nasafi points out that this is the view of the philosophers, who divide eternity and origination into essential and temporal.
4
Grasp the rejection
Al-Nasafi states that this view rejects many principles (qawa'id) of kalam. He does not elaborate here but promises further investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القديم هذا تصريح بما علم التزاما إذ الواجب لا يكون إلا قديما أى لا ابتداء لوجوده إذ لو كان حادثا مسبوقا بالعدم، لكان وجوده من غيره ضرورة . حتى وقع في كلام بعضهم : أن الواجب والقديم مترادفان . لكنه ليس بمستقيم، للقطع بتغاير المفهومين. وإنما الكلام في النساوى بحسب الصدق . فإن بعضهم على أن القديم أعم من الواجب لصدقه على صفات الواجب، بخلاف الواجب، فإنه لا يصدق عليها، ولا استحالة في تعدد الصفات القديمة، وإنما المستحيل تعدد الذوات القديمة ..
وفي كلام بعض المتأخرين كالإمام حميد الدين الضرير - رحمه الله - ومن تبعه، تصريح بأن واجب الوجود لذاته هو الله تعالى وصفاته. واستدلوا على أن كل ما هو قديم فهو واجب لذاته بأنه لو لم يكن واجبا لذاته لكان جائز العدم في نفسه، فيحتاج في وجوده إلى مخصص، فيكون محدثا إذ لا نعنى بالمحدث إلا ما يتعلق وجوده بإيجاد شيء آخر. ثم اعترضوا بأن الصفات لو كانت واجبة لذاتها لكانت باقية والبقاء معنى فيلزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى .
فأجابوا بأن كل صفة فهى باقية ببقاء هو نفس تلك الصفة . وهذا كلام فى غاية الصعوبة فإن القول بتعدد الواجب لذاته، مناف للتوحيد . والقول بإمكان الصفات ينافي قولهم بأن كل ممكن فهو حادث.
فإن زعموا بأنها قديمة بالزمان بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالعدم -
وهذا لا ينافي الحدوث الذاتي بمعنى الاحتياج إلى ذات الواجب -
فهو قول بما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من انقسام كل من القدم والحدوث إلى الذاتي والزماني. وفيه رفض لكثير من القواعد . وسيأتي لهذا زيادة تحقيق.
Al-qadim (the eternal) is explicit knowledge of what is known implicitly, for the necessary being (al-wajib) is only eternal, i.e., no beginning to its existence, because if it were originated (hadith) preceded by non-existence, its existence would necessarily be from another. Until it occurred in the speech of some of them: that al-wajib and al-qadim are synonyms. But it is not correct, because the two concepts are certainly different. Rather, the discussion among the Nasafis is regarding truth-value. For some of them hold that al-qadim is more general than al-wajib because it is true of the attributes of the Necessary Being, unlike al-wajib, which is not true of them, and there is no impossibility in multiple eternal attributes; rather, the impossible is multiple eternal essences. And in the speech of some later scholars, like Imam Hamid al-Din al-Darir - may Allah have mercy on him - and those who follow him, there is an explicit statement that the Necessary of Existence by essence (wajib al-wujud li dhatihi) is Allah and His attributes. And they argued that everything that is eternal is necessary by essence, because if it were not necessary by essence, it would be possible of non-existence in itself, so it would need a specifier (mukhassis) in its existence, thus it would be originated, for we mean by originated nothing but that whose existence is dependent on the bringing into existence by something else. Then they objected that if the attributes were necessary by themselves, they would be everlasting (baqiya), and everlastingness is a meaning (ma'na), so it would necessitate the subsistence of a meaning by a meaning. So they answered that each attribute is everlasting by an everlastingness that is identical to that attribute. And this speech is extremely difficult, because the claim of multiple necessary beings by essence is contrary to tawhid. And the claim of the possibility of the attributes contradicts their statement that everything possible is originated. If they claim that they are eternal in time (qadimat bil-zaman) meaning not preceded by non-existence - and this does not contradict essential origination (al-huduth al-dhati) meaning the need for the essence of the Necessary Being - then this is an assertion of what the philosophers held, namely the division of both eternity and origination into essential and temporal. And in it is a rejection of many principles. And further investigation of this will come.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qadim' (eternal) and discusses its relationship with 'wajib' (necessary). It begins by stating that 'qadim' is explicit knowledge of what is known implicitly, because the Necessary Being (wajib) can only be eternal — if it were originated (hadith), it would need a cause. Some scholars claimed that 'wajib' and 'qadim' are synonymous, but the author rejects this because the two concepts are distinct. In terms of truth-value, some hold that 'qadim' is more general because it applies to the attributes of the Necessary Being, whereas 'wajib' does not apply to attributes. Multiple eternal attributes are possible, but multiple eternal essences are impossible. The passage then presents the view of later scholars like Imam Hamid al-Din al-Darir, who assert that both God and His attributes are necessary by essence (wajib li dhatihi). Their argument: everything eternal is necessary by essence, otherwise it would be contingent and need a specifier, making it originated. An objection is raised: if attributes are necessary by themselves, they would be everlasting (baqiya), and everlastingness is a meaning (ma'na) subsisting in them, leading to a meaning subsisting in a meaning. Their answer: each attribute is everlasting by an everlastingness identical to itself. The author critiques this as extremely difficult and contrary to tawhid because it implies multiple necessary beings. Furthermore, if they claim the attributes are eternal in time but not in essence, that is the philosophers' view dividing eternity and origination into essential and temporal, which rejects many principles. The author promises further investigation.

Key Terms
القديم بالزمان
الحدوث الذاتي
الفلاسفة
القواعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the attributes are eternal in time but originated in essence is acceptable.
Correction: Al-Nasafi rejects this as a philosophical innovation that undermines kalam principles.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is problematic about the view that attributes are eternal in time but not in essence?
A) It is the same as the philosophers' view and rejects many principles.
B) It is too complicated.
C) It makes the attributes more than one.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The division of eternity into essential and temporal is a philosophical doctrine that contradicts foundational kalam principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself trying to 'split' God's attributes—accepting one aspect while downplaying another—to make His nature more palatable to modern logic? What area of your belief is most vulnerable to this philosophical division?
?
If you truly believe that every attribute of God is eternal and not contingent, how would that transform your prayer life tonight? Will you thank Him for His knowledge, power, and mercy as if each is inseparable from His essence?
?
Al-Nasafi warns that a seemingly harmless distinction can 'reject many principles.' Are there small compromises in your faith that you have dismissed as harmless? Will you ask God to reveal them before they undermine your foundation?

The Six Essential Attributes of Allah
Al-Nasafi lists the essential attributes and gives rational and scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six essential attributes of Allah as affirmed by Al-Nasafi and the rational and scriptural proofs for them.

Core Idea
The perfect order of creation demands a Creator with life, power, knowledge, hearing, sight, and will.

Al-Nasafi begins by naming six attributes that are essential for Allah. He then gives a rational argument: our intellect judges that the Maker of such a perfect world must possess these attributes. He also notes that revelation confirms them and explains which attributes can be known by reason alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the content.
Notice the concise style of Al-Nasafi.
2
Identify the six attributes
List the six attributes mentioned by Al-Nasafi and note their Arabic names.
The attributes are: الحى, القادر, العليم, السميع, البصير, الشائي.
3
Understand the rational proof
Examine the rational argument: why does the order of the world necessitate these attributes? Use the commentary.
The argument is based on intellectual intuition (بديهية العقل).
4
Distinguish between rational and scriptural proofs
Notice that some attributes like tawhid can be known by reason, while others require revelation for confirmation.
The passage says the Shari'a brings them, but some do not depend on the Shari'a for proof.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why it is important that these attributes are rationally necessary and not arbitrary.
This shows that the attributes are not just revealed but also rationally grounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحى القادر العليم السميع البصير الشائي أي المريد لأن بديهية العقل جازمة بأن محدث العالم على هذا النمط البديع والنظام المحكم مع ما يشتمل عليه من الأفعال المتقنة، والنقوش المستحسنة، لا يكون بدون هذه الصفات.
على أن أضدادها نقائص، يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنها.
وأيضا قد ورد الشرع بها وبعضها مما لا يتوقف ثبوت الشرع عليها فيصح التمسك بالشرع فيها كالتوحيد، بخلاف وجود الصانع وكلامه ونحو ذلك مما يتوقف ثبوت الشرع عليه.
The Living, the Powerful, the Knowing, the Hearing, the Seeing, the Willing (al-Shai') i.e., the Intender, because the intellectual intuition (badihiyyat al-'aql) is decisive that the Maker of the world (muhdith al-'alam) in this wonderful pattern and precise system, along with what it contains of perfect actions and beautiful inscriptions, cannot be without these attributes. Moreover, their opposites are deficiencies that Allah must be exalted above. Also, the Shari'a has brought them, and some of them are such that the establishment of the Shari'a does not depend on them, so it is valid to rely on the Shari'a for them, like tawhid, unlike the existence of the Maker and His speech and similar matters on which the establishment of the Shari'a depends.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage affirms six essential attributes of Allah: life (hayat), power (qudra), knowledge ('ilm), hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and will (irada, expressed as 'shai'' meaning 'willing'). It provides a rational proof: the intellectual intuition (badihiyyat al-'aql) tells us that the Creator of such an orderly and perfect world must possess these attributes. Moreover, the opposites of these attributes are imperfections (naqa'is) from which Allah must be free. The passage also notes that the Shari'a (revealed law) mentions these attributes. Some attributes, like tawhid (oneness), can be known independently of revelation, but the existence of the Maker (al-Sani') and His speech (kalam) are themselves necessary for the establishment of the Shari'a.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You claim to believe Allah sees and hears everything. But are you living as if He doesn't? Think of a private thought or action you engaged in recently that you would be ashamed to have witnessed. How will you align your heart with the reality that His sight is always upon you?

The Necessity of Allah's Attributes: Reason and Revelation
Sanusi argues rationally and scripturally for the necessity of Allah's seven positive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the perfect order of the world rationally necessitates that the Creator possesses attributes like life, power, and will, and how revelation confirms this without circular reasoning.

Core Idea
The world's perfection demands a perfect Creator with attributes.

This lesson presents a key argument from classical kalam: the intricate design of the universe forces our rational intuition to conclude that its Maker must possess certain attributes. The author Sanusi then shows that revelation independently confirms these attributes, while carefully avoiding circular reasoning. This passage is foundational for understanding how reason and revelation work together in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage in Arabic slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the list of attributes and the argument structure.
The author uses two main proofs: rational and scriptural.
2
Analyze the rational proof
Identify the core rational argument: the world's perfection (wondrous pattern, perfected acts) requires a Creator with life, power, knowledge, etc. The opposite would be deficiencies, which cannot be attributed to Allah.
This is a teleological argument.
3
Understand the scriptural proof and methodological nuance
The author adds that revelation confirms these attributes, but makes a distinction: some attributes (like tawhid) are known by reason independently, so revelation can be used as evidence for them without circularity. Others (like existence of the Creator) are necessary for the validity of revelation itself.
This shows sophisticated methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحى القادر العليم السميع البصير الشائي أي المريد لأن بديهية العقل جازمة بأن محدث العالم على هذا النمط البديع والنظام المحكم مع ما يشتمل عليه من الأفعال المتقنة، والنقوش المستحسنة، لا يكون بدون هذه الصفات. على أن أضدادها نقائص، يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنها . وأيضا قد ورد الشرع بها وبعضها مما لا يتوقف ثبوت الشرع عليها فيصح التمسك بالشرع فيها كالتوحيد ، بخلاف وجود الصانع وكلامه ونحو ذلك مما يتوقف ثبوت الشرع عليه .
The Living, the Powerful, the Knowing, the Hearing, the Seeing, the Willing—i.e., the Intender—because the intuitive judgment of reason is decisive that the maker of the world in this wondrous pattern and precise order along with what it contains of perfected acts and pleasing designs cannot be without these attributes. Moreover, their opposites are deficiencies, and it is obligatory to declare Allah exalted above them. And also, the Shari'a has confirmed these attributes, and some of them are such that the establishment of the Shari'a does not depend on them, so it is permissible to use the Shari'a as evidence for them—like tawhid—unlike the existence of the Maker and His speech and the like, which are such that the establishment of the Shari'a depends on them.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a dual proof—rational and scriptural—for seven attributes: life, power, knowledge, hearing, sight, and will. The author first states that reason decisively judges that the Creator of such an intricate world must possess these attributes; their opposites are deficiencies that must be negated of Allah. Then he adds that the Shari'a has also confirmed these attributes, but there is a methodological distinction: some attributes (like tawhid) are independently known by reason, so the Shari'a's validity does not depend on them—rather the Shari'a confirms them. Others (like the existence of the Creator and His speech) are prerequisites for the Shari'a's very establishment, so one cannot prove the Shari'a from them without circularity. This explains how the author uses revelation alongside reason without circular reasoning.

Misconception: One might think the author is proving God's existence here.
Correction: Actually, the author assumes the existence of the Creator and proves the necessity of His attributes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The perfection of the world rationally proves that its Creator must possess positive attributes, and revelation confirms this without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says the opposites of these attributes are deficiencies, and Allah is above them. How can you actively replace those thoughts with tanzīh by praising Him as al-Ḥayy, al-Qādir, etc.? What phrase will you repeat when such thoughts arise?
?
Sanusi uses both reason and revelation. Are you more prone to trust your intellect alone or to blindly follow tradition without reflection? How does this lesson call you to integrate both without falling into circular reasoning? What step will you take this week to balance your heart and mind in knowing Allah more deeply?

Permissibility of Using Rational Terms for Allah
Refutation of anthropomorphists and justification of rational terms via consensus and synonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians justify applying terms like 'existent' and 'necessary' to Allah, even when they are not explicitly mentioned in revelation, and how they refute anthropomorphic tendencies.

Core Idea
Not everything said about Allah must come directly from revelation; consensus and language provide valid foundations.

This passage tackles a critical question: can we use rational terms like 'existent' and 'necessary' for Allah when the Qur'an and Sunnah do not explicitly use them? The author defends this practice by appealing to consensus (ijma') and linguistic reasoning. However, he also notes that one of the arguments is open to scrutiny. This lesson will walk you through the argument and its nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the problem and objection
Notice how the author first states the wrong practice of anthropomorphists and Christians. Then he presents an objection: why use rational terms not found in revelation?
3
Follow the author's answers
The author gives two answers: first, consensus (ijma') as a revealed proof; second, a linguistic argument about synonymy and necessary concomitance. Understand each.
The second argument is presented with 'wa qad yuqal' (and it may be said), indicating it is not the author's own but cited from others.
4
Consider the author's caveat
The passage ends with 'wa fihi nazar' (in this there is consideration). Reflect on why the author might critique the second argument.
This shows the critical spirit of kalam scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت المجسمة والنصارى إلى إطلاق الجسم والجوهر عليه بالمعنى الذي يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه. فإن قيل: كيف صح إطلاق الموجود والواجب والقديم عليه ونحو ذلك مما لم يرد به الشرع. قلنا: بالإجماع وهو من الأدلة الشرعية. وقد يقال: إن الله والواجب والقديم ألفاظ مترادفة، والموجود لازم للواجب، وإذ ورد الشرع بإطلاق اسم بلغة، فهو إذن بإطلاق ما يرادفه من تلك اللغة، أو من لغة أخرى. وما يلازم معناه. وفيه نظر.
And the anthropomorphists (mujassima) and the Christians (nasara) went to the application of body (jism) and substance (jawhar) to Him in the sense from which Allah Most High must be declared free. For if it is said: How is the application of existent (mawjud), necessary (wajib), and eternal (qadim) to Him, and the like of that, permissible, while the Law (shar) has not mentioned it? We say: By consensus (ijma), and it is from the revealed proofs (adilla shar'iyya). And it may be said: Indeed Allah, the Necessary, and the Eternal are synonymous terms, and existent is necessary to the Necessary, and when the Law has permitted the application of a name in a language, then it is permission to apply its synonym from that language or from another language, and what is necessary to its meaning. And in this there is consideration (nazar).
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage opens a discussion on which attributes can be ascribed to Allah. It first states that the anthropomorphists (mujassima) and Christians wrongfully apply the terms 'body' (jism) and 'substance' (jawhar) to Allah, in senses that must be negated. Then a hypothetical objection is raised: how can we apply terms like 'existent' (mawjud), 'necessary' (wajib), and 'eternal' (qadim) to Allah, when these terms are not explicitly used in the Qur'an or Sunnah? The answer is that their permissibility is established by consensus (ijma) of the Muslim community, which is itself a valid revealed proof. The author then presents a further argument: the terms Allah, Necessary, and Eternal are synonyms, and 'existent' is a necessary concomitant of the Necessary. If the Law allowed a name in one language, it implicitly allows its synonym in any language. However, the author notes that this argument is open to scrutiny ('wa fihi nazar').

Misconception: Some think that only terms explicitly found in the Qur'an and Sunnah can be used to describe Allah.
Correction: The author shows that consensus (ijma') and linguistic principles (synonymy, necessary concomitance) provide valid grounds for using rational terms, though the latter is subject to scrutiny.

Key Takeaway
The justification for using rational terms for Allah rests on consensus (ijma') and the linguistic principle of synonymy and necessary concomitance, though the latter is not without scrutiny.

How to Understand 'Likeness' Between Creator and Creation
Definition and refutation of a linguistic objection on likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how similarity (mumathala) is correctly defined and why it does not imply total equality between Creator and creation.

Core Idea
Likeness is relative to a specific attribute, not absolute equality in all respects.

The passage begins by quoting al-Bidaya, which states that human knowledge and God's knowledge share nothing in common because one is temporal and the other eternal. However, this raises a puzzle: everyday language uses 'like' for things that differ in many ways. How can both statements be true? The author resolves this by showing that similarity only applies to the particular aspect under consideration, not to every attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow. Notice how the author moves from a strict definition of likeness to a linguistic objection and then to a reconciliation.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two views: one from al-Bidaya that likeness requires sharing all attributes, and another from al-Tabsira that people use 'like' for partial similarity. Understand the tension between them.
The conflict sets up the need for a solution.
3
Study the reconciliation
The author explains that 'total equality' meant equality in the specific respect of likeness (e.g., measure in the hadith), not all accidental properties. This resolves the contradiction.
This is the key insight: likeness is restricted to the relevant attribute.
4
Apply to divine attributes
Reflect on how this applies to God's attributes: God's knowledge is unlike human knowledge because they differ in the attribute of necessity and eternality. Yet they share the name 'knowledge' only in a broad sense.
This protects tawhid while allowing meaningful statements about God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في البداية : إن العلم منا موجود وعرض ومحدث وجائز الوجود ومتجدد في كل زمان فلو أثبتنا العلم صفة الله تعالى لكان الله موجودا وصفة وقديما وواجب الوجود ودائما من الأزل إلى الأبد، فلا يماثله علم الخلق بوجه من الوجوه هذا كلامه . فقد صرح بأن المماثلة عندنا إنما تثبت بالاشتراك في جميع الأوصاف، حتى لو اختلفا في وصف واحد انتفت المماثلة . قال الشيخ أبو المعين في التبصرة : « إنا نجد أهل اللغة لا يمتنعون من القول بأن زيدا مثل عمرو فى الفقه إذا كان يساويه فيه ويسد مسده في ذلك الباب . وإن كان بينهما مخالفة بوجوه كثيرة. وما يقوله الأشعرية من أنه لا مماثلة إلا بالمساواة من جميع الوجوه فاسد . لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «الحنطة بالحنطة، مثلا بمثل ». وأراد : الاستواء فى الكيل ، لا غير، وإن تفاوت الوزن وعدد الحبات والصلابة والرخاوة. والظاهر أنه لا مخالفة. لأن مراد الأشعرى المساواة من جميع الوجوه، فيما به المماثلة، كالكيل مثلا. وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل كلام البداية أيضا. وإلا فاشتراك الشيئين في جميع الأوصاف، ومساواتهما من جميع الوجوه يدفع التعدد فكيف يتصور التماثل؟
He said in al-Bidaya: 'Knowledge from us is existent, accidental, originated, contingent, and renewed in every time. If we affirm knowledge as an attribute of Allah the Exalted, then Allah would be existent, an attribute, eternal, necessary of existence, and perpetual from eternity to eternity. So the knowledge of creation is not similar to it in any respect.' This is his statement. He clearly stated that likeness according to us is only established by sharing in all attributes, so that if they differ in one attribute, likeness is negated. Shaykh Abu al-Mu'in said in al-Tabsira: 'We find that the people of language do not refrain from saying that Zayd is like 'Amr in fiqh if he equals him in it and takes his place in that area, even if there are differences in many aspects. What the Ash'aris say — that there is no likeness except by equality in all respects — is erroneous. Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "Wheat for wheat, like for like." He intended equality in measure, nothing else, even if weight, number of grains, hardness, and softness differ.' And the apparent view is that there is no contradiction. Because the Ash'ari intended equality in all respects, in the aspect of likeness, such as measure, for example. And on this basis should the statement of al-Bidaya also be interpreted. Otherwise, the sharing of two things in all attributes and their equality in all respects would negate multiplicity; how then can likeness be conceived?
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the correct understanding of similarity (mumathala) when comparing the attributes of the Creator to those of creation. It begins by quoting al-Bidaya, which states that human knowledge is created and contingent, while God's knowledge is eternal and necessary, so they are not similar in any way. The author comments that this means likeness is only established when two things share all attributes; if they differ in even one attribute, likeness is negated. Then a potential objection is raised from linguistic usage: people say Zayd is like Amr in fiqh even though they differ in many other aspects. This would seem to contradict the claim that likeness requires total equality. The author quotes Shaykh Abu al-Mu'in from al-Tabsira, who argues that the Ash'ari position that likeness requires equality in all respects is false, citing the hadith about wheat being exchanged like for like, where 'like' refers only to equality in measure, not in every property. The author then reconciles the two positions: the Ash'ari meant equality in the specific aspect that grounds the likeness (like measure in the transaction), not in all accidental properties. Therefore, when al-Bidaya says God's knowledge is not like human knowledge, it means they are not equal in the aspect of knowledge itself, because God's knowledge is eternal and necessary while human knowledge is temporal and contingent. The passage concludes that if likeness required complete equality in all attributes, it would be impossible for two distinct things to be alike, which is absurd. Thus, the correct view is that likeness is relative to the specific respect in which similarity is claimed.

Key Terms
المماثلة (mumathala)
الاشتراك (ishitirak)
المساواة (musawa)
الكيل (kayl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That similarity in one attribute implies similarity in all attributes, or that total equality is required for any likeness.
Correction: Likeness is restricted to the particular attribute under consideration; total equality would negate multiplicity and is not needed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Prophet (peace be upon him) intend by 'like for like' in the hadith about wheat?
A) Equality in weight and number of grains
B) Equality in every possible attribute
C) Equality in measure (kayl) only
D) Equality in type only

Key Takeaway
Likeness between Creator and creation is only denied in the specific attribute under consideration, not in all possible aspects; otherwise, no two distinct things could be alike.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is not like anything,' does your heart truly resonate with that, or do you still entertain a mental image of Him? What hidden comparisons might you be carrying? Confront them now and repent.
?
This lesson shows that even scholars must be precise about language. In your own speech about Allah, are you careful not to unintentionally imply a likeness? Will you commit to pausing before describing Allah and asking yourself if your words could be misleading?
?
How often do you reflect on the reality of Allah's knowledge—eternal, necessary, and encompassing all? If you truly believed that His knowledge is not like your limited, forgetful mind, would you rely more on His guidance and less on your own understanding? What one change can you make today to live that truth?

Proof that God Has Attributes Additional to His Essence
The author proves God's attributes using the logic of derived terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the use of derived attributes (like 'Knower') proves that God has real, distinct attributes (knowledge, power, life) that are additional to His essence.

Core Idea
Derived names point to real attributes—God is Knower, so He truly has knowledge.

This passage presents a logical proof from everyday language. We routinely describe God as Knowing, Living, Powerful. The author argues that these descriptions are not empty titles; they reflect real meanings that go beyond the simple fact that God exists. Understanding this reasoning is crucial for defending the classical Sunni position on divine attributes against those who deny their reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style; each sentence is packed with meaning.
2
Identify the claim and evidence
The author asserts that God has attributes. The evidence is that we say He is Knowing, Living, Powerful. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the key concept
Focus on the phrase 'صدق المشتق على الشيء يقتضى ثبوت مأخذ الاشتقاق له'. This is the logical principle: if a derivative attribute (like 'Knower') is truthfully applied, the source of derivation (knowledge) must exist in that thing.
This principle is central to the proof.
4
Apply the argument
Now see how the principle works for each attribute: since God is 'Alim (Knower), He must have knowledge; since He is Qadir (Powerful), He must have power; since He is Hayy (Living), He must have life.
5
Review the conclusion
The passage ends with 'فثبت له صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة'. Confirm that the conclusion follows from the principle and evidence.
This refutes the view that attributes are identical to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله صفات) لما ثبت من أنه عالم حى قادر إلى غير ذلك ومعلوم أن كلا من ذلك، يدل على معنى زائد على مفهوم الواجب، وليس الكل الفاظا مترادفة، وأن صدق المشتق على الشيء يقتضى ثبوت مأخذ الاشتقاق له، فثبت له صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة . وغير ذلك .
And He has attributes. Because it has been established that He is Knowing, Living, Powerful, and so on. And it is known that each of these indicates a meaning additional to the concept of the Necessary, and they are not all synonymous expressions. And that the truthfulness of a derivative attribute upon a thing necessitates the establishment of the source of derivation for it. Hence, the attributes of knowledge, power, life, and others are established for Him.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the reality of God's attributes as distinct from His essence (sifat ma'nawiyya). It begins by stating that God has attributes (sifat). The evidence is that we describe God as Knowing (alim), Living (hayy), Powerful (qadir), etc. These predicates are not mere names; each indicates a meaning (ma'na) that is additional to the mere concept of the Necessary Being. If they were synonymous, they would be redundant. The key argument is: when we call something 'knowing' (mushtaq), truthfulness implies that there is a real basis for that derivation (makhadh al-ishtiqaq), i.e., the attribute of knowledge. So from the fact that God is described as Knowing, we deduce that He really has knowledge. Similarly for power, life, etc. This refutes those who claim that God's attributes are identical with His essence (e.g., some Mu'tazila) or that they are just names.

Key Terms
الصفات (al-sifat) - attributes
المشتق (al-mushtaq) - derived attribute
مأخذ الاشتقاق (makhadh al-ishtiqaq) - source of derivation
المعنى (al-ma'na) - meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling God 'Knower' is just a name without a real attribute, or that attributes are identical to the essence.
Correction: The passage shows that the truthfulness of a derivative attribute like 'Knower' necessitates a real basis (knowledge) in God, proving attributes are distinct and real.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the truthfulness of a derived attribute like 'Knower' upon God necessitate?
A) That He has the attribute of knowledge as a real meaning
B) That He is identical with knowledge
C) That knowledge is a separate entity
D) That He does not actually have knowledge because He is unlike creation

Key Takeaway
This passage establishes that God's attributes are real and distinct from His essence by using the logical connection between a derived description and its source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is truly Knower with real knowledge, and that knowledge encompasses every secret thought you've ever had—what thought in your mind right now would you be most ashamed for Him to see? Will you repent from it before finishing this reflection?
?
The text says God's attributes are 'additional to the concept of the Necessary.' Do you treat God as a mere concept to be intellectually accepted, or as a living, powerful Knower whose attributes demand a response from your entire being? What would change today if you truly believed His knowledge sees your every intention?
?
You affirm that God is Qadir (Powerful) with real power. In what area of your life are you living as though His power is merely a name—not a reality that can change your situation? What step of reliance (tawakkul) will you take this week to prove you believe it?

Nothing Escapes God's Knowledge and Power
Proof and refutation of deviant sects regarding divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and textual proof that Allah's knowledge and power are all-encompassing, and the refutations of deviant sects that limit them.

Core Idea
God's knowledge and power are absolute; any limitation would imply deficiency.

This passage presents a concise yet powerful proof from both reason and revelation that nothing lies outside Allah's knowledge or power. After establishing the principle, the author enumerates and refutes five erroneous positions held by various sects, showing how they contradict this fundamental Islamic doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Note the concise style: the proof is given in just a few lines.
2
Identify the rational argument
The author starts with a rational proof: ignorance or inability would be a deficiency, and deficiency implies need for a determiner, which is incompatible with divinity.
This is a standard kalam argument from perfection.
3
Note the textual proof
After the rational argument, the author cites definitive texts (al-nusus al-qat'iyya) that affirm the universality of God's knowledge and power.
These texts are from the Quran and hadith, though not quoted here.
4
Examine each sectarian claim
The passage refutes five groups: philosophers, Dahriyya, an-Nazzam, al-Balkhi, and general Mu'tazila. For each, note what they deny about God's attributes.
The refutations are brief but direct.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this doctrine is central to tawheed: limiting God's knowledge or power undermines His perfection and lordship.
This connects to the larger creedal framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخرج عن علمه وقدرته شيء) لأن الجهل بالبعض، أو العجز عن البعض نقص، وافتقار إلى مخصص، مع أن النصوص القطعية ناطقة بعموم العلم وشمول القدرة . فهو بكل شيء عليم وعلى كل شيء قدير لا كما يزعم الفلاسفة: أنه لا يعلم الجزئيات، ولا يقدر على أكثر من واحد . والدهرية على أنه تعالى لا يعلم ذاته . « والنظام» (۱) على أنه لا يقدر على خلق الجهل والقبيح . والبلخى ) على أنه لا يقدر على مثل مقدور العبد وعامة المعتزلة : أنه لا يقدر على نفس مقدور العبد (۲) .
(And nothing escapes His knowledge and power) because ignorance of some things, or inability regarding some things, is deficiency, and a need for a determiner, while the definitive texts speak of the universality of knowledge and the comprehensiveness of power. So He is of everything Knowing, and over everything Powerful. Not as the philosophers claim: that He does not know particulars, nor is He able to do more than one. And the Dahriyya claim that He does not know His own essence. And an-Nazzam (1) claims that He is not able to create ignorance and evil. And al-Balkhi claims that He is not able to do the like of the servant's capability, and the generality of the Mu'tazila: that He is not able to do the exact object of the servant's capability (2).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof that nothing escapes God's knowledge and power, combined with a refutation of several sects that deny this. The argument begins with a rational proof: ignorance of anything or inability regarding anything would be a deficiency in God, and deficiency implies need for a determiner, which is incompatible with divinity. Then the author cites definitive texts (nass qat'i) that assert the universality of His knowledge and the comprehensiveness of His power, concluding: 'So He is of everything Knowing, and over everything Powerful.' The author then refutes five positions: (1) The philosophers, who claim God does not know particulars and can only do one thing. (2) The Dahriyya (materialists), who claim God does not know His own essence. (3) an-Nazzam (a Mu'tazili), who claims God cannot create ignorance and evil. (4) al-Balkhi (another Mu'tazili), who claims God cannot do the like of what the servant is capable of. (5) The general Mu'tazila, who claim God cannot do exactly what the servant does. The refutations are stated concisely by contrasting their claims with the established proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's inability to do evil is a limitation on His power.
Correction: The passage shows that 'inability' here would be a deficiency; but God's power is absolute over all possible things, and evil is not a positive act that God would perform—rather, His power is perfect.

Checkpoint

A) Because the definitive texts say so.
B) Because ignorance or inability would be a deficiency and imply need for a determiner.
C) Because the philosophers proved it.
D) Because the Mu'tazila agreed.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge and power are absolute, encompassing all things; limiting them equates to deficiency, which is impossible for God.

Proof that God Has Real Attributes
Proof that God has real attributes, refuting the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and scriptural proof that God's attributes are real and distinct from His essence.

Core Idea
If something is truly described by a quality, that quality must be real.

This passage presents a concise but powerful argument for the reality of God's attributes. It combines a logical-linguistic principle with scriptural and experiential evidence to refute the Mu'tazilite position that God has no attributes distinct from His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow, then read the English translation. Note the key terms.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the derivation argument
Focus on the statement: 'صدق المشتق على الشيء يقتضى ثبوت مأخذ الاشتقاق له'. This is the core logical proof that derived terms indicate real attributes.
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite claim that God is Knowing without knowledge.
3
Examine the refutation
See how the author dismisses the Mu'tazilite view as obviously impossible, using the analogy of 'black without blackness'.
This shows the absurdity of denying real attributes.
4
Consider the confirmatory evidence
The passage adds that definitive texts and perfect actions (af'al mutqana) also prove real attributes, not just labels.
This reinforces the logical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله صفات) لما ثبت من أنه عالم حى قادر إلى غير ذلك ومعلوم أن كلا من ذلك، يدل على معنی زائد على مفهوم الواجب، وليس الكل الفاظا مترادفة، وأن صدق المشتق على الشيء يقتضى ثبوت مأخذ الاشتقاق له، فثبت له صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة . وغير ذلك . لا كما تزعم المعتزلة من أنه عالم لا علم له، وقادر لا قدرة له (۱) ، إلى غير ذلك. فإنه محال ظاهر ، بمنزلة قولنا : أسود لا سواد له. وقد نطقت النصوص بثبوت علمه وقدرته وغيرهما، ودل صدور الأفعال المتقنة على وجود علمه وقدرته لا على مجرد تسميته عالما قادرا .
(And He has attributes) because it is established that He is Knowing, Living, Powerful, and so on. And it is known that each of these indicates an additional meaning beyond the concept of the Necessary (al-wajib), and that they are not all synonymous words. And the truthfulness of the derived term (al-mushtaqq) regarding a thing necessitates the existence of the source of derivation (ma'khadh al-ishtiqaq) for it. So there is established for Him the attribute of knowledge, power, life, and others. Not as the Mu'tazila claim: that He is Knowing without knowledge, Powerful without power (1), and so on. For that is an obvious impossibility, analogous to our saying: 'black without blackness.' And the texts have spoken of the establishment of His knowledge, power, and others, and the occurrence of perfect acts indicates the existence of His knowledge and power, not merely His being termed 'Knowing' and 'Powerful'.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God has real attributes (sifat) distinct from His essence. The proof has two parts. First, a logical-linguistic argument: the fact that we describe God as 'Knowing', 'Living', 'Powerful' etc. indicates that these descriptions convey meanings additional to the mere concept of 'the Necessary Existent'. They are not synonymous; each carries a distinct meaning. Furthermore, the truth of a derived term (like 'knowing') for something requires that the source of derivation (knowledge) exists in that thing. Hence, God must have the attributes of knowledge, power, life, etc. Second, the author refutes the Mu'tazila who say 'He is Knowing without knowledge' etc., calling it obviously impossible, like saying 'black without blackness'. He then adds two confirmations: definitive texts (nusus) affirm these attributes, and the occurrence of perfect actions (af'al mutqana) demonstrates the existence of His knowledge and power, not just the labels.

Correction: The passage argues that this is impossible because the truth of the derived term 'Knowing' necessitates the existence of knowledge as a real attribute.

The Real Debate on Divine Attributes
Sanusi clarifies the real debate on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the debate on divine attributes is truly about—whether God's attributes are real eternal qualities additional to His essence or merely identical to it.

Core Idea
The debate is not whether God has attributes, but whether they are real and additional to His essence.

The Ash'ari masters affirm that God's attributes (knowledge, power, life) are eternal realities, not like human accidents. This sets the stage for the real dispute: are these attributes additional to God's essence or identical to it? The passage clarifies first what the dispute is not, then poses the real question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage clarifying the non-dispute
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that the author begins by stating what the debate is NOT about: knowledge and power are not in dispute as attributes.
The author cites his teachers' affirmation that God has eternal life and knowledge.
2
Identify the affirmations of attributes
Find the statements about life and knowledge: they are eternal, not accidents, not necessary/acquired. This is the established position.
These affirmations serve as a foundation for the real debate.
3
Understand the real question
The author then poses the real debate: just as human knowledge is an accident subsisting in the knower, does God have an eternal attribute of knowledge subsisting in Him and additional to Him?
This is the core point of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس النزاع فى العلم والقدرة التى هى من جملة الكيفيات والملكات، لما صرح به مشايخنا رحمهم الله تعالى : من أن الله تعالى حى، وله حياة أزلية ليست بعرض ولا مستحيل البقاء ( والله تعالى عالم وله علم ازلى شامل، ليس بعرض ولا مستحيل البقاء ولا ضرورى ولا مكتسب . وكذا في سائر الصفات، بل النزاع في أنه كما أن للعالم منا علما، هو عرض قائم به زائد عليه، حادث فهل لصانع العالم علم وهو صفة أزلية قائمة به زائدة عليـه وكـذا جـمـيـع الصفات ؟ فأنكره الفلاسفة والمعتزلة . وزعموا أن صفاقه عين ذاته، بمعنى أن ذاته تسمى باعتبار التعلق بالمعلومات عالما، وبالمقدورات قادرا إلى غير ذلك. فلا يلزم تكثر في الذات ولا تعدد في القدماء والواجبات (۲) .والجواب : ما سبق من أن المستحيل تعدد الذوات القديمة. وهو غير لازم ويلزمكم كون العلم مثلا قدرة وحياة وعالما وحيا وقادرا وصانعا للعالم ومعبودا للخلق وكون الواجب غير قائم بذاته. إلى غير ذلك من المحالات . ( أزلية) لا كما تزعم الكرامية (۳)، من أن له صفات لكنها حادثة لاستحالة قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى .
(And the dispute is not about knowledge and power which are among the modalities (kayfiyyat) and dispositions (malakat), as our masters, may Allah have mercy on them, have declared: that Allah is Living, and He has an eternal life that is not an accident nor impossible of continuance. And Allah is Knowing, and He has an eternal, all-encompassing knowledge that is not an accident nor impossible of continuance nor necessary nor acquired. Similarly for all other attributes. Rather, the dispute is: just as the knower among us has knowledge, which is an accident subsisting in him, additional to him, and contingent, so does the Maker of the world have knowledge that is an eternal attribute subsisting in Him, additional to Him? And similarly for all attributes? The philosophers and the Mu'tazila denied this. They claimed that His attributes are identical to His essence, meaning that His essence is called 'Knowing' by virtue of relation to known things, and 'Powerful' by virtue of relation to things capable of being brought about, and so on. Thus no multiplicity in the essence nor plurality of eternal and necessary beings follows. The answer: what has preceded, that multiplicity of eternal essences is impossible. And it is not necessary for us [to affirm], and what follows for you is that knowledge, for example, would be power and life, and He would be Knowing, Living, Powerful, Maker of the world, Worshipped by creation, and that the Necessary would not be subsistent by His essence, and other impossibilities. (Eternal) not as the Karamiyya claim: that He has attributes but they are contingent, due to the impossibility of contingent things subsisting in His essence.)
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage first clarifies what the debate is NOT about. The author, following the Ash'ari masters, affirms that God's attributes (knowledge, power, life) are eternal realities, not accidents (a'rad) like human attributes, nor necessary or acquired. Having clarified that, the real debate is identified: just as a human's knowledge is an accident subsisting in him and additional to his essence, does God have an eternal attribute of knowledge subsisting in Him and additional to His essence? The philosophers and Mu'tazila deny this, claiming attributes are 'ayn al-dhat (identical to essence) — that the essence is called 'Knowing' only in relation to known objects, etc., to avoid multiplicity. The author then gives two refutations: (1) The impossibility of multiple eternal essences (already established) does not apply to the Ash'ari position (since attributes are not separate essences). (2) The opponents' position leads to absurdities: if knowledge is identical to essence, then knowledge would also be power and life, and the same essence would be called 'Knower', 'Living', 'Powerful', 'Maker', 'Worshipped' — but these are distinct meanings. Also, the Necessary would not be subsistent by His essence (since attributes are not distinct). Finally, the author rejects the Karamiyya position that attributes are contingent (haditha) because contingent things cannot subsist in God's eternal essence.

Key Terms
الكيفيات
الملكات
العرض

Misconception: One may think the debate is about whether God has attributes at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the existence of attributes like knowledge and power is not disputed; the debate is whether they are real eternal qualities additional to the essence or merely identical to it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the real debate about divine attributes?
A) Whether God has knowledge and power.
B) Whether God's attributes are eternal or contingent.
C) Whether God's attributes are additional to His essence or identical to it.
D) Whether human attributes are like God's attributes.

Key Takeaway
The real debate on divine attributes is whether they are real eternal qualities additional to God's essence or merely identical to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you unconsciously treat it as just a fancy way of saying 'God knows' without any reality? How would your reliance on His knowledge change if you truly believed it is a distinct, eternal reality?
?
This lesson says the debate is about whether divine attributes are 'additional' to the essence. Have you ever felt uncomfortable with this idea, fearing it might compromise tawhid? What does your heart need to accept to be at peace with the Ash'ari position?
?
Imagine you are arguing about God's attributes with someone who says they are identical to His essence. Can you, from the source passage, defend the view that they are additional? If not, re-read the Arabic. How does this affect your confidence in what you believe about your Creator?

Refuting the Denial of Real Attributes
Sanusi refutes the philosophers/Mu'tazila and Karamiyya on attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of the philosophers/Mu'tazila and the Karamiyya positions on divine attributes.

Core Idea
Denying real attributes leads to absurdities; claiming attributes are contingent is impossible.

Having clarified the real debate, the author now refutes two opposing views: the philosophers and Mu'tazila who say attributes are identical to the essence, and the Karamiyya who say attributes are contingent. The refutation shows that the former leads to logical absurdities and the latter is impossible because contingent things cannot subsist in an eternal essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of philosophers/Mu'tazila
Read the passage describing their view and the Ash'ari response. Note that they claim attributes are 'ayn al-dhat (identical to essence).
Their motivation is to avoid multiplicity of eternal entities.
2
Understand the first refutation argument
The author says the impossibility of multiple eternal essences does not apply to the Ash'ari position because attributes are not separate essences.
The argument is that the opponents' position leads to worse absurdities.
3
Grasp the absurdities of the opponents' view
If attributes are identical to essence, then knowledge would be power and life, and the same essence would have contradictory descriptions.
This shows the opposite view is untenable.
4
Refutation of the Karamiyya
Read the brief refutation: attributes cannot be contingent because contingent things cannot subsist in God's eternal essence.
This rejects the idea of attributes being created.

Source Text

Refuting the Denial of Divine Attributes
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazila denial of attributes using analogy, scripture, and rational inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila position that God is knowing without knowledge is an evident impossibility, and how both scripture and rational reflection affirm that God possesses real attributes.

Core Idea
God's attributes are not mere names; they are real and proven by revelation and reason.

The Mu'tazila claimed that God is knowing and powerful without having attributes of knowledge and power. This seems like a subtle distinction, but the author shows it is a logical impossibility. In this lesson, we will examine the refutation and the proofs that establish God's attributes as real.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the strong language: 'evident impossibility'.
2
Understand the analogy
The author compares the Mu'tazila claim to saying 'black without blackness'. Why is this impossible? Reflect on the relationship between an attribute and its entity.
An attribute cannot be separated from its subject without contradiction.
3
Examine the two proofs
The author provides two proofs: first from scripture, second from the observation of perfect acts. Identify each and see how they together refute the Mu'tazila.
The second proof is rational: effects indicate their cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا كما تزعم المعتزلة من أنه عالم لا علم له، وقادر لا قدرة له، إلى غير ذلك. فإنه محال ظاهر، بمنزلة قولنا: أسود لا سواد له. وقد نطقت النصوص بثبوت علمه وقدرته وغيرهما، ودل صدور الأفعال المتقنة على وجود علمه وقدرته لا على مجرد تسميته عالما قادرا.
Not as the Mu'tazila claim, that He is knowing without having knowledge, and powerful without having power, and so forth. For that is an evident impossibility, like our saying: black without blackness. And the texts have spoken of the establishment of His knowledge, power, and others, and the occurrence of precise acts indicates the existence of His knowledge and power, not merely the naming of Him as knowing and powerful.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila position that God is knowing and powerful without having attributes of knowledge and power distinct from His essence. The author declares this claim an evident impossibility, drawing an analogy: it is like saying 'black without blackness'—a contradiction in terms. He then provides two proofs: first, the scriptural texts affirm that God has knowledge, power, and other attributes; second, the observed perfect and precise acts of God (creation, etc.) prove that He actually possesses knowledge and power, not just the titles. The argument is that the effect (precise acts) demonstrates the real attribute (knowledge and power) as its cause.

Correction: The Mu'tazila accept the names but deny that God has distinct attributes of knowledge and power; they claim He is knowing without knowledge.

B) Black without blackness

Key Takeaway
