The Dispute Over Eternal Attributes
Defines the dispute as about eternal attributes not created ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the dispute over God's eternal attributes and the Sunni refutation of the view that they are identical to the essence.

Core Idea
The dispute is whether God's attributes are additional to His essence or identical to it.

This passage from the Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyyah clarifies the real point of contention regarding God's attributes. It is not about temporary, human-like attributes, but about whether eternal attributes are distinct from God's essence. The author then presents the Mu'tazila and philosophers' denial and offers a refutation, showing the absurd consequences of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author first clarifies what the dispute is NOT about.
2
Understand the clarification
The author states the dispute is not about knowledge and power as 'qualities and faculties' (temporal attributes). Why? Because the Sunni masters affirm God's attributes are eternal and not accidents. Understand this distinction.
This sets the stage for the real dispute.
3
Identify the two positions
Find where the author states the Sunni view: that God has eternal attributes additional to His essence. Then find the opposing view of the Mu'tazila and philosophers: that attributes are identical to the essence. Note the reasoning given for each.
The Mu'tazila argue that additional attributes would imply multiple eternal beings.
4
Examine the refutation
The author answers with 'what preceded' (the impossibility of multiple eternal essences) and then shows the absurd consequences of the opposing view. List the consequences mentioned: knowledge would be power, life, etc., and the Necessary Being would not be self-subsistent.
These consequences show the opposing view leads to contradictions.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider how the Sunni view avoids both multiplicity of eternal beings and the absurdities of collapse. Write down the core idea: attributes are additional to the essence yet not separate essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس النزاع فى العلم والقدرة التى هى من جملة الكيفيات والملكات، لما صرح به مشايخنا رحمهم الله تعالى: من أن الله تعالى حى، وله حياة أزلية ليست بعرض ولا مستحيل البقاء، والله تعالى عالم وله علم ازلى شامل، ليس بعرض ولا مستحيل البقاء ولا ضرورى ولا مكتسب. وكذا في سائر الصفات، بل النزاع في أنه كما أن للعالم منا علما، هو عرض قائم به زائد عليه، حادث فهل لصانع العالم علم وهو صفة أزلية قائمة به زائدة عليه وكذا جميع الصفات؟ فأنكره الفلاسفة والمعتزلة. وزعموا أن صفاقه عين ذاته، بمعنى أن ذاته تسمى باعتبار التعلق بالمعلومات عالما، وبالمقدورات قادرا إلى غير ذلك. فلا يلزم تكثر في الذات ولا تعدد في القدماء والواجبات. والجواب: ما سبق من أن المستحيل تعدد الذوات القديمة. وهو غير لازم ويلزمكم كون العلم مثلا قدرة وحياة وعالما وحيا وقادرا وصانعا للعالم ومعبودا للخلق وكون الواجب غير قائم بذاته. إلى غير ذلك من المحالات.
The dispute is not about the knowledge and power that are among the qualities and faculties, as our masters—may Allah have mercy on them—have declared: that Allah is living, and He has an eternal life which is not an accident nor subject to ceasing to exist; and Allah is knowing and He has an eternal encompassing knowledge, which is not an accident nor subject to ceasing to exist, nor necessary nor acquired. And similarly for the rest of the attributes. Rather, the dispute is: just as the learned among us has knowledge that is an accident subsisting in him and additional to him, temporal, so does the maker of the world have knowledge that is an eternal attribute subsisting in Him and additional to Him? And likewise all attributes? The philosophers and the Mu'tazila denied this. They claimed that His attributes are identical to His essence, meaning that His essence is called 'knowing' by virtue of relation to known things, and 'powerful' by relation to things within power, and so on. Hence no multiplicity follows in the essence nor multiplicity of eternal things and necessary beings. The answer: what preceded, that multiplicity of eternal essences is impossible. And it is not necessary (for us), and it necessitates that knowledge, for example, be power, life, knowing, living, powerful, maker of the world, object of worship of creation, and that the necessary being not be subsistent by itself. And other impossibilities.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage first clarifies that the dispute is not about human-like attributes (which are accidents), because the Hanafi masters affirm that God's attributes are eternal and not accidents. The real dispute is whether God has eternal attributes that are additional to His essence, like our attributes are additional to us but temporal. The philosophers and Mu'tazila deny this, claiming that attributes are identical to the essence, avoiding multiplicity in the eternal. The author gives the answer: while multiplicity of eternal essences is impossible, the attributes being additional does not necessitate that multiplicity, because they are not separate essences. On the contrary, their position leads to absurdities: if knowledge is identical to the essence, then knowledge would also be power, life, etc., collapsing distinctions. Also, if attributes are not additional, then the Necessary Being would not be described properly. Therefore the correct view is that attributes are eternal and additional to the essence, yet not separate essences.

الملكات
صفة أزلية
زائدة
الفلاسفة
عين الذات
تعدد القدماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the debate is about whether God has any attributes at all.
Correction: The actual debate is whether His attributes are additional to His essence or identical to it; all parties agree He has attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence the author says follows from the Mu'tazila and philosophers' view that attributes are identical to the essence?
A) Multiplicity in the essence
B) That knowledge would also be power and life
C) That attributes are temporal
D) That God has no attributes

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position affirms that God's attributes are eternal and additional to His essence, without implying multiplicity of eternal beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Are you willing to embrace the Sunni position even though it seems to add complexity to your understanding of God? Or do you find yourself attracted to simpler explanations that may be flawed? What is at stake in your soul if you choose a view that reduces God's attributes to mere relations?

Divine Attributes

Attributes: Neither Identical Nor Other
Answers the objection that attributes imply many eternal beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari principle that divine attributes are 'neither identical to nor other than the essence' resolves the objection of tawhid violation and distinguishes Islamic theology from Christian trinitarianism.

Core Idea
Attributes are neither the essence nor separate—a middle path preserving tawhid.

In this passage, the author tackles a critical objection: if God has multiple eternal attributes, doesn't that imply multiple eternal beings? He first clarifies what an attribute is, then presents the Mu'tazila argument, and finally provides the decisive Ash'ari response—the attributes are 'neither He nor other.' He illustrates the danger of the other extreme by noting Christian trinitarianism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of an attribute
Read the opening line: attributes subsist in the essence. This is the standard Ash'ari position. Note the contrast with the Mu'tazila view that speech subsists in another.
This sets the stage for why attributes are not separate entities.
2
Grasp the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argue that affirming eternal attributes distinct from the essence leads to multiple eternal beings and undermines tawhid. Identify this argument in the passage.
This is the key challenge that the rest of the passage answers.
3
See the Christian comparison
The author points out that Christians were declared disbelievers for affirming three eternal beings (Trinity), implying that if Muslims affirmed eight or more, they would face the same issue.
This highlights the urgency of a correct formulation.
4
Learn the response: 'neither He nor other'
The answer is that the attributes are neither identical to the essence (which would collapse them) nor other than the essence (which would make them separate gods). They are non-other, non-identical.
This preserves tawhid while maintaining the reality of the attributes.
5
Recognize why Christians erred
The author explains that the Christian hypostases (existence, knowledge, life) were considered distinct essences that could separate and transfer, making them separate entities.
The Islamic attributes are not separate essences, so the objection does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قائمة بذاته ضرورة أنه لا معنى لصفة الشيء إلا ما يقوم به لا كما تزعم المعتزلة من أنه متكلم بكلام هو قائم بغيره لكن مرادهم نفى كون الكلام صفة له لا إثبات كونه صفة له غير قائم بذاته. ولما تمسكت المعتزلة بأن في إثبات الصفات إبطال التوحيد، لما أنها موجودات قديمة مغايرة لذات الله تعالى فيلزم قدم غير الله تعالى، وتعدد القدماء، بل تعدد الواجب لذاته، على ما وقعت الإشارة إليه فى كلام المتقدمين والتصريح به في كلام المتأخرين من أن واجب الوجود بالذات هو الله تعالى وصفاته. وقد كفرت النصارى بإثبات ثلاثة من القدماء، فما بال الثمانية أو أكثر. أشار إلى جوابه بقوله ( وهى لا هو ولا غيره يعني ان صفات الله تعالى ليست عين الذات ولا غير الذات، فلا يلزم قدم الغير ولا تكثر القدماء . والنصارى وإن لم يصرحوا بالقدماء المتغايرة، لكن لزمهم ذلك لأنهم أثبتوا الأقانيم الثلاثة التى هى الوجود والعلم والحياة، وسموها الآب والابن وروح القدس. وزعموا أن أقنوم العلم قد انتقل إلى بدن عيسى عليه السلام فجوزوا الانفكاك عن المحل والانتقال. فكانت الأقانيم ذواتا متغايرة.
Subsisting in His essence, necessarily, for there is no meaning to an attribute of a thing except what subsists in it—not as the Mu'tazila claim that He is speaking with a speech that subsists in another, but their intent is to deny that speech is an attribute of Him, not to affirm that it is an attribute of Him not subsisting in His essence. And when the Mu'tazila argued that affirming attributes nullifies tawhid (monotheism), because they are eternal existents distinct from the essence of Allah, so it necessitates the eternity of other than Allah and multiplicity of eternal beings, indeed multiplicity of the necessary being in essence—as was indicated in the speech of the early scholars and explicitly stated in the speech of the later scholars: that the necessary being in essence is Allah and His attributes. And the Christians were declared disbelievers for affirming three eternal beings, so what about eight or more? He pointed to His answer by saying: (And they are neither He nor other), meaning that Allah's attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other than the essence, so no eternity of another follows nor multiplicity of eternal beings. And the Christians, though they did not explicitly state multiple eternal beings, it necessarily followed for them because they affirmed three hypostases, which are existence, knowledge, and life, and they called them Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. And they claimed that the hypostasis of knowledge transferred to the body of Jesus (peace be upon him), so they permitted separation from the subject and transfer. Thus the hypostases were distinct essences.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage begins by reiterating that attributes must subsist in the essence, rejecting the Mu'tazila view that God's speech subsists in another (like the angel). Then it presents the Mu'tazila objection: affirming eternal attributes distinct from the essence implies multiple eternal things, compromising tawhid. The author notes that the Christians were condemned for affirming three eternal ones (the Trinity), so how can Muslims affirm eight or more? The answer is the principle 'they are neither He nor other'—the attributes are not identical to the essence (which would collapse distinctions) nor are they separate essences like the Christian hypostases. Therefore, affirming attributes does not necessitate multiple eternal beings; they are aspects of one essence. The Christian error was that they made the hypostases distinct essences, even allowing separation and transfer. Thus the Islamic view safeguards tawhid while preserving the reality of the attributes.

إبطال التوحيد

أقانيم
لا هو ولا غيره

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming multiple attributes implies multiple gods (like the Christian Trinity).
Correction: The attributes are not separate essences; they are 'neither He nor other', so they do not multiply the divine essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between Islamic attributes and Christian hypostases?
A) Islamic attributes are created, Christian hypostases are eternal
B) Islamic attributes are 'neither He nor other', while Christian hypostases are distinct essences
C) Islamic attributes are only eight, Christian hypostases are three
D) Islamic attributes are identical to the essence, Christian hypostases are not

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari principle 'neither He nor other' safeguards tawhid while upholding the reality of divine attributes, distinguishing Islamic theology from tritheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Christians believed in three hypostases and ended up with three gods. In your daily life, do you ever treat Allah's mercy as independent from His justice, as if they are in tension? What would change if you truly believed they are one in His essence?
?
When you praise Allah as Al-'Aleem or Ar-Rahman, do you feel the weight that these are realities belonging to Him alone, not separate entities? Take a moment: what specific mental habit do you need to abandon to worship Allah as One in every sense?

The Mu'tazila Objection: Attributes and Multiplicity
Mu'tazila argue that affirming attributes leads to multiple eternal beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that affirming eternal attributes for Allah leads to a multiplicity of eternal beings, and why they considered this a violation of tawheed.

Core Idea
Affirming eternal attributes, for the Mu'tazila, means many eternal beings — a threat to tawheed.

The Mu'tazila raised a powerful objection: if we affirm that Allah's attributes are eternal and distinct from His essence, then we are positing multiple eternal entities alongside Allah. This, they argued, compromises the unity of God (tawheed) and even echoes Christian trinitarianism. The passage records this argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once through, then the English translation. Notice how the author begins with a definition of attribute before presenting the Mu'tazila claim.
The first part clarifies what an attribute is — something subsisting in the essence.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
Find where the Mu'tazila argument is stated: affirming attributes as eternal and other than Allah nullifies tawheed. Underline the key consequences: eternity of other than Allah, multiplicity of eternal beings, multiplicity of the necessary-by-itself.
3
Understand the logical chain
The Mu'tazila reason: attributes are existents, eternal, and other than Allah's essence. Therefore, there is something eternal besides Allah. That leads to multiple eternal beings, and even multiple necessary beings (since each attribute would be necessary by itself).
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Consider the Christian parallel
The author notes that Christians were declared disbelievers for affirming three eternal beings (Father, Son, Holy Spirit). The Mu'tazila use this to highlight the gravity: if three eternal beings are kufr, what about eight or more attributes?
This comparison intensifies the objection.
5
Reflect on the implied response
Though the passage mainly presents the objection, the author sets it in a framework that rejects the Mu'tazila premise. Notice the opening line: attributes subsist in the essence, not separate. This already counters the objection.
The response is that attributes are not 'other' in the sense of independent existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قائمة بذاته ضرورة أنه لا معنى لصفة الشيء إلا ما يقوم به لا كما تزعم المعتزلة من أنه متكلم بكلام هو قائم بغيره لكن مرادهم نفى كون الكلام صفة له لا إثبات كونه صفة له غير قائم بذاته. ولما تمسكت المعتزلة بأن في إثبات الصفات إبطال التوحيد (١) ، لما أنها موجودات قديمة مغايرة لذات الله تعالى فيلزم قدم غير الله تعالى، وتعدد القدماء، بل تعدد الواجب لذاته، على ما وقعت الإشارة إليه فى كلام المتقدمين والتصريح به في كلام المتأخرين من أن واجب الوجود بالذات هو الله تعالى وصفاته. وقد كفرت النصارى بإثبات ثلاثة من القدماء، فما بال الثمانية أو أكثر.
(It is necessarily self-subsistent: there is no meaning to the attribute of a thing except what subsists in it, not as the Mu'tazila claim that He is speaking with a speech that subsists in another—but their intent is to deny that speech is an attribute of Him, not to affirm that it is an attribute of Him not subsisting in His essence. And when the Mu'tazila clung to the argument that in affirming attributes there is nullification of tawheed (unity), because they are existents, eternal, other than the essence of Allah, may He be exalted, so there necessarily follows the eternity of something other than Allah, and multiplicity of eternal beings, indeed multiplicity of the necessary-by-itself, as was indicated in the speech of the earlier scholars and explicitly stated in the speech of the later scholars: that the Necessary Existence by essence is Allah and His attributes. And the Christians have been disbelieved for affirming three eternal beings, so what about eight or more?)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage records the Mu'tazila objection against affirming eternal attributes for Allah. They argue that attributes would be existents (موجودات), eternal (قديمة), and other (مغايرة) than Allah's essence. This would imply the eternity of something other than Allah, and thus a multiplicity of eternal beings (تعدد القدماء), even a multiplicity of necessary-by-itself beings (تعدد الواجب لذاته). The author notes that this implication was indicated by earlier scholars and made explicit by later ones, namely that the Necessary Existence by essence is Allah and His attributes—so if attributes are other, they would also be necessary. The Mu'tazila then draw a parallel to Christianity: Christians affirmed three eternal beings (Father, Son, Holy Spirit) and were thus considered disbelievers; so how much worse to affirm eight or more attributes? The argument is that affirming multiple eternal beings leads to polytheism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila completely deny attributes. Actually, they deny attributes as eternal and distinct, but may affirm them as non-eternal or as aspects of the essence.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny that Allah has attributes; they deny that the attributes are eternal and distinct from the essence, because that would imply multiple eternal beings.

Checkpoint

A) It affirms tawheed perfectly.
B) It leads to multiplicity of eternal beings.
C) It is the same as the Christian doctrine of the Trinity.
D) It is the position of the earlier scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that affirming attributes as eternal and distinct from Allah's essence leads to multiple eternal beings, contradicting tawheed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If Christians were disbelieved for affirming three eternal beings, how much more careful must you be when you speak or think about Allah's attributes? Are there any ways you have inadvertently described Allah's attributes as if they were independent beings?
?
The Mu'tazila were so concerned about protecting tawheed that they denied real attributes. Do you have any fear that affirming attributes somehow diminishes your tawheed? What step can you take today to internalize that attributes are not partners but perfections of Allah?

Attributes: Neither the Essence Nor Other Than It
Sanusi gives the Ash'ari answer on attributes and contrasts with Christian hypostases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that divine attributes are neither identical to the essence nor distinct from it, and why this avoids the pitfalls of polytheism and Christian trinitarianism.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are a mystery: neither the essence nor other.

Sanusi clarifies the Ash'ari position on divine attributes, which navigates between two extremes. The Mu'tazila argued that attributes are identical to the essence, while Christians made them distinct persons. The Ash'ari middle path preserves tawhid without denying the reality of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the key phrase 'neither He nor other'.
This phrase is a third logical category beyond identity and distinction.
2
Understand the Ash'ari answer
The statement means attributes are not identical to the essence (which would be a denial of real attributes) nor distinct (which would imply multiple eternal beings). Grasp this middle position.
The consequence: no eternity of a distinct other, no multiplicity of eternal beings.
3
Contrast with Christian doctrine
Sanusi points out that Christians, though not explicitly saying eternal beings are distinct, are forced into that by affirming three hypostases (existence, knowledge, life) and allowing detachment and transfer (as in the incarnation of knowledge into Jesus). Thus their hypostases become distinct essences.
This shows how the Ash'ari answer avoids a similar error.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters for tawhid. The Ash'ari position upholds the oneness of Allah while affirming that His attributes are real and meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أشار إلى جوابه بقوله ( وهى لا هو ولا غيره يعني ان صفات الله تعالى ليست عين الذات ولا غير الذات، فلا يلزم قدم الغير ولا تكثر القدماء . والنصارى وإن لم يصرحوا بالقدماء المتغايرة، لكن لزمهم ذلك لأنهم أثبتوا الأقانيم الثلاثة التى هى الوجود والعلم والحياة، وسموها الآب والابن وروح القدس. وزعموا أن أقنوم العلم قد انتقل إلى بدن عيسى عليه السلام فجوزوا الانفكاك عن المحل والانتقال. فكانت الأقانيم ذواتا متغايرة .
He pointed to its answer by his saying: (And they are not He nor other than Him), meaning that the attributes of Allah, may He be exalted, are neither the essence nor other than the essence, so the eternity of what is other does not follow, nor the multiplicity of eternal beings. And the Christians, though they did not explicitly state that the eternal beings are distinct, it nonetheless followed for them because they affirmed the three hypostases, which are existence, knowledge, and life, and they called them the Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. And they claimed that the hypostasis of knowledge transferred to the body of Jesus, peace be upon him, so they permitted detachment from the locus and transference. Thus the hypostases became distinct essences.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari answer to the Mu'tazila objection. The author cites the statement 'they (attributes) are neither He nor other than Him' to mean that the attributes of Allah are not identical to the essence (عين الذات) nor distinct from it (غير الذات). Therefore, the implication that something other than Allah is eternal does not arise, nor does multiplicity of eternal beings. The author then contrasts this with Christian doctrine: although Christians did not explicitly affirm distinct eternal beings, they are forced into that by affirming three hypostases (أقانيم)—existence, knowledge, life—named Father, Son, Holy Spirit. Moreover, they claimed that the hypostasis of knowledge transferred into Jesus's body, allowing detachment and transference. This makes the hypostases truly distinct essences, whereas the Ash'ari attributes are not distinct essences.

Key Terms
عين الذات
غير الذات
أقانيم
الانفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'neither identical nor other' is a contradictory or meaningless phrase.
Correction: In kalam, this is a third logical category: attributes are real and distinct in meaning but not separate entities. It avoids both identity (which would negate real attributes) and distinctness (which would imply multiple eternal beings).

Checkpoint
What does the statement 'attributes are neither He nor other than Him' prevent?
A) The eternity of the attributes.
B) The necessity of the divine essence.
C) The implication of an eternal other and multiplicity of eternal beings.
D) The existence of attributes altogether.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position upholds tawhid by affirming real attributes without making them separate entities, thus avoiding the Christian error of distinct hypostases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine Allah's attributes—like His knowledge or life—as if they are 'things' distinct from Him, almost like parts? How does the Ash'ari formulation 'neither the essence nor other' confront that tendency in your own heart?
?
The Christians allowed the hypostasis of knowledge to detach and enter Jesus. Is there any 'attribute' of Allah that you subconsciously think can be given to a created being—a prophet, a saint, or even yourself? What is the danger of that thought, and how does this passage expose it?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Akbar' or say 'SubhanAllah', do you truly feel that nothing shares in His attributes? Or do you, like the Christians, unintentionally set up 'eternal partners' in your mind by treating His attributes as separate entities? What changes if you live the truth that they are neither He nor other than He?

Objection: Multiplicity Without Distinctness
An objection argues numbers show multiplicity without distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that multiplicity does not require distinctness, as illustrated by the orders of numbers and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
Multiplicity can exist without distinctness, as numbers show.

A potential objector challenges the Ash'ari claim that denying distinctness removes multiplicity. Using the familiar example of numbers and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna, the objector argues that multiplicity is observed even where parts are not distinct from wholes. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Notice the objector's two main points: the numbers example and the consensus note.
The passage is short but packs two distinct arguments.
2
Identify the objector's main claim
The objector denies that multiplicity depends on distinctness (defined as possibility of detachment). Identify the core claim in the first sentence.
The objector is countering the Ash'ari position that avoiding distinctness avoids multiplicity.
3
Analyze the numbers example
The objector uses the orders of numbers (1,2,3…) to show multiplicity without distinctness. Understand why each number is part of the next, yet they remain multiple.
For example, 'two' includes 'one' as a part, but they are different numbers.
4
Consider the additional consensus point
The objector adds that Ahl al-Sunna agree on the multiplicity of attributes, whether distinct or not. This reinforces that the Ash'ari attempt to avoid multiplicity is problematic.
This point shows that even if attributes are not distinct, multiplicity is still affirmed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يمنع توقف التعدد والتكثر على التغاير بمعنى جواز الانفكاك، للقطع بأن مراتب الأعداد من الواحد والاثنين والثلاثة، إلى غير ذلك متعددة متكثرة، مع أن البعض جزء من البعض، والجزء لا يغاير الكل. وأيضا لا يتصور نزاع من أهل السنة والجماعة في كثرة الصفات وتعددها متغايرة كانت أو غير متغايرة .
And one may object by denying that multiplicity and numerousness depend on distinctness in the sense of possibility of detachment, because it is certain that the orders of numbers—one, two, three, etc.—are multiple and numerous, even though some are parts of others, and the part is not distinct from the whole. And also, no dispute is conceivable among Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a regarding the abundance of attributes and their multiplicity, whether they are distinct or not.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage records an objection against the Ash'ari position that avoids multiplicity by denying distinctness. The objector argues that multiplicity does not require distinctness in the sense of possible detachment (جواز الانفكاك). The proof is from numbers: the orders of numbers (one, two, three, etc.) are multiple and numerous even though each higher number includes the lower as a part, and a part is not distinct from the whole (e.g., two includes one as a part, yet they are multiple). Therefore, the Ash'ari claim that denying distinctness avoids multiplicity is refuted. Additionally, the objector notes that there is no disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna that the attributes are many; whether they are distinct or not, the multiplicity is acknowledged. So the Ash'ari avoidance seems problematic.

Key Terms
التعدد
التغاير
جواز الانفكاك
مراتب الأعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'distinctness' in kalam always implies separability or detachment.
Correction: In this passage, distinctness (al-taghayur) is defined specifically as the possibility of detachment (jawaz al-infikak). The objector argues that multiplicity can exist without this type of distinctness.

Checkpoint
What example does the objector use to show multiplicity without distinctness?
A) The Trinity.
B) The orders of numbers.
C) The attributes of Allah.
D) The parts of a body.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses the orders of numbers to show that multiplicity does not require distinctness in the sense of possible detachment, challenging the Ash'ari position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the example of numbers—where two contains one without being separate—change your perspective? Will you accept that multiplicity does not imply division in Allah, even if your mind cannot fully grasp it?
?
In your own life, do you ever treat the 'parts' of your faith (like belief in attributes, actions, knowledge) as if they are separate or detachable from each other? How can the idea of multiplicity without distinctness help you see your faith as a coherent whole? What step can you take today to integrate your understanding of tawhid into your daily worship?
?
The objector argues that Ahl al-Sunna agree on the multiplicity of attributes whether distinct or not. Do you, like the objector, trust the consensus of the scholars on matters that transcend full human comprehension? Or do you privately insist on clear logical separation? Examine your heart: can you submit to a truth that your logic alone cannot prove? Commit to humbly accepting revealed truths even when they challenge your reasoning.

The Correct Formulation: Attributes Necessary by Essence
Author proposes a precise formulation to avoid multiplicity errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is more precise to say that divine attributes are necessary by the essence of Allah, not by themselves, and how this avoids the charge of multiple eternal beings.

Core Idea
Attributes are possible in themselves but necessary by Allah's essence.

The author refines the theological language about Allah's attributes. Some opponents argue that affirming multiple eternal attributes implies multiple gods. To counter this, Sanusi proposes a precise formulation: attributes are not necessary in themselves; they are necessary due to their connection with the divine essence. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the author's rhythm, then read the translation. Pay attention to the key terms like 'ذوات قديمة' and 'واجب الوجود'.
The author is correcting a common theological mistake.
2
Distinguish 'necessary by itself' vs 'necessary by another'
Notice the author says attributes are not wajib al-wujud li-dhatiha (necessary by themselves), but rather they are wajib for that which is neither identical nor other. This means they are necessary by virtue of the essence.
3
Understand the possibility of eternal attributes
The author explains that in themselves, attributes are possible (mumkin), but there is no impossibility in a possible thing being eternal if it subsists in the Eternal and is necessary by it.
This avoids the charge of multiplicity because not every eternal is a god.
4
Reflect on the preferred phrasing
The author concludes that one should say 'Allah is eternal with His attributes' rather than 'eternal beings'. Reflect on why this wording is safer.
It prevents the mind from supposing each attribute is an independent entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأولى أن يقال : المستحيل تعدد ذوات قديمة لا ذات وصفات وأن لا يجتزأ على القول بكون الصفات واجبة الوجود لذاتها، بل يقال : هي واجبة لا لغيرها، بل لما ليس عينها ولا غيرها . أعنى ذات : الله - تعالى وتقدس. ويكون هذا مراد من قال : الواجب الوجود لذاته : هو الله تعالى وصفاته. يعني : أنها واجبة لذات الواجب تعالى . وأما في نفسها فهى ممكنة، ولا استحالة فى قدم الممكن إذا كان قائما بذات القديم واجبا به غير منفصل عنه، فليس كل قديم إلها حتى يلزم من وجود القدماء، وجود الآلهة. لكن ينبغي أن يقال : الله تعالى قديم بصفاته. ولا يطلق القول بالقدماء، لئلا يذهب الوهم إلى أن كلا منها، قائم بذاته موصوف بصفات الألوهية .
So it is better to say: what is impossible is the multiplicity of eternal essences, not essence and attributes; and one should not presume to say that the attributes are necessary in existence by themselves. Rather, it is said: they are necessary not for something other than themselves, but for that which is neither identical to them nor other than them—I mean the essence of Allah, may He be exalted and sanctified. And this is the meaning of those who said: the Necessary Existence by essence is Allah and His attributes. That is, they are necessary for the essence of the Necessary. As for themselves, they are possible, and there is no impossibility in the eternity of a possible thing if it subsists in the essence of the Eternal, being necessary by it, not separate from it. For not every eternal is a god such that from the existence of eternal beings the existence of gods would follow. Rather, it should be said: Allah, may He be exalted, is eternal with His attributes. And the phrase 'eternal beings' should not be used absolutely, lest the mind suppose that each of them is self-subsistent qualified by divine attributes.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage provides a refined Ash'ari formulation to address the objection. The author states that the real impossibility is the multiplicity of eternal essences (ذوات قديمة), not the coexistence of an essence with its attributes. One should not say attributes are necessary in existence by themselves (واجبة الوجود لذاتها). Instead, they are necessary not for something other than themselves, but for that which is neither identical nor other—namely the essence of Allah. This is the correct interpretation of the statement 'the Necessary Existence by essence is Allah and His attributes'—meaning they are necessary by virtue of the Necessary Essence. In themselves, attributes are possible (ممكنة); there is no impossibility in a possible thing being eternal if it subsists in the Eternal and is necessary by it, inseparable. Not every eternal is a god, so affirming eternal attributes does not imply multiple gods. Finally, one should say 'Allah is eternal with His attributes,' not 'eternal beings,' to avoid the misconception that each attribute is a self-subsistent entity with divine attributes.

Key Terms
ذوات قديمة
واجب الوجود
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributes are necessary in themselves (wajib al-wujud li-dhatiha).
Correction: Attributes are possible in themselves and only necessary by virtue of the divine essence, thus not independent eternal beings.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what should one say about the attributes to avoid error?
A) They are eternal beings.
B) They are necessary in themselves.
C) They are necessary by the essence, possible in themselves.
D) They are identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The key is to affirm that attributes are not independent entities but are inseparable from the divine essence, being necessary by it.

Schools on Attributes and the Ash'ari Objection
Summarizes schools and records a logical objection to Ash'ari negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of three theological schools on divine attributes and the logical objection raised against the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari middle ground on attributes faces a logical charge of contradicting itself.

This passage summarizes the different approaches to divine attributes taken by major Islamic theological schools. It then presents a pointed logical objection to the Ash'ari view, arguing that their attempt to avoid both otherness and identity leads to a contradiction. This objection sets up a key debate in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the content. Notice the summary of schools first, then the objection.
2
Identify the three schools and their views
List the three schools mentioned: Mu'tazila/philosophers, Karramiyya, and Ash'ariyya. What does each deny about divine attributes?
The Ash'ari position is the focus of the objection.
3
Analyze the logical objection
The objection claims the Ash'ari negation is both 'رفع للنقيضين' (removal of contradictories) and 'جمع بينهما' (conjunction). Work through the argument: why does negating otherness implicitly affirm identity? Why is that a problem?
Consider the claim that no intermediate exists between identity and otherness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولصعوبة هذا المقام، ذهبت المعتزلة والفلاسفة إلى نفى الصفات. والكرامية إلى نفى قدمها والأشاعرة إلى نفى غيريتها وعينيتها. فإن قيل : هذا النفى فى الظاهر رفع للنقيضين، وفي الحقيقة جمع بينهما ؛ لأن نفى الغيرية صريحا مثلا إثبات للعينية ضمنا . وإثباتها مع نفى العينية صريحا جمع بين النقيضين وكذا نفى العينية صريحا، جمع بينهما لأن المفهوم من الشيء، إن لم يكن هو المفهوم الآخر، فهو غيره وإلا فهو عينه ولا يتصور بينهما واسطة .
And due to the difficulty of this position, the Mu'tazila and philosophers went to denying the attributes; and the Karramiyya to denying their eternity; and the Ash'ariyya to denying their otherness and identity. For if it is said: This negation is in appearance a removal of the two contradictories, but in reality a conjunction of them; because the explicit negation of otherness, for example, is an implicit affirmation of identity. And affirming it together with the explicit negation of identity is a conjunction of contradictories. Similarly, the explicit negation of identity is a conjunction of them, because the concept of a thing, if it is not the other concept, then it is other than it; otherwise it is identical to it, and no intermediate is conceivable between them.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage first summarizes the positions of different schools regarding divine attributes: the Mu'tazila and philosophers deny attributes altogether; the Karramiyya deny their eternity (while affirming them as created); the Ash'ariyya deny that attributes are other than the essence (غيرية) and deny that they are identical (عينية). Then an objection is raised: the Ash'ari negation appears in one way to remove both contradictories (رفع للنقيضين) but in reality it combines them (جمع بينهما). The argument is that if one explicitly negates otherness, that implicitly affirms identity (since if not other, then it is identical). So the Ash'ari would be simultaneously affirming identity (implicitly) and negating it (explicitly), which is a conjunction of contradictories. Similarly, negating identity explicitly would imply otherness implicitly, again a contradiction. The objector asserts that between identity and otherness there is no intermediate concept—everything either is identical to another thing or is other than it—so the Ash'ari claim of a third state is logically impossible. The passage ends without the Ash'ari response within this window.

Key Terms
نفى الصفات
نفى القدم
نفى الغيرية والعينية
رفع النقيضين
جمع بين النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the objection raise against the Ash'ari position?
A) It affirms too many attributes.
B) It denies the existence of attributes.
C) It appears to remove contradictories but actually combines them.
D) It makes attributes eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on divine attributes faces a serious logical objection that must be addressed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector insists there is no intermediate between identity and otherness. When you reflect on God's attributes, do you tend to make them exactly like human attributes (identity) or completely different (otherness)? How does this lesson challenge you to hold both truths without forcing a false choice?
?
The Ash'ari position was a difficult middle path. Have you ever faced a theological puzzle that seemed logically impossible? Did you dismiss it, or did you humbly accept that your intellect may be limited? What will you do the next time such a tension arises?
?
The objection uses logic to attack a position about God. Are you willing to let go of the need to fully understand every aspect of God's nature? What would it cost you to trust revelation over your own reasoning when they seem to conflict?

The Correct Formulation on Divine Attributes
Author states the correct formulation on the necessity and possibility of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct theological formulation regarding divine attributes: that they are not necessary in themselves but are necessary due to the divine essence, and that they are possible in themselves yet pre-eternal when subsisting in the pre-eternal essence.

Core Idea
Attributes are necessary by Allah, not in themselves; possible yet pre-eternal.

This passage from the sharh of al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyyah gives the precise formulation for speaking about divine attributes. The author corrects potential misunderstandings that could lead to polytheism or denial of attributes. You will learn the precise language to use in order to preserve tawhid while affirming the reality of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation together. Notice the author's careful phrasing and the way he corrects potential errors.
Pay attention to the key terms: واجب, ممكن, قديم, صفات, ذات.
2
Identify the correct statements
The passage makes several positive claims. Identify: (1) attributes are necessary not in themselves but for that which is neither identical to nor other than the essence; (2) in themselves they are possible; (3) pre-eternality of a possible thing is acceptable if it subsists in the pre-eternal essence.
3
Understand the warnings
The author warns against saying attributes are necessary in themselves (واجبة الوجود لذاتها) and against using the plural 'the pre-eternal ones' (القدماء). Reflect on why these formulations are problematic.
The plural expression could imply multiple self-subsisting eternal entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأولى أن يقال : المستحيل تعدد ذوات قديمة لا ذات وصفات وأن لا يجتزأ على القول بكون الصفات واجبة الوجود لذاتها، بل يقال : هي واجبة لا لغيرها، بل لما ليس عينها ولا غيرها . أعنى ذات : الله - تعالى وتقدس. ويكون هذا مراد من قال : الواجب الوجود لذاته : هو الله تعالى وصفاته. يعني : أنها واجبة لذات الواجب تعالى . وأما في نفسها فهى ممكنة، ولا استحالة فى قدم الممكن إذا كان قائما بذات القديم واجبا به غير منفصل عنه، فليس كل قديم إلها حتى يلزم من وجود القدماء، وجود الآلهة. لكن ينبغي أن يقال : الله تعالى قديم بصفاته. ولا يطلق القول بالقدماء، لئلا يذهب الوهم إلى أن كلا منها، قائم بذاته موصوف بصفات الألوهية .
So the better thing to say is: the impossible is the multiplicity of eternal essences, not [one] essence and attributes; and one should not dare to say that the attributes are necessary of existence in themselves, but rather it is said: they are necessary not for something other than [the essence], but for that which is neither identical to it nor other than it. I mean the essence of God – exalted and sanctified be He. And this is the intended meaning of whoever said: the Necessary Existence in itself is God and His attributes. Meaning: they are necessary due to the essence of the Necessary (the Exalted). But in themselves they are possible; and there is no impossibility in the pre-eternity of the possible when it subsists in the pre-eternal essence, being necessary by it, inseparable from it. So not every pre-eternal thing is a god such that the existence of the pre-eternal things entails the existence of multiple gods. Rather, one should say: God (the Exalted) is pre-eternal with His attributes. And one should not use the plural expression 'the pre-eternal ones', lest the imagination go to that each of them is self-subsisting, described with the attributes of divinity.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal clarification and formulation. The author begins by stating the correct position regarding divine attributes. He first asserts that the real impossibility is not in having one essence with many attributes, but in having multiple pre-eternal essences. He then warns against saying that the attributes are necessary in themselves (واجبة الوجود لذاتها). Instead, one should say they are necessary 'not for another' (لا لغيرها) but 'for that which is neither identical to them nor other than them' – meaning the divine essence. He clarifies that whoever said 'the Necessary of existence in itself is God and His attributes' meant that the attributes are necessary due to the essence of the Necessary, not in themselves. In themselves, they are possible (ممكنة). This is crucial because it avoids both polytheism (by not making attributes independent entities) and denial of attributes. The author then explains that there is no contradiction in a possible thing being pre-eternal if it subsists in a pre-eternal essence and is inseparable from it. He emphasizes that not every pre-eternal thing is a god, countering the argument that multiple pre-eternal things imply multiple gods. Finally, he advises using the singular expression 'God is pre-eternal with His attributes' and avoiding the plural 'the pre-eternal ones' to prevent the misconception that each attribute is a self-subsisting deity.

Key Terms

ممكن
قديم
صفات
ذات
عين
غير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That saying 'attributes are necessary' means they are necessary in themselves, leading to multiple necessary beings.
Correction: The author clarifies that attributes are necessary due to the essence, not in themselves. In themselves, they are possible, which avoids multiple necessary beings.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct way to describe the relationship of the attributes to the divine essence?
A) They are necessary in themselves like the essence.
B) They are necessary due to the essence, but possible in themselves.
C) They are created and not pre-eternal.
D) They are identical to the essence without any distinction.

Key Takeaway
The correct formulation distinguishes between necessity by essence and possibility in themselves, preserving tawhid while affirming the reality of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attributes—His power, His mercy, His knowledge—do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'forces' floating around, rather than as His own perfections? What does this reveal about the state of your tawhid?
?
If every quality you possess is ultimately possible in itself and only sustained by Allah, how does that change the way you view your own strengths and weaknesses? Will you thank Him today for a quality you previously took credit for?
?
The author warns against using the plural 'the pre-eternal ones' because it leads the imagination astray. What 'plural' thoughts have you entertained about Allah—such as treating His mercy as something that exists apart from His justice? How will you guard your mind from such subtle shirk?

Objection Against Negating Otherness and Identity
An objection that negating both otherness and identity is contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that the Ash'ari position of negating both otherness and identity of the divine attributes with the essence appears to combine contradictories and thus seems logically impossible.

Core Idea
Negating both otherness and identity is contradictory if no middle ground exists.

The classical theological debate over divine attributes often hinges on logical consistency. Here an opponent raises a sharp objection against the Ash'ari middle path, arguing that denying both otherness and identity forces a contradictory affirmation of both. This passage presents that challenge, setting the stage for a defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: if- then reasoning and the claim of contradiction.
The objection uses explicit negation and implicit affirmation.
2
Identify the key assumption
The objector assumes that otherness and identity are strict contradictories (naqidan) with no middle ground (wāsita). This is crucial.
Without this assumption, the objection collapses.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow how negating otherness explicitly implies affirming identity implicitly, and then negating identity alongside that implied affirmation leads to a combination of contradictories.
The objector argues that the Ash'ari position is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا النفى فى الظاهر رفع للنقيضين، وفي الحقيقة جمع بينهما ؛ لأن نفى الغيرية صريحا مثلا إثبات للعينية ضمنا . وإثباتها مع نفى العينية صريحا جمع بين النقيضين وكذا نفى العينية صريحا، جمع بينهما لأن المفهوم من الشيء، إن لم يكن هو المفهوم الآخر، فهو غيره وإلا فهو عينه ولا يتصور بينهما واسطة .
For if it is said: This negation is in appearance a removal of the two contradictories, but in reality it is a combination between them; because the negation of otherness explicitly, for example, is an affirmation of identity implicitly. And affirming it [i.e., otherness] along with the negation of identity explicitly is a combination of contradictories. Likewise, the negation of identity explicitly is a combination of them because the concept of a thing, if it is not the other concept, it is other than it, and if not, it is identical to it, and no intermediary is conceivable between them.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Ash'ari position of negating both otherness (غيرية) and identity (عينية) of the attributes with the essence. The objector argues that this apparent removal of two contradictories (رفع للنقيضين) is in fact a combination of them (جمع بينهما). The reasoning is: if you explicitly negate otherness, that implicitly affirms identity, because if the attribute is not other than the essence, it must be identical. Then, if you also explicitly negate identity, you end up affirming both identity and otherness implicitly. The objector assumes that the relationship between otherness and identity is strictly disjunctive: either a thing is identical to another or it is other; there is no middle ground (لا يتصور بينهما واسطة). Therefore, the Ash'ari claim is logically impossible.

Key Terms
نفي
إثبات
غيرية
عينية
نقيضين
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this objection is the final verdict and the Ash'ari position is false.
Correction: This is an objection that will be answered later in the text; the Ash'ari position has its own defense.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection against the Ash'ari position?
A) The attributes cannot be pre-eternal.
B) Otherness and identity are assumed to be contradictories with no middle ground.
C) The attributes are completely distinct from the essence.
D) The Ash'ariyya deny the existence of attributes.

Key Takeaway
The objection hinges on the assumption that otherness and identity are strict contradictories with no possible intermediary, making the Ash'ari position seem self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If logical categories like otherness and identity cannot fully capture the relationship between God and His attributes, what does that say about the limits of your own reasoning when it comes to understanding Allah's nature? Will you accept a mystery that clashes with your logic, or will you insist on a human framework?
?
When you recite Allah's attributes (like al-‘Alīm, al-Qadīr), do you ever fall into the trap of imagining them as separate additions to His essence? This lesson's objection exposes a subtle error—have you unknowingly committed it in your heart? How can you correct your mental image of your Lord?
?
The objector assumes that if something is not 'other' it must be 'identical'. In your own understanding of tawḥīd, are there areas where you impose a false 'either-or' on Allah's reality? Will you humble yourself and admit that His oneness transcends your binary categories, even if that makes you uncomfortable?

Defining Otherness and Identity for Divine Attributes
Author responds by defining otherness and identity, allowing an intermediary state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari scholars define 'otherness' and 'identity' to reconcile the distinction between God's essence and attributes without separation.

Core Idea
Otherness means separable; identity means identical; God's attributes are neither.

In response to the objection that attributes must be either identical to or entirely distinct from the essence, the author clarifies that 'otherness' and 'identity' have technical definitions that allow a middle ground. This passage lays out those definitions and shows how they apply to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of otherness
The passage states that 'otherness' means two existents such that one can be conceived existing without the other, i.e., separation is possible. Notice the condition of conceivability.
This is a technical definition, not ordinary usage.
2
Identify the definition of identity
Identity is defined as the union of the concept without any difference. This means two things are identical if their concepts are exactly the same, with no distinction at all.
3
Explore the intermediary state
The author explains that otherness and identity are not strict contradictories; there is an intermediary: a thing whose concept differs from another but which cannot exist without it, like a part with the whole or an attribute with its essence.
This is the key to solving the objection.
4
Apply to divine attributes
The passage then applies this: God's essence and attributes have different concepts, but they are inseparable because both are eternal. Therefore, they are neither other (separable) nor identical (same concept), but in the intermediary category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : قد فسروا الغيرية بكون الموجودين بحيث يقدر ويتصور وجود أحدهما مع عدم الآخر، أى يمكن الانفكاك بينهما. والعينية باتحاد المفهوم بلا تفاوت أصلا، فلا يكونان نقيضين، بل يتصور بينهما واسطة بأن يكون الشيء بحيث لا يكون مفهومه مفهوم الآخر، ولا يوجد بدونه كالجزء مع الكل والصفة مع الذات، وبعض الصفات مع البعض. فإن ذات الله تعالى وصفاته أزلية، والعدم على الأزلي محال . والواحد من العشرة يستحيل بقاؤه بدونها، وبقاؤها بدونه. إذا هو منها. فعدمها ،عدمه، ووجودها ،وجوده بخلاف الصفات المحدثة. قيام الذات بدون تلك الصفات المعينة متصور، فيكون غير الذات . كذا ذكره المشايخ
We say: They [the scholars] have interpreted otherness as [the case of] two existents such that the existence of one can be conceived and imagined with the non-existence of the other, i.e., separation is possible between them. And identity as the union of the concept without any difference at all. So they are not contradictories; rather, an intermediary is conceivable between them, such that a thing is such that its concept is not the concept of the other, and it does not exist without it, like the part with the whole, and the attribute with the essence, and some attributes with others. For the essence of God (the Exalted) and His attributes are eternal, and non-existence is impossible for the eternal. And the one of the ten cannot endure without it [the ten], nor can the ten endure without it, because it is from it. So its non-existence is its non-existence, and its existence is its existence, unlike temporally originated attributes. The subsistence of the essence without those specific attributes is conceivable, so they would be other than the essence. Thus the mashāyikh have mentioned.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the previous objection. The author states that the scholars (المشايخ) have defined 'otherness' (الغيرية) in a specific way: two existents are 'other' if one can be conceived existing without the other, i.e., separation (انفكاك) is possible between them. 'Identity' (العينية) means that the concept (مفهوم) of one is exactly the same as the other, with no difference. Under these definitions, otherness and identity are not strict contradictories; rather, an intermediary state is conceivable. The intermediary is when one thing has a different concept from another but cannot exist without it, like a part with the whole, or an attribute with its essence. This precisely describes the relationship between God's essence and attributes: they have distinct concepts (e.g., 'existence' vs. 'power') yet cannot exist separately. The author gives examples: the number one is not conceptually the same as ten, but one cannot exist without ten (in the context of being a part). Similarly, God's attributes are eternal, so non-existence cannot be imagined. For temporal attributes, separation is conceivable, so they would be other. Thus, the Ash'ari position is defended: attributes are neither strictly other (since inseparable) nor strictly identical (since conceptually distinct).

Key Terms
غيرية
عينية
انفكاك
مفهوم
جزء
كل
صفة
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this definition of otherness requires two independent existents; but here it includes dependence.
Correction: The definition only requires conceivability of one existing without the other, not actual independence. In the intermediary case, one cannot exist without the other, so they are not other.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the definition of 'otherness' (الغيرية)?
A) Two things having different concepts.
B) Two things where one can exist without the other.
C) Two things that are completely unlike each other.
D) Two things coexisting in time.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are neither wholly other nor wholly identical to His essence; they are conceptually distinct but eternally inseparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah's mercy or power, do you treat these attributes as if they are separate 'things' He possesses? How does knowing they are eternally inseparable from His essence change the way you understand His mercy toward you specifically today?
?
The author argues that attributes are not other than the essence because separation is impossible for the eternal. Have you ever thought that God's anger could be separated from His mercy? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for your hope in His forgiveness?
?
If the part cannot exist without the whole, and the whole cannot exist without the part, then your connection to Allah is not like that—you are not an essential part of Him. But His attributes are. Does this make you feel distant or secure? Let this truth lead you to praise Him for His self-sufficiency and your absolute need for Him.

Critique of the Mashāyikh's Definition of Otherness
Author critiques the definition of otherness by separability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's logical critique of the mashāyikh's definition of otherness in terms of separability, and why neither two-sided nor one-sided separability adequately captures the concept of otherness in the context of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Defining otherness by separability leads to logical contradictions.

The mashāyikh defined otherness (al-mughāyara) as the possibility of separation. However, the author subjects this definition to a rigorous critique. By examining two possible interpretations—separation from both sides or from one side—he shows that both lead to counterexamples, especially when applied to the relationship between the world and the Creator, accidents and their substrates, and parts and wholes. This lesson walks through his critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the author begins with a critical remark (وفيه نظر) and then analyzes two interpretations.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author considers two possible meanings of the mashāyikh's definition: (1) separability from both sides, and (2) separability from one side. Find where each is addressed.
The first is introduced with 'إن أرادوا صحة الانفكاك من الجانبين' and the second with 'وإن اكتفوا بجانب واحد'.
3
Examine the counterexamples
For each interpretation, the author provides counterexamples. For two-sided: the world and the Creator (no two-sided separation possible), and accident and substrate. For one-sided: part and whole, essence and attribute. Understand why these refute the definition.
The counterexamples rely on consensus (اتفاق) or logical necessity.
4
Consider the potential rejoinder
The author anticipates an objection: perhaps they meant hypothetical separability, even if impossible in reality. He refutes it by contrasting the world-Creator case with the part-whole case: for the world, we can conceive its existence without the Creator as a hypothesis, but for a part of a whole, it cannot be conceived without the whole because its identity depends on it.
5
Conclude with the key insight
The author concludes that the relation (إضافة) is crucial, and the impossibility of separation is apparent. This shows that otherness cannot be defined by separability alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر لأنهم إن أرادوا صحة الانفكاك من الجانبين، انتقض بالعالم مع الصانع، والعرض مع المحل. إذ لا يتصور وجود العالم مع عدم الصانع لاستحالة عدمه، ولا وجود العرض - كالسواد مثلا بدون المحل . وهو ظاهر مع القطع بالمغايرة اتفاقا. وإن اكتفوا بجانب ،واحد، لزمت المغايرة بين الجزء والكل، وكذا بين الذات والصفة للقطع بجواز وجود الجزء بدون الكل، والذات بدون الصفة. وما ذكروا من استحالة بقاء الواحد بدون العشرة، ظاهر الفساد. لا يقال : المراد إمكان تصور وجود كل منهما، مع عدم الآخر، ولو بالفرض، وإن كان محالا . والعالم قد يتصور موجودا، ثم يطلب بالبرهان ثبوت الصانع، بخلاف الجزء مع الكل، فإنه كما يمتنع وجود العشرة بدون الواحد يمتنع وجود الواحد من العشرة بدون العشرة، إذ لو وجد لما كان واحدا من العشرة. والحاصل أن وصف الإضافة معتبر وامتناع الانفكاك ظاهر.
And there is consideration in it, because if they intended the validity of separation from both sides, it is refuted by the world with the Creator, and the accident with its substrate. For the existence of the world cannot be conceived with the non-existence of the Creator due to the impossibility of His non-existence, nor the existence of the accident (like blackness) without the substrate. This is obvious despite the consensus on otherness. And if they are satisfied with one side, then otherness would be entailed between the part and the whole, and also between the essence and the attribute, due to the certainty of the possibility of the existence of the part without the whole, and the essence without the attribute. And what they mentioned of the impossibility of the one's endurance without the ten is clearly unsound. One should not say: the intended meaning is the possibility of conceiving the existence of each of them with the non-existence of the other, even hypothetically, even if it is impossible. For the world may be conceived as existing, then the proof is sought for the existence of the Creator, unlike the part with the whole, because just as the existence of ten without one is impossible, so too the existence of one of the ten without ten is impossible; for if it existed, it would not be one of the ten. The conclusion is that the description of relation (إضافة) is considered, and the impossibility of separation is apparent.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is the author's critique of the definition of otherness provided by the mashāyikh. The author points out a problem (وفيه نظر) with their definition. He analyzes two possibilities: If they mean separation from both sides (صحة الانفكاك من الجانبين), then counterexamples arise: the world and the Creator are considered other (مغايرة) by consensus, yet the world cannot exist without the Creator, and the Creator's non-existence is impossible, so two-sided separation is not possible. Also, an accident (عرض) like blackness cannot exist without its substrate, yet they are other. So two-sided separation is too strict. If they mean separation from one side only (جانب واحد), then that would entail otherness between a part and the whole (since the part can exist without the whole? Actually, the author says the part can exist without the whole? He says 'جواز وجود الجزء بدون الكل' – which is a common example: a part can be removed from the whole and exist independently, like a hand from a body? But then the whole without the part also exists? The author argues that one-sided separation would make the part and the whole 'other', which is problematic. Also, the essence without the attribute? But attributes are inseparable. He also dismisses the example of the one and the ten as unsound (ظاهر الفساد). He then anticipates a possible rejoinder: that the separation is only imagined hypothetically, even if impossible in reality. He counters that for the world and the Creator, one can imagine the world existing without the Creator hypothetically (to seek proof), but for the part and the whole, you cannot even imagine a part of ten existing without the ten because its identity depends on being a part. The final point is that the relation (إضافة) is crucial, and the impossibility of separation is clear. This critique shows the complexity of defining otherness in the context of divine attributes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Otherness is defined by the possibility of actual separation in existence.
Correction: The author shows that this definition fails, as many things considered other (e.g., world and Creator) cannot actually separate. Otherness does not require actual separability.

Checkpoint
What is one problem with defining otherness as two-sided separability, according to the author?
A) It would make attributes independent of the essence.
B) It would fail to distinguish the world and the Creator, who are considered other despite no two-sided separation.
C) It would make all numbers the same.
D) It would imply that attributes are created.

Key Takeaway
The mashāyikh's definition of otherness fails because no plausible interpretation of separability (two-sided, one-sided, or hypothetical) avoids logical counterexamples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think of your own existence, do you truly believe that you cannot be without your Creator, or do you live as if you could be separated from Him? In what moment today did you act as if your existence was independent?
?
The mashāyikh's definition fails because it cannot account for the relation between essence and attribute. In your life, do you define yourself by your attributes (e.g., success, knowledge) as if they were separate from your essence? How does this lesson correct that?

The Attributes: Non-Difference Claim and Refutation
Author discusses the claim that attributes are distinct and answers an objection with a reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments against the claim that the divine attributes are not distinct from each other, and the author's response to an objection based on predication.

Core Idea
Attributes are not identical nor merely predicates; their distinction is real.

In this passage, the author tackles a sophisticated theological mistake: the idea that because attributes are eternal, they cannot be distinct from each other. He explains why this reasoning fails, and then addresses an objection that tries to save the claim by appealing to the relationship between predicates and subjects. Finally, he cites a marginal view to illustrate the absurdity of that position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of non-difference
Read the opening lines where the author states that some explicitly claimed non-difference between attributes based on their eternity. Notice the reasoning: because non-existence of attributes is inconceivable (they are eternal), they must be identical.
The claim is about the attributes themselves, not their relation to the essence.
2
See why the author rejects it
The author points out that we can conceive of some attributes (like knowledge) existing while others are established only through proof. This shows they are not identical. Also, the notion of non-difference is inconsistent with the accident-substrate relationship.
The argument also extends to relational names: if relations entailed non-difference, all correlatives would be identical, which is absurd.
3
Examine the objection about predicates
An objector proposes that 'not it and not other' could mean unity in existence but difference in concept, like predicates and subjects (e.g., 'man is a writer'). This would preserve both unity and distinction.
The objector uses the analogy of predication, where subject and predicate are one in existence but distinct in meaning.
4
Follow the author's response
The author responds that this applies only to adjectives like 'knowing' (which are predicated of the essence), not to attribute nouns like 'knowledge' (which are not predicated in the same way). It also does not apply to non-predicative parts like 'one of ten' or 'hand of Zayd'.
The author distinguishes between 'ālim (knowing) as an adjective and 'ilm (knowledge) as a noun.
5
Consider the reference to al-Tabṣira
The author cites a view that parts (like one of ten) are other than the whole, held only by Ja‘far ibn Ḥārith, opposed by all Mu‘tazila. This illustrates the weakness of extending non-difference to parts.
The argument shows that if one part were 'other', it would lead to self-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنا نقول : قد صرحوا بعدم المغايرة بين الصفات، بناء على أنها لا يتصور عدمها، لكونها أزلية مع القطع بأنه يتصور وجود البعض، كالعلم مثلا ثم يطلب بالبرهان إثبات البعض الآخر، فعلم أنهم لم يريدوا هذا المعنى، مع أنه لا يستقيم فى العرض مع المحل. ولو اعتبر وصف الإضافة لزم عدم المغايرة بين كل متضايفين كالأب والابن وكالاخوين وكالعلة مع المعلول، بل بين الغيرين لأن الغير من الأسماء الإضافية . ولا قائل بذلك .....
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون مرادهم أنها لا هو، بحسب المفهوم، ولا غيره بحسب الوجود كما هو حكم سائر المحمولات بالنسبة إلى موضوعاتها فإنه يشترط الاتحاد بينهما بحسب الوجود ليصح الحمل والتغاير بحسب المفهوم ليفيد الحمل. كما في قولنا: الإنسان كاتب، بخلاف قولنا الإنسان ،حجر، فإنه لا يصح، وقولنا : الإنسان إنسان فإنه لا يفيد
قلنا : إن هذا إنما يصح في مثل العالم والقادر بالنسبة إلى الذات، لا في مثل العلم والقدرة، مع أن الكلام فيه، ولا في الأجزاء الغير المحمولة كالواحد من العشرة واليد من زيد .
وذكر في التبصرة ) أن كون الواحد من العشرة واليد من زيد ) غيره مما لم يقل به أحد من المتكلمين سوى ) جعفر بن حارث ) وقد خالف في ذلك جميع المعتزلة وعد ذلك من جهالاته . وهذا لأن العشرة اسم الجميع الأفراد ومتناول لكل فرد من آحاده مع أغياره، فلو كان الواحد غيرها لصار غير نفسه، لأنه من العشرة، وأن تكون العشرة بدونه وكذا لو كان يد زيد غيره لكان اليد غير نفسها. هذا كلامه. ولا يخفى ما فيه من الضعف
We say: They have explicitly stated that there is no difference (maghāyara) between the attributes, on the basis that their non-existence is inconceivable, because they are eternal, while it is certain that the existence of some of them, like knowledge for example, is conceivable, and then proof is sought to establish the rest. So it is known that they did not intend this meaning; moreover, it is not consistent for an accident with its substrate. And if the description of relation (iḍāfa) were considered, it would necessitate non-difference between every pair of correlatives, like father and son, and two brothers, and cause and effect; rather, between two things that are other, because 'other' is among relational names. And no one says that.....
If it is said: Why is it not permissible that their intended meaning is that they are not 'it' (huwa) in terms of concept, and not 'other' (ghayr) in terms of existence, as is the case with all predicates (maḥmūlāt) relative to their subjects? For it is required that there be unity (ittiḥād) in terms of existence for the predication (ḥaml) to be valid, and difference (taghāyur) in terms of concept for the predication to be informative, as in our saying: 'Man is a writer,' contrary to our saying: 'Man is a stone,' which is invalid, and our saying: 'Man is man,' which is not informative.
We say: This is only valid for such as 'knowing' ('ālim) and 'powerful' (qādir) in relation to the essence (dhāt), not for such as 'knowledge' ('ilm) and 'power' (qudra), even though the discussion concerns them, nor for parts that are not predicated (ghayr al-maḥmūla), like one of ten and the hand of Zayd.
It is mentioned in al-Tabṣira that the notion of 'one of ten' and 'hand of Zayd' being other [than the whole] is something that no one among the theologians has held except Ja‘far ibn Ḥārith, and he opposed in that all the Mu‘tazila, and that was counted among his ignorances. This is because 'ten' is a name for the totality of individuals and includes each individual of its units along with its others; so if the one were other than it, it would become other than itself, because it is of the ten, and the ten would be without it. Similarly, if the hand of Zayd were other than him, the hand would be other than itself. This is his statement. And it is not hidden what weakness is in it.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a polemical discussion about the nature of the divine attributes. The author addresses a claim that attributes are not different from each other (non-mughayara) based on their eternity. He explains that this cannot mean they are identical in existence because we can conceive of some attributes (like knowledge) existing, and we need proof for others. Then he raises an objection proposing that the intended meaning might be that attributes are neither identical nor different in existence but rather united in existence while distinct in concept, like predicates and subjects. The author responds that this applies only to adjectives (like 'knowing') when predicated of the essence, not to the attribute nouns (like 'knowledge') themselves, nor to parts that are not predicated (like one of ten). He then cites a reference from al-Tabṣira criticizing a view that parts are other than the whole, held only by Ja‘far ibn Ḥārith, which contradicts all Mu‘tazila and is considered ignorance. The author concludes that the weakness of this view is obvious.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since attributes are eternal, they cannot be distinct from each other.
Correction: Eternity does not entail identity. Different eternal realities can coexist; we can conceive of one attribute existing without another, and proof is needed to establish each. Moreover, the accident-substrate distinction and relational names show that non-difference would lead to absurdities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the objection about 'not it and not other' not apply to attributes like 'knowledge' and 'power'?
A) Because they are not predicated of the essence.
B) Because they are unlike 'knowing' and 'powerful'.
C) Because they are parts that are not predicated.
D) Because they are relational names.

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes are distinct from each other and from the essence; they are neither identical nor merely conceptually distinct predicates, and the attempt to reduce them to predication fails for attribute nouns and non-predicative parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God's attributes are not identical to His essence nor mere mental concepts. Do you truly rely on God's knowledge and power as distinct realities when you pray, or do you treat them as interchangeable? This week, identify one specific need that only God's knowledge can address and one only His power can, and pray accordingly.
?
The lesson shows that confusing attributes with predicates can lead to theological error. In your own life, do you reduce God's mercy to a mere feeling or concept, forgetting that it is a real attribute active in your life? Write down one instance where you experienced God's mercy as distinct from His justice, and thank Him for that specific attribute.
?
The 'one of ten' example illustrates the absurdity of treating a part as 'other' than the whole. How often do you compartmentalize your faith, treating one aspect of God's revelation as separate from the rest? Commit to studying one attribute of God this week with the understanding that it is truly distinct yet integrated into the whole of who He is.
