Divine Speech as a Mental Attribute
Defines divine speech as a mental attribute incompatible with silence and defect, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is a mental attribute subsisting in God's essence, incompatible with silence and defect, and how this refutes views treating it as composed of sounds and letters.

Core Idea
God's speech is a mental meaning, not sounds or letters.

This lesson examines a foundational passage on the nature of divine speech. The author defines speech as an attribute that is a meaning subsisting in the essence, and clarifies that it cannot involve silence or defect. The passage also addresses and refutes an opposing view, showing that even the opposites of speech have both verbal and mental aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the refutation of Hanabila and Karamiyya right at the start.
2
Identify the refuted groups
The passage mentions Hanabila and Karamiyya. What do they claim about God's speech? How does the author refute them?
They say it is an accident of sounds and letters yet eternal.
3
Analyze the definitions
The author defines divine speech as an attribute devoid of silence and defect. Understand the precise meanings of 'silence' and 'defect' as given in the text.
Silence is cessation of speaking despite ability; defect is inability of speech organs.
4
Follow the objection and answer
An objection is raised that silence and defect only apply to verbal speech. The author answers by distinguishing inward vs. outward silence and defect.
The answer parallels the mental/verbal distinction.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Summarize in your own words: speech is a mental attribute, and its opposites also have mental and verbal aspects.
This reinforces the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى هذا رد على الحنابلة والكرامية القائلين بأن كلامه تعالى عرض من جنس الأصوات والحروف، ومع ذلك فهو قديم . (وهو) أي الكلام . (صفة) أى معنى قائم بالذات . منافية للسكوت ) الذى هو ترك التكلم مع القدرة عليه. ( والآفة) التى هى عدم مطاوعة الآلات إما بحسب الفطرة كما فى الخرس، أو بحسب ضعفها وعدم بلوغها حد القوة كما في الطفولية . فإن قيل : هذا الكلام إنما يصدق على الكلام اللفظى دون الكلام النفسى . إذ السكوت والخرس إنما ينافي التلفظ. قلنا : المراد السكوت والآفة الباطنيان، بأن لا يريد في نفسه التكلم، أو لا يقدر على ذلك. فكما أن الكلام لفظى ونفسى، فكذا ضده . أعنى السكوت والخرس
And in this is a refutation of the Hanabila and Karamiyya who say that His speech, exalted, is an accident of the genus of sounds and letters, yet it is eternal. (And it) i.e. speech (is an attribute) i.e. a meaning subsisting in the essence. (Devoid of silence) which is the cessation of speaking while having the ability to do so. (And the defect) which is the lack of compliance of the instruments, either by nature as in muteness, or due to their weakness and not reaching the limit of strength as in childhood. If it is said: This speech only applies to verbal speech, not to mental speech, since silence and muteness only oppose utterance. We say: The intended are inward silence and defect, meaning that he does not intend in himself to speak, or is not able to do so. Just as speech is verbal and mental, so is its opposite, namely silence and muteness.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the doctrine that God's speech is a mental attribute. The author begins by stating that this refutes the views of the Hanabila and Karamiyya who claim that God's speech is an accident consisting of sounds and letters, yet still eternal. The correct view is that speech is an attribute, a meaning subsisting in the essence, which is incompatible with silence (the cessation of speaking despite ability) and defect (the inability of instruments to produce sound, either congenital as in muteness or developmental as in childhood). Then an objection is raised: this description of speech as incompatible with silence and defect only applies to verbal speech, not mental speech, because silence and muteness only oppose actual utterance. The answer is that the intended silence and defect are inward: that the person does not intend to speak in himself or is unable to do so. So just as speech has verbal and mental aspects, so its opposite (silence and muteness) also has both aspects.

God's Speech as One Attribute with Multiple Relations
Explains God's speech as one attribute becoming many by relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attribute of speech is a single eternal attribute that becomes command, prohibition, and news through different relations, and why this view upholds tawhid.

Core Idea
God's speech is one; multiplicity comes only from relations, not from the attribute itself.

The passage builds on the concept of divine attributes as taught in Ash'ari theology. It clarifies that God's speech is a single attribute, and the variety of forms (command, prohibition, news) arises due to the different relations to created things, not because the attribute itself is multiple. This understanding preserves the absolute unity of God's essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the central claim that God's speech is a single attribute that becomes many through relations.
Notice the phrase 'صفة واحدة تتكثر'.
2
Examine the analogy with other attributes
The author compares speech to knowledge and power. Each is one eternal attribute; multiplicity is only in relations. Understand why this analogy is used.
This analogy helps to see the pattern of divine attributes.
3
Analyze the objection and the refutation
The passage presents an objection that speech must have divisions. Find the response and the refutation of the view that reduces all to news.
The refutation emphasizes that we know by necessity the difference between command, prohibition, and news.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله تعالى متكلم بها آمر ناه مخبر ) يعنى أنه صفة واحدة تتكثر إلى الأمر والنهى والخبر باختلاف التعلقات كالعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات . فإن كلا منها صفة واحدة قديمة، والتكثر والحدوث إنما هو فى التعلق والإضافات لما أن ذلك اليق بكمال التوحيد، ولأنه لا دليل على تكثر كل منها في نفسها . فإن قيل : هذه أقسام للكلام لا يعقل وجوده بدونها . قلنا : إنه ممنوع، بل إنما يصير أحد تلك الأقسام عند التعلقات وذلك فيما لا يزال . وأما فى الأزل فلا انقسام أصلا. وذهب بعضهم : إلى أنه فى الأزل خبر ومرجع الكل إليه . لأن حاصل الأمر إخبار عن استحقاق الثواب على الفعل، والعقاب على الترك . والنهى على العكس. وحاصل الاستخبار : الخبر عن طلب الإعلام. وحاصل النداء: الخبر عن طلب الإجابة . ورد بأنا نعلم اختلاف هذه المعانى بالضرورة، واستلزام البعض للبعض لا يوجب الاتحاد.
(And God Almighty speaks with it as a commander, prohibitor, informer) meaning that it is one attribute that becomes many into command, prohibition, and news by difference of relations, like knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes. For each of them is one eternal attribute, and multiplicity and occurrence are only in relation and additions, because that is more befitting to the perfection of tawhid, and because there is no proof for multiplicity of each in themselves. If it is said: These are divisions of speech, without which it is not conceivable to exist. We say: That is denied; rather it becomes one of those divisions only at the time of relations, and that is in what is not yet (time). But in pre-eternity there is absolutely no division. And some have held that it is in pre-eternity news, and everything refers back to it, because the summary of command is an informing about deserving reward for action and punishment for abandonment, and prohibition is the opposite. And the summary of interrogation is an informing about a request for information. And the summary of vocative is an informing about a request for response. It was refuted by saying: We know by necessity the difference between these meanings, and one implying another does not necessitate unity.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of God's attribute of speech as one single eternal attribute that, through different relations, becomes command, prohibition, and news. This is analogous to other attributes like knowledge and power: each is one, but multiplicity arises from relations and additional aspects. The author gives two reasons: it better suits the perfection of tawhid (absolute unity), and there is no proof for multiplicity in the essence itself. An objection is raised: these are necessary divisions of speech; speech cannot be conceived without them. The answer is that this is denied: speech only becomes one of these divisions when relations occur, and that is in temporal existence. In pre-eternity, there is no division at all. Then a specific view is mentioned: some say that in pre-eternity, speech is essentially news, and everything else (command, prohibition, etc.) reduces to news. They argue that command is informing about deserving reward for action and punishment for abandonment, prohibition the opposite, interrogation is informing about a request for information, etc. This is refuted by stating that we know by necessity the difference between these meanings, and one implying another does not make them identical.

The Eternity of Command and Prohibition
Al-Nasafi answers the objection that eternal commands without subjects are absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition defends the eternity of God's command and prohibition against the charge of absurdity.

Core Idea
God's eternal commands are meaningful because their objects exist in His knowledge.

This passage addresses a common objection against the eternity of God's attribute of speech. If commands and prohibitions require existing subjects, how can they be eternal? The answer, as al-Nasafi explains, lies in the distinction between the command's obligation and its fulfillment in time, and in God's transcendence beyond temporal categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector claims that eternal commands and prohibitions without existing commanded beings are foolish and futile. Also note the objection about information in the past tense being false.
The objection targets two aspects: command/prohibition and information.
2
Consider the first answer
Al-Nasafi offers a first response: we can simply deny that God's eternal speech is literally command, prohibition, or news. If so, no problem arises.
This is a valid position, but the author also provides a deeper answer.
3
Explore the second answer
If we affirm that God's eternal speech is command and prohibition, then the command is an obligation to be fulfilled when the commanded being exists. The existence of that being in God's knowledge suffices for the command to be meaningful, analogous to imagining a future son.
This shows the command is not foolish; it has a purpose tied to future existence.
4
Reflect on information and time
For information (news), al-Nasafi explains that God's speech is not qualified by past, present, or future because God transcends time. Just as His knowledge is eternal and unchanging, so His speech about events is not temporally bound.
This resolves the objection that past-tense information would be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأمر والنهي بلا مأمور ولا منهى سفه وعبث . والإخبار فى الأزل بطريق المضى كذب محض يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه. قلنا : إن لم نجعل كلامه في الأزل أمرا ونهيا وخيرا فلا إشكال : وإن جعلناه، فالأمر فى الأزل لإيجاب تحصيل المأمور به في وقت وجود المامور وصيرورته أهلا لتحصيله. فيكفى وجود المأمور في علم الأمر، كما إذا قدر الرجل ابنا له، فأمره بأن يفعل كذا بعد الوجود . والإخبار بالنسبة إلى الأزل لا يتصف بشيء من الأزمنة، إذ لا ماضي ولا مستقبل ولا حال بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى، لتنزيهه عن الزمان، كما أن علمه أزلى لا يتغير بتغير الأزمان.
If it is said: Command and prohibition without a commanded and without a prohibited are foolishness and futility. And information in pre-eternity in the past tense is pure falsehood, from which God must be exalted. We say: If we do not make His speech in pre-eternity command, prohibition, and news, then there is no problem. And if we do make it so, then command in pre-eternity is for obligating the achievement of the commanded thing at the time of its existence and its becoming fit for achievement. So the existence of the commanded in the knowledge of the command is sufficient, as when a man imagines a son for himself and commands him to do something after existence. As for information with respect to pre-eternity, it is not qualified by any time, for there is no past, future, or present with respect to God, due to His transcendence above time, just as His knowledge is eternal and does not change with the change of times.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection against the eternity of God's attribute of speech, specifically the aspects of command, prohibition, and news. The objector argues that commands and prohibitions without any existing commanded or prohibited being are absurd (safah wa 'abath). Additionally, if God's speech in pre-eternity includes information in the past tense (e.g., 'Zayd prayed'), that would be false if the event has not yet occurred, which is impossible for God. The answer is twofold. First, we could simply deny that God's speech in pre-eternity is actually command, prohibition, and news in the literal sense; then no problem arises. Second, if we affirm that it is, then the command in pre-eternity is understood as obligating the performance of the commanded act at the time when the commanded being comes into existence and becomes capable. The existence of that being in God's eternal knowledge is sufficient for the command to be meaningful, analogous to a man imagining a future son and commanding him in his mind. As for information (news), it is not qualified by any temporal tense (past, present, future) when related to God, because God transcends time. Just as His knowledge is eternal and does not change, so His speech about events is not bound by time.

الإخبار
الزمان
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Commands in God's knowledge are meaningful because they obligate fulfillment at the time of the subject's existence; the subject's existence in God's knowledge suffices.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Two Senses of the Quran: Eternal and Temporal
Clarifies the dual usage of the term 'Quran'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'Quran' is used in two distinct senses: the eternal divine attribute of speech and the temporally occurring recited words.

Core Idea
The Quran is both eternal in essence and temporal in expression.

After establishing that Allah's speech is pre-eternal and mental, the author clarifies a potential confusion: the word 'Quran' can refer to the eternal attribute of speech (al-kalam al-nafsi al-qadim) or to the recited words that were created in time (al-nazm al-matlu al-hadith). This distinction is essential for understanding theological debates about the createdness or uncreatedness of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation carefully. Note how the author introduces the idea that the Quran is applied in two ways.
The passage ends with 'so he said' indicating that the author will explain further, but the core point is clear.
2
Identify the two senses
Find in the text the two applications: eternal mental speech (الكلام النفسي القديم) and recited created arrangement (النظم المتلو الحادث). Understand that both are referred to as 'Quran'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters. It resolves apparent contradictions: when we say the Quran is eternal, we mean the divine attribute; when we say it is created, we refer to the recited words in time.
This distinction is central to Sunni creed on the nature of the Quran.

Objection and Answer on Eternal Commands
The author answers the objection that eternal commands are absurd without subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the coherence of eternal divine commands and reports despite the absence of existent subjects.

Core Idea
Eternal commands are meaningful because God knows the future subjects.

This passage addresses a common objection against the eternity of God's speech: if commands and prohibitions are eternal, they would be foolish before subjects exist. The author provides two clear responses, showing that divine knowledge suffices and that God transcends time. Let's examine the objection and the careful answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the challenge: eternal commands without subjects are absurd (safah wa 'abath), and eternal past-tense reports would be lies (kadhib).
The objector assumes commands require an existing subject.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
Notice the conditional response: first, if we don't consider God's speech literally as command/prohibition/report, there is no problem; second, if we do, the command is to necessitate the action at the subject's time of existence.
The second part includes an analogy.
3
Grasp the analogy and time-transcendence argument
The author likens it to a man who destines a son and commands him after existence. Also, eternal reports are not tied to past, present, or future because God is beyond time.
God's eternal knowledge underlies the command's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأمر والنهي بلا مأمور ولا منهى سفه وعبث . والإخبار فى الأزل بطريق المضى كذب محض يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه قلنا : إن لم نجعل كلامه في الأزل أمرا ونهيا وخيرا فلا : إشكال : وإن جعلناه، فالأمر فى الأزل لإيجاب تحصيل المأمور به في وقت وجود المامور وصيرورته أهلا لتحصيله. فيكفى وجود المأمور في علم الأمر، كما إذا قدر الرجل ابنا له، فأمره بأن يفعل كذا بعد الوجود والإخبار بالنسبة إلى الأزل لا يتصف بشيء من الأزمنة، إذ لا ماضي ولا مستقبل ولا حال بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى، لتنزيهه عن الزمان، كما أن علمه أزلى لا يتغير بتغير الأزمان.
If it is said: The command and prohibition without a commanded one and a prohibited one are foolishness and futility. And informing in eternity in the past tense is pure lying that God must be exalted from. We say: If we do not make His speech in eternity a command, prohibition, and report, then there is no problem. But if we make it so, then the command in eternity is to necessitate the acquisition of the commanded thing at the time of the existence of the commanded one and his becoming worthy of acquiring it. So the existence of the commanded one in the knowledge of the Commander is sufficient, just as if a man destined a son for himself, then commanded him to do such-and-such after existence. And informing with respect to eternity is not characterized by any of the times, for there is no past, future, or present with respect to God, due to His exaltation above time, just as His knowledge is eternal, not changing with the change of times.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's answer. The objector argues that if God's commands and prohibitions are eternal, then before the existence of the commanded subjects, they would be absurd (safah wa 'abath). Moreover, eternal reports in the past tense about future events would be falsehood. The author responds with two options. First, if one does not regard God's eternal speech as literally being command, prohibition, or report, then no problem arises. But if one does, then the command in eternity is understood as a command to necessitate the commanded action at the time when the subject exists and becomes capable. The existence of the commanded one in God's knowledge suffices for the command to be meaningful. The author gives an analogy: a person who destines a son for himself commands that son after he exists. Similarly, eternal reports are not tied to past, present, or future because God transcends time. God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms

نهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal commands require actual existent subjects at the time of command.
Correction: The author explains that the existence of the subject in God's knowledge is sufficient, and the command is to necessitate the action at the subject's time of existence.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that eternal commands are absurd because there is no commanded subject?
A) By denying that God's speech is eternal.
B) By saying that the subject exists in God's knowledge, which is sufficient.
C) By claiming that the command is only metaphorical.
D) By stating that the subject exists eternally.

Key Takeaway
Eternal divine commands are logically sound because they are rooted in God's eternal knowledge and are tied to the subject's future existence, not requiring contemporaneous subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God's commands are eternal and based on His knowledge, does it challenge your assumption that God speaks to you only in the present moment? How will you start listening to His eternal commands as they apply to your life today?
?
The author says describing God in the past tense is not lying because God transcends time. Do you sometimes imagine God as bound by time, forgetting that He sees your whole life at once? What would change in your worship if you truly lived aware of His atemporal presence?
?
If God's commands are meaningful even before you existed, what does that say about the importance of every command He has given? Which command of God have you been neglecting as if it were outdated, forgetting it is eternally relevant to you?

Why We Say 'The Quran is the Speech of Allah, Uncreated'
Author clarifies the proper phrase: Quran is speech of Allah, uncreated, not merely 'uncreated'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholars specify 'speech of Allah' when affirming the Quran as uncreated, and how this avoids the error of equating eternal inner speech with temporal letters.

Core Idea
The Quran's uncreatedness belongs to its inner meaning, not to its physical form.

In this passage, the author carefully refines the theological phrase 'The Quran is uncreated' by appending 'speech of Allah.' He does this to prevent a gross misunderstanding: that the very letters and sounds are eternal. This lesson unpacks that terminological precision and the refutation of those who held the opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm. Then read the translation. Notice how the author is very precise about wording.
This passage is a classic example of theological terminology refinement.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'Quran'
The author says 'Quran' can refer to eternal inner speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) or to the temporal recited arrangement (al-naẓm al-matlū). Recognise this distinction.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the rest of the lesson.
3
Understand the precaution in wording
Why does the author insist on saying 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated' rather than just 'The Quran is uncreated'? Trace the reasoning about avoiding the implication that sounds and letters are eternal.
The Hanbalis are mentioned as holding that erroneous view.
4
Reflect on the ruling
Note the strong statement: whoever says the Quran is created is a disbeliever. This shows the gravity of the issue.

Source Text

ولما صرح بأزلية الكلام، حاول التنبيه على أن القرآن أيضا قد يطلق على هذا الكلام النفسى القديم كما يطلق على النظم المتلو الحادث. فقال : القرآن كلام الله غير مخلوق والقرآن كلام الله تعالى غير مخلوق) وعقب القرآن بكلام الله ، لما ذكره المشايخ من أنه يقال : القرآن كلام الله تعالى غير مخلوق . ولا يقال : القرآن غير مخلوق، لئلا يسبق إلى الفهم أن المؤلف من الأصوات والحروف .قديم. كما ذهب إليه الحنابلة جهلا أو عنادا، وأقام غير المخلوق مقام غير الحادث، تنبيها على اتحادهما، وقصدا إلى جسرى الكلام على وفق الحديث، حيث قال : القرآن كلام الله « تعالى غير مخلوق » . ومن قال إنه مخلوق، فهو كافر بالله العظيم. وتنصيصا على محل الخلاف بالعبارة المشهورة فيما بين الفريقين، وهو أن القرآن مخلوق أو غير مخلوق . ولهذا تترجم المسألة، بمسألة خلق القرآن . وتحقير الخلاف بيننا وبينهم : يرجع إلى إثبات الكلام النفسي ونفيه، وإلا فنحن لا نقول بقدم الألفاظ والحروف، وهم لا يقولون بحدوث كلام نفسى ودليلنا ما مر أنه ثبت بالإجماع وتواتر النقل عن الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم أنه متكلم، ولا معنى له سوى أنه متصف بالكلام ويمتنع قيام اللفظي الحادث بذاته تعالى. فتعين النفسي القديم. وأما استدلالهم بأن القرآن متصف بما هو من صفات المخلوق وسمات الحدوث من التأليف والتنظيم والإنزال والتنزيل، وكونه
And when he explicitly stated the eternality of speech, he attempted to draw attention to the fact that the Qur'an also may be applied to this eternal inner speech just as it is applied to the recited temporal arrangement. So he said: 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, uncreated.' And he appended 'Qur'an' to 'speech of Allah' because of what the scholars mentioned: that it is said: 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, uncreated,' and it is not said: 'The Qur'an is uncreated,' lest it come to mind that the composition of sounds and letters is eternal, as the Hanbalis held out of ignorance or obstinacy. And he substituted 'uncreated' for 'non-temporal' as a reminder of their identity, and intending to run the speech according to the hadith where he said: 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, uncreated.' Whoever says it is created is an unbeliever in Allah the Great. And as a specification of the point of contention in the famous phrase between the two groups, which is whether the Qur'an is created or uncreated. For this reason the issue is titled 'the issue of the creation of the Qur'an.' The verification of the dispute between us and them reduces to affirming or denying inner speech; otherwise, we do not say that the words and letters are eternal, and they do not say that inner speech is temporal. Our proof is what has passed: that it is established by consensus and the transmitted reports from the prophets (peace be upon them) that He is speaking, and there is no meaning to that except that He is characterized by speech, and it is impossible for temporal verbal speech to subsist in His essence. So the eternal inner speech is determined. As for their argument that the Qur'an is characterized by what are attributes of the created and signs of temporality, such as composition, organization, sending down, revelation, and that it is—
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage addresses the doctrine that the Qur'an is the uncreated speech of Allah. The author begins by connecting to the previous discussion on the eternality of speech. The word 'Qur'an' can refer both to the eternal inner speech (al-kalām al-nafsī al-qadīm) and to the temporal recited arrangement (al-naẓm al-matlū al-ḥādith). The author then explains the precise wording: one should say 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, uncreated' rather than simply 'The Qur'an is uncreated' to avoid the misconception that the physical letters and sounds are eternal—a view attributed to the Hanbalis out of ignorance or obstinacy. The author justifies substituting 'uncreated' (ghayr makhlūq) for 'non-temporal' (ghayr ḥādith) to emphasize their equivalence and to align with hadith. Whoever says the Qur'an is created is a disbeliever. The author then clarifies that the real dispute reduces to whether there is inner speech (kalām nafsī) or not. The author's proof is based on consensus and prophetic reports that God is 'speaking' (mutakallim), which means He is characterized by speech, and it is impossible for temporal verbal speech (al-lafẓī al-ḥādith) to inhere in His essence. Thus, the speech must be eternal inner speech. The author then begins to present the opponents' argument that the Qur'an has characteristics of created things, like composition and being sent down, but this argument is cut off at the end of the page.

حادث
الحنابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'the Quran is uncreated' means the physical letters and sounds are eternal.
Correction: The author clarifies that only inner speech is eternal; the recited words are temporal.

Checkpoint
Why does the author prefer the phrase 'the Qur'an is the speech of Allah, uncreated' over simply 'the Qur'an is uncreated'?
A) To emphasize that the physical words are also eternal.
B) To avoid the misunderstanding that the composition of sounds and letters is eternal.
C) To agree with the Hanbalis.
D) To contradict the hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's uncreatedness applies to its eternal inner meaning, not to its physical recitation.

?

The author warns against saying 'the Quran is uncreated' without qualification because it might lead to false belief. ?

The Proof that the Quran is Uncreated Inner Speech
Author presents proof for uncreated inner speech and reduces dispute to affirming inner speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and transmitted proof that the Quran is eternal inner speech, and how the dispute reduces to affirming or denying inner speech.

Core Idea
God's speech must be eternal because temporal words cannot inhere in His essence.

Having clarified the terminology, the author now presents the core proof that the Quran—as God's speech—is uncreated. The argument rests on consensus and prophetic reports that God is 'speaking,' which necessarily means He possesses an eternal attribute of speech, not temporal sounds and letters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the structure
The passage has three parts: (1) specification of the dispute, (2) reduction to inner speech, (3) the proof. Identify each.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main argument is complete.
2
Understand the reduction of the dispute
The author says the entire debate about whether the Quran is created or uncreated boils down to one thing: do you affirm inner speech or not? Both sides agree that words and letters are temporal.
This shows that the real disagreement is not about the physical words.
3
Examine the proof
The proof uses consensus and transmitted reports that God is 'speaking' (mutakallim). The only way God can be speaking is if He has an attribute of speech, and since temporal speech cannot subsist in God, the speech must be eternal inner speech.
This is a foundational proof for the Ash'ari position.
4
Consider the opponents' argument
The author begins to present the opponents' argument that the Quran has characteristics of created things like composition and being sent down. This argument is cut off but will be answered in later lessons.
Think about how you would respond to that argument.

The Quran is the Uncreated Speech of Allah
Sanusi states the doctrine that the Quran is uncreated and clarifies proper phrasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the orthodox doctrine that the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah and why precise phrasing matters to avoid theological error.

Core Idea
The Quran is Allah's uncreated speech; calling it merely 'uncreated' risks error.

This lesson presents a foundational doctrine of Sunni Islam: the Quran is not created but is the uncreated speech of Allah. Sanusi explains the precise wording used by the orthodox scholars to safeguard against misunderstandings, especially the error of the Hanabila who considered the letters and sounds of the Quran eternal. He also declares that anyone who claims the Quran is created falls into disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the author's language.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Compare the two phrasings
The passage contrasts saying 'القرآن كلام الله غير مخلوق' with just 'القرآن غير مخلوق'. Notice why the longer phrase is preferred.
The shorter phrase risks implying the letters/sounds are eternal.
3
Understand the substitution
Sanusi mentions that the author substituted 'غير مخلوق' for 'غير حادث' to show equivalence and align with a hadith. Reflect on why this substitution matters.
This highlights the unity of the terms.
4
Grasp the ruling
The passage declares that whoever says the Quran is created is a disbeliever. Recognize the seriousness of this theological stance.
5
Connect to the broader issue
The passage concludes by noting that this issue is known as 'the issue of the creation of the Quran'. Think about why this label is used.
This frames the historical debate.

And the Quran is the speech of Allah Most High, uncreated. And he attached the Quran to the speech of Allah, because of what the shaykhs mentioned: that it is said: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah Most High, uncreated.' And it is not said: 'The Quran is uncreated,' lest it leap to the mind that the composed one from sounds and letters is eternal, as the Hanabila went, out of ignorance or stubbornness. And he substituted 'uncreated' for 'non-accidental', as a notification of their unity, and intending to bring the speech in accordance with the hadith, where he said: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah Most High, uncreated.' And to specify the place of dispute with the famous phrase between the two groups, which is that the Quran is created or uncreated. And for this the issue is translated as the issue of the creation of the Quran.

Commentary
This passage defines the orthodox position on the Quran's nature. It begins by stating the doctrine: the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah. The author explains why the phrase 'the Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated' is used rather than just 'the Quran is uncreated': to avoid the misunderstanding that the composed letters and sounds themselves are eternal, as the Hanabila wrongly held. He then notes that the author of the Aqaid replaced the term 'uncreated' with 'non-accidental' to emphasize the identity of the two terms and to align with a hadith. It then declares that whoever says the Quran is created is a disbeliever. Finally, it states that the point of contention between the groups is whether the Quran is created or uncreated, and thus the issue is called the issue of the creation of the Quran.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'the Quran is uncreated' alone is sufficient and correct.
Correction: The correct phrasing is 'the Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated' to avoid implying that the letters and sounds themselves are eternal.

Key Takeaway

Proof for Eternal Inner Speech (Kalam Nafsi)
Sanusi presents the proof for eternal inner speech and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute (kalam nafsi) distinct from created verbal forms.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is an eternal inner attribute, not created letters or sounds.

Sanusi now turns to a critical dispute: is Allah's speech eternal or created? He establishes that the real issue is affirming or denying inner speech (kalam nafsi). He then presents a concise proof based on consensus and the impossibility of created accidents in Allah, and answers two opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the real point of disagreement
Read how Sanusi frames the dispute: it is about affirming or denying inner speech, not about the temporality of letters and sounds.
Both sides agree that the Arabic Quran is created; the disagreement is on the attribute behind it.
2
Follow the core proof
The proof: Allah is called 'speaking' by consensus and revelation; this must mean He has an attribute of speech; created speech cannot subsist in Him; therefore, the speech must be eternal inner speech.
This is a deductive argument based on what can or cannot be attributed to Allah.
3
Analyze the response to the Hanabila argument
The opponents point to the Quran's created features (composition, revelation, Arabic). Sanusi says this argument only refutes the Hanabila, not him, because he agrees the composition is created.
This shows the importance of precision: the discussion is about the meaning, not the form.
4
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazila view
The Mu'tazila say Allah is 'speaking' by creating sounds. Sanusi refutes by analogy: the one who moves is the one in whom motion resides, not the one who creates it. Likewise, the speaker is the one in whom speech resides, not the one who creates it.
This clarifies that 'speaker' refers to a subject of the attribute, not an agent of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيق الخلاف بيننا وبينهم : يرجع إلى إثبات الكلام النفسي ونفيه، وإلا فنحن لا نقول بقدم الألفاظ والحروف، وهم لا يقولون بحدوث كلام نفسى ودليلنا ما مر أنه ثبت بالإجماع وتواتر النقل عن الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم أنه متكلم، ولا معنى له سوى أنه متصف بالكلام ويمتنع قيام اللفظي الحادث بذاته تعالى. فتعين النفسي القديم. وأما استدلالهم بأن القرآن متصف بما هو من صفات المخلوق وسمات الحدوث من التأليف والتنظيم والإنزال والتنزيل، وكونه عربيا مسموعا فصيحا معجزا . إلى غير ذلك فإنما يقوم حجة على الحنابلة لا علينا. لأنا قائلون بحدوث التنظيم وإنما الكلام في المعنى القديم. والمعتزلة لما لم يمكنهم إنكار كونه تعالى متكلما ذهبوا إلى أنه متكلم بمعنى إيجاد الأصوات والحروف فى محالها، أو إيجاد أشكال الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ، وإن لم يقرأ على اختلاف بينهم. وأنت خبير بأن المتحرك : من قامت به الحركة، لا من أوجدها وإلا لصح اتصاف البارى بالأعراض المخلوقة له - تعالى عن ذلك علوا كبيرا.
And the verification of the dispute between us and them goes back to affirming inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) and denying it. Otherwise, we do not say with the pre-eternity of letters and sounds, and they do not say with the origination of inner speech. And our proof is what has passed: that it is established by consensus and successive transmission from the prophets, peace be upon them, that He is speaking (mutakallim), and there is no meaning to that except that He is described with speech. And it is impossible for created verbal speech to subsist in His essence, Most High. So the eternal inner speech is determined. And as for their argument that the Quran is described with what are attributes of the creature and marks of temporality—such as composition, organization, revelation, sending down, and its being Arabic, audible, eloquent, miraculous, etc.—this only stands as a proof against the Hanabila, not against us. For we say the composition is originated, but the discussion is about the eternal meaning. And the Mu'tazila, since they could not deny that He, Most High, is speaking, went to saying that He is speaking in the sense of creating sounds and letters in their loci, or creating the forms of writing in the Preserved Tablet, with differences among them. And you are aware that the mover is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who brings it into existence; otherwise, it would be correct to describe the Creator with the accidents created by Him—exalted is He above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the doctrine of eternal inner speech (kalam nafsi). It first identifies the real point of disagreement: affirming or denying inner speech. The author clarifies that neither they nor their opponents hold the extreme views: they do not say the letters and sounds are eternal, and the opponents do not say inner speech is originated. Then he presents the proof: it is established by consensus and successive transmission from the prophets that Allah is speaking (mutakallim), and the meaning of that is that He is described with speech. Since it is impossible for created, verbal speech to subsist in Allah, it is necessary that eternal inner speech exists. Then he responds to an objection: the opponents argue that the Quran has characteristics of creatures (composition, revelation, being Arabic, etc.). The author says this argument only works against the Hanabila (who take the external Quran as eternal), not against us, because we affirm the createdness of the composition; the dispute is about the eternal meaning. Then he describes the Mu'tazila position: they say Allah is speaking by creating sounds and letters in some location or on the Preserved Tablet. The author refutes this by analogy: the one who moves is the one in whom motion resides, not the one who creates motion; if that were so, Allah would be described with created accidents. This refutation shows the Mu'tazila view is impossible.

Key Terms

اللفظي الحادث
المتحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof for kalam nafsi relies solely on consensus without addressing the impossibility of created accidents.
Correction: The proof combines consensus (that Allah is speaking) with the rational principle that created accidents cannot subsist in Allah, necessitating an eternal attribute.

Checkpoint

C) Because the Mu'tazila admit it
D) Because the Hanabila said so

Key Takeaway
Allah's attribute of speech is eternal and internal, distinct from any created verbal expression.

The Mu'tazila reduced Allah's speech to a created accident. In what subtle ways are you tempted to reduce Allah's attributes—by explaining away His majesty to fit your intellect? Answering the Strongest Mu'tazili Shubha
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazili argument from the Quran's physicality and gives the orthodox answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the orthodox response refutes the Mu'tazili argument that the Quran's physical manifestations prove it is created.

Core Idea
The Quran's written and recited forms express an eternal meaning, not the meaning itself.

The Mu'tazila argue that since the Quran is written in copies, recited by tongues, and heard by ears, it must be temporal and created. The orthodox response, however, distinguishes between the physical expressions and the eternal speech of Allah. In this lesson, we examine the strongest doubt of the Mu'tazila and the precise answer given by Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Sanusi first states the doubt, then quotes from the creed to answer.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili doubt
Find the premise that the Quran is transmitted between covers and recited, and the conclusion that it must be temporal. Understand why this seems strong.
The Mu'tazili reasoning relies on observable qualities being signs of creation.
3
Understand the orthodox answer
See how Sanusi explains that the Quran is 'written, preserved, recited, heard' yet does not indwell in these things. Grasp the key distinction between the eternal meaning and its physical expressions.
The answer preserves both the physical presence of the Quran and its eternal nature.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The passage ends with 'As is said: fire...' – think about how the word 'fire' is not fire itself. This illustrates the distinction between signifier and signified.
The analogy is incomplete but suggests that the physical Quran points to the eternal speech.

ومن أقوى شبه المعتزلة : أنكم متفقون على أن القرآن اسم لما نقل إلينا، بين دفتي المصاحف تواترا. وهذا يستلزم كونه مكتوبا في المصاحف، مقروءا بالالسن مسموعا بالآذان . وكل ذلك من سمات الحدوث بالضرورة فأشار إلى الجواب بقوله (وهو) أى القرآن الذى هو كلام الله تعالى . مكتوب في مصاحفنا أى بأشكال الكتابة وصور الحروف الدالة عليه محفوظ في قلوبنا ) أي بالألفاظ المخيلة. (مقروء بألستنا ) بالحروف الملفوظة المسموعة . مسموع بآذاننا بذلك أيضا . غير حالاً فيها أى مع ذلك ليس حالا في المصاحف ولا في القلوب والألسنة والآذان، بل هو معنى قديم قائم بذات الله تعالى يلفظ ويسمع بالنظم الدال عليه، ويحفظ بالنظم المخيل، ويكتب بنقوش وصور وأشكال موضوعة للحروف الدالة عليه. كما يقال : النار
And among the strongest doubts of the Mu'tazila is: you all agree that the Quran is a name for what has been transmitted to us between the covers of the codices via successive transmission. And this entails that it is written in the codices, recited by tongues, heard by ears. And all of that is necessarily among the marks of temporality. So he indicated the answer by his saying: 'And it'—i.e., the Quran which is the speech of Allah Most High—'is written in our copies' i.e., by the forms of writing and shapes of letters indicating it; 'preserved in our hearts' i.e., by imagined words; 'recited by our tongues' i.e., by uttered, heard letters; 'heard by our ears' also by that. 'It does not indwell in them' i.e., despite that it is not indwelling in the copies nor in the hearts, tongues, and ears. Rather, it is an eternal meaning subsisting in the essence of Allah Most High, which is uttered and heard by the composition indicating it, and preserved by the imaginary composition, and written by engravings, figures, and shapes placed for the letters indicating it. As is said: fire...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the strongest Mu'tazili doubt and its answer. The objection: you all agree that the Quran is what has been transmitted to us in the codices, and this implies it is written in copies, recited by tongues, heard by ears—all of which are marks of temporality. So it must be created. The author then gives the answer by explaining the statement of the earlier creed: 'It (the Quran) is written in our copies, preserved in our hearts, recited by our tongues, heard by our ears, yet it does not indwell in them.' The explanation: it is written by the shapes of letters, preserved by imagined words, recited by uttered sounds, heard by ears, but it is not actually present in these things. Rather, it is an eternal meaning subsisting in the essence of Allah, which is expressed through the composed words, preserved through imagined words, and written through shapes. The analogy with fire (though incomplete) suggests that the word 'fire' is not fire itself, just as the written or spoken Quran is not the eternal speech itself.

محفوظ
معنى قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran being written and recited proves that it is created.
Correction: The Quran is written and recited only as expressions of the eternal meaning; the meaning itself is uncreated, subsisting in Allah's essence.

The Quran's physical manifestations are indicators of an eternal, uncreated meaning that subsists in Allah's essence.

Answer to the Mu'tazila's Strongest Argument on the Quran's Createdness
Mu'tazila objection and Ash'ari response using indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris respond to the Mu'tazila's strongest argument that the Quran must be created because it is written, recited, and heard.

Core Idea
The Quran's physical forms (letters/sounds) indicate the eternal speech; they are not the speech itself.

The Mu'tazila present a powerful objection: since the Quran is transmitted in mushafs, recited, and heard—all temporal acts—it must be created. How can the Ash'aris maintain it is eternal? The answer lies in a crucial distinction between the ancient meaning (kalām qadīm) and its physical indicators. This passage explains that distinction with a clarifying analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the response.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's argument

They consider all these to be characteristics of temporality.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
Note how the author distinguishes between the indicator (letters, sounds, mental forms) and the indicated (the eternal meaning). The Quran is written/recited only in terms of these indicators, not the meaning itself.
The fire analogy reinforces that the reality (fire) is not the same as its written or spoken form.

ومن أقوى شبه المعتزلة : أنكم متفقون على أن القرآن اسم لما نقل إلينا، بين دفتي المصاحف تواترا. وهذا يستلزم كونه مكتوبا في المصاحف، مقروءا بالالسن مسموعا بالآذان . وكل ذلك من سمات الحدوث بالضرورة فأشار إلى الجواب بقوله (وهو) أى القرآن الذى هو كلام الله تعالى . مكتوب في مصاحفنا أى بأشكال الكتابة وصور الحروف الدالة عليه محفوظ في قلوبنا ) أي بالألفاظ المخيلة. And among the strongest arguments of the Mu'tazila: that you agree that the Quran is a name for that which has been transmitted to us, between the two covers of the mushafs, by tawatur. And this entails that it is written in the mushafs, recited by tongues, heard by ears. And all of that is from the characteristics of temporality necessarily. So he pointed to the answer by his saying: 'And it'—i.e. the Quran which is the speech of Allah—'is written in our mushafs'—i.e. by the forms of writing and shapes of letters that indicate it—'preserved in our hearts'—i.e. by the mental expressions—'recited by our tongues'—by the pronounced letters that are heard—'heard by our ears'—by that as well—'not residing in them'—i.e. despite that it is not residing in the mushafs nor in the hearts, tongues, and ears. As it is said: 'Fire is a burning substance; it is remembered by utterance and written with a pen, and it does not necessitate that the reality of fire be a sound or a letter.'
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's strongest argument and the Ash'arite response. The Mu'tazila argue: you all agree that the Quran is what is transmitted in the mushafs, recited, and heard—all of which are temporal characteristics. So it must be created. The author responds by clarifying that the Quran is written, recited, and memorized only in terms of the forms (shapes, sounds, mental images) that indicate the ancient meaning, not that the ancient meaning itself resides in those things. The example of fire illustrates this: fire is a burning substance; we utter the word 'fire' and write it, but the reality of fire is not the sound or the letter. Similarly, the ancient speech (kalām qadīm) is an attribute of Allah; the written letters, recitations, and memories are indicators (dālil) of it, not the speech itself.

The Four Modes of Existence Applied to the Quran
The author explains the four modes of existence and how they apply to the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four modes of existence (external, mental, verbal, written) and how they clarify seemingly contradictory statements about the Quran.

Core Idea
The Quran exists in four modes: external, mental, spoken, written—only the first is eternal.

This passage introduces a key methodological tool: the fourfold existence of things. By distinguishing between a thing's reality in the external world, in the mind, in speech, and in writing, we can resolve apparent contradictions in statements about the Quran. When we say "the Quran is uncreated," we refer to its eternal reality; when we say "I recited half the Quran," we refer to its created verbal or written forms. This distinction helps avoid confusion about the nature of Allah's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: first the four modes are listed, then their hierarchy, then application to Quranic statements.
Pay attention to the terms: a'yān, adhhān, 'ibāra, kitāba.
2
Identify the four modes
List the four modes of existence mentioned: existence in external entities, in minds, in verbal expression, and in writing. Understand each one.
These correspond to four levels of reality for any thing.
3
Understand the hierarchical relationship
Notice the chain: writing indicates verbal expression, verbal expression indicates mental concept, mental concept indicates external reality. This hierarchy shows that each level points to the previous one.
This hierarchy is important for interpreting statements about the Quran.
4
See how Quranic statements map to modes
Read the examples: 'the Quran is uncreated' refers to external reality (eternal attribute); 'I recited half the Quran' refers to verbal expression; 'I memorized the Quran' refers to mental existence; 'forbidden to touch' refers to the written form.
Note that only the first mode (external) is described with eternal attributes; the others are created.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can match a statement to its mode.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيقه أن للشيء وجودا في الأعيان، ووجودا في الأذهان، ووجودا في العبارة ووجودا فى الكتابة. والكتابة تدل على العبارة. وهى على ما في الأذهان. وهو على ما في الأعيان. فحيث يوصف القرآن بما هو من لوازم القديم، كما في قولنا : القرآن غير مخلوق . فالمراد حقيقته الموجودة في الخارج. وحيث يوصف بما هو من لوازم المخلوقات والمحدثات يراد به الألفاظ المنطوقة والمسموعة. كما في قولنا : قرأت نصف القرآن أو المخيلة كما في قولنا حفظت القرآن أو الأشكال المنقوشة، كما في قولنا : يحرم للمحدث مس القرآن.
And its verification: that a thing has existence in external entities (a'yān), existence in minds (adhhān), existence in verbal expression ('ibāra), and existence in writing (kitāba). Writing indicates verbal expression, and it [verbal expression] indicates what is in minds, and it [what is in minds] indicates what is in external entities. So wherever the Quran is described with what is among the necessary concomitants of the eternal, as in our saying: 'The Quran is uncreated', what is intended is its reality existing in the external. And wherever it is described with what is among the necessary concomitants of created and originated things, what is intended by it is the pronounced and heard utterances—as in our saying: 'I recited half of the Quran'—or the mental [expressions]—as in our saying: 'I memorized the Quran'—or the inscribed forms—as in our saying: 'It is forbidden for the one in a state of impurity to touch the Quran.'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage provides a tahqīq (verification) of the Quran's nature using the theory of four modes of existence. Every thing (shay') has four levels: existence in external reality (a'yān), in the mind (adhhān), in verbal expression ('ibāra), and in writing (kitāba). These levels are hierarchical: writing indicates verbal expression, which indicates the mental concept, which indicates the external reality. Applying this to the Quran: when we say 'the Quran is uncreated', we refer to its external reality—the ancient attribute of Allah. When we say 'I recited half the Quran' or 'I memorized the Quran' or 'it is forbidden to touch the Quran without ablution', we refer to the verbal, mental, or written manifestations, which are created. Thus, statements about the Quran are understood according to which mode of existence is intended.

Key Terms
الأعيان
الأذهان
العبارة
الكتابة
القديم
المخلوقات
المحدثات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The written Quran is uncreated because it is the word of Allah.
Correction: The written Quran is a creation; only the eternal speech in Allah's attribute is uncreated. The writing indicates the verbal expression, which indicates the mental concept, which indicates the external reality—only that external reality is uncreated.

Checkpoint
When someone says 'I memorized the Quran', to which mode of existence does this refer?
A) Existence in external entities
B) Existence in the mind
C) Existence in verbal expression
D) Existence in writing

Key Takeaway
The Quran's attributes differ based on which mode of existence is referred to: only its external reality is eternal, while its verbal, mental, and written forms are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran aloud, do you ever confuse the sound of your own voice with the eternal speech of Allah? How can you cultivate a deeper awareness that what you recite is a created reflection of an uncreated reality, and what difference would that make in your khushūʿ?
?
You hold a copy of the Quran written on paper. If that physical book is created and subject to decay, does that diminish its value for you? How should your understanding of the four modes affect the reverence you show when touching the Mushaf?
?
Which mode of the Quran do you most often engage with—recitation, memorization, listening, or reading? In your daily practice, do you ever pause to connect that created mode back to the external, eternal reality it points to? What one change can you make this week to anchor your interaction with the Quran in the awareness of its divine origin?

Can Allah's Eternal Speech Be Heard?
The author presents the disagreement on whether the eternal speech can be heard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing scholarly opinions on whether Allah's eternal attribute of speech can be heard by created beings, and how the Quranic verse 'until he hears the speech of Allah' is interpreted in light of this dispute.

Core Idea
Scholars differ: can the eternal, uncreated speech of Allah be heard by human ears?

This passage addresses a complex question in Islamic theology: is the eternal speech of Allah—His attribute of speech—audible to created beings? Two major positions are reported: Imam al-Ash'ari considered it permissible, while al-Isfara'ini and Imam al-Maturidi denied it. The passage begins to interpret Quran 9:6 in this context, though the text is cut off.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two distinct opinions: one allows hearing the eternal speech, the other forbids it. Highlight the names of the scholars associated with each view.
The text ends abruptly when interpreting the verse; note that the full argument is missing.
2
Identify the key scholars and their stances
List the scholars mentioned: al-Ash'ari (permitted), al-Isfara'ini (forbade), and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (chose the forbidding). Understand that this is a known ikhtilāf within Sunni theology.
Al-Maturidi's position is described as his 'choice' (ikhtiyār), indicating a preferred view.
3
Reflect on the incomplete verse interpretation
The passage begins to explain Quran 9:6 but is cut off. Consider how the verse might be interpreted differently based on each position. The common understanding is that the 'hearing' refers to the created expression (lafẓ) rather than the eternal attribute itself.
This shows how theological positions shape Quranic exegesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلام القديم الذى هو صفة الله تعالى . فذهب الأشعرى إلى أنه يجوز أن يسمع . ومنعه الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراينى وهو اختيار الشيخ أبي منصور - رحمه الله - فمعنى قوله تعالى: ﴿ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلامَ اللهِ ﴾ [التوبة: ٦] يسمع

Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether the eternal speech of Allah (al-kalām al-qadīm) can be heard by created beings. It reports two positions: Imam al-Ash'ari held that it is permissible (yajūz) for it to be heard, while al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini denied that (mana'a), and this latter view is the choice (ikhtiyār) of al-Shaykh Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi). The passage then begins to interpret the verse 'until he hears the speech of Allah' (Quran 9:6) in light of this, but the text is cut off. The implication is that the hearing mentioned in the verse refers to the created expression that indicates the ancient speech, not the speech itself.

Applying the Four Levels to the Quran
The author applies the fourfold existence to the Quran's descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four levels of existence apply to the Quran, distinguishing between its eternal attribute and its temporal manifestations in speech, memory, and writing.

Core Idea
The Quran is one in its eternal essence but multiple in its temporal expressions.

This passage applies the fourfold classification of existence to the Quran itself. The Quran, as the eternal speech of Allah, can be described in different ways depending on the aspect we are referring to. The author shows us how to correctly interpret statements about the Quran by identifying which level of existence is intended.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author gives specific examples for each level.
2
Identify the eternal aspect
The passage first mentions describing the Quran with what belongs to the eternal. This refers to its uncreated essence as an attribute of Allah.
Look for the phrase 'غير مخلوق'.
3
Identify the three temporal aspects
The passage then lists three temporal descriptions: uttered words, memorized form, and written shapes. Each corresponds to a different created manifestation.
Examples: 'قرأت نصف القرآن', 'حفظت القرآن', 'مس القرآن'.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider how this distinction resolves apparent contradictions. The Quran is both eternal and temporal depending on which level is intended.
This is key to understanding correct Islamic doctrine about the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فحيث يوصف القرآن بما هو من لوازم القديم، كما في قولنا : القرآن غير مخلوق . فالمراد حقيقته الموجودة في الخارج. وحيث يوصف بما هو من لوازم المخلوقات والمحدثات يراد به الألفاظ المنطوقة والمسموعة. كما في قولنا : قرأت نصف القرآن أو المخيلة كما في قولنا حفظت القرآن أو الأشكال المنقوشة، كما في قولنا : يحرم للمحدث مس القرآن.
So when the Quran is described with what belongs to the attributes of the eternal, as in our saying: 'The Quran is uncreated,' then what is intended is its reality existing externally. And when it is described with what belongs to the attributes of created and originated things, then what is intended by it is the uttered and audible words, as in our saying: 'I read half of the Quran,' or the memorized form as in our saying: 'I memorized the Quran,' or the inscribed shapes as in our saying: 'It is forbidden for a person in a state of ritual impurity to touch the Quran.'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage applies the fourfold classification to the Quran. When we say the Quran is uncreated (qadim), we mean its eternal attribute of speech (the internal speech). When we describe it with temporal attributes (e.g., reading, memorizing, touching), we refer to its verbal, mental, or written manifestations. The author gives explicit examples for each temporal aspect: uttered words for reading, mental form for memorizing, and written shapes for physical copies.

Correction: The passage shows that the Quran can be correctly described in multiple ways depending on context: its eternal essence is uncreated, while its verbal, mental, and written forms are created.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's descriptions vary based on which level of existence is being referenced — its eternal reality or its temporal manifestations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Debate on Hearing the Eternal Speech of Allah
The author presents a debate on hearing the eternal speech of Allah and interprets a key Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on whether the eternal attribute of speech can be heard, and how the verse about Musa (AS) hearing Allah's speech is interpreted.

Core Idea
Hearing the eternal attribute of Allah's speech is a matter of scholarly disagreement.

This passage addresses a subtle theological question: can the eternal attribute of Allah's speech (kalam nafsi) be heard? The Ash'ari scholar al-Ash'ari held that it is possible, while the Maturidi scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfaraini forbade it, a position preferred by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. To reconcile the verse where Musa (AS) hears Allah's speech, it is interpreted as hearing an indicative sound, not the attribute itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the core debate.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that al-Ash'ari permits hearing, while al-Isfaraini forbids it.
The author notes that the latter is the choice of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.
3
Understand the interpretation of the verse
The verse is explained as hearing something that indicates speech, not the attribute directly.
4
Reflect on the conclusion about Musa
Musa heard a sound indicating Allah's speech, and because it was without intermediary, he was called Kalim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلام القديم الذي هو صفة الله تعالى. فذهب الأشعري إلى أنه يجوز أن يسمع. ومنعه الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني وهو اختيار الشيخ أبي منصور - رحمه الله. فمعنى قوله تعالى: {حتى يسمع كلام الله} (التوبة:6) يسمع ما يدل عليه كما يقال: سمعت علم فلان. فموسى عليه السلام سمع صوتا، دالا على كلام الله تعالى. لكن لما كان بلا واسطة الكتاب والملك، خص باسم الكليم.
As for the eternal speech which is an attribute of Allah ta'ala: al-Ash'ari held that it may be heard. But al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfaraini forbade that, and this is the choice of Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him). So the meaning of His saying {until he hears the speech of Allah} is that he hears what indicates it, just as it is said: 'I heard the knowledge of so-and-so.' So Musa (peace be upon him) heard a sound that indicated the speech of Allah. But because it was without the intermediary of the book or the angel, he was specially called the Kalim (the one spoken to).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi) can be heard. The Ash'ari school allows it, while Abu Ishaq al-Isfaraini denies it. The author notes that the latter is the choice of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. The verse about Musa hearing the speech of Allah is interpreted as hearing an indicative sound, not the eternal attribute directly. Musa's unique distinction of hearing this sound without intermediary earned him the title 'Kalim Allah'.

Key Takeaway
Hearing the eternal attribute of Allah's speech is debated; the preferred view is that it is not heard but rather an indicative sound is heard, as with Musa (AS).

Kalam Allah as a Homonym
Objection and answer on 'kalam Allah' as a homonym.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'kalam Allah' is a homonym and how that resolves the objection against calling the composed Quran truly the speech of Allah.

Core Idea
'Kalam Allah' is a homonym with two distinct literal meanings, not a metaphor.

This passage addresses a critical objection. If 'kalam Allah' were strictly the eternal attribute, the composed Quran could not be called His speech. The answer introduces the concept of homonymy, preserving the reality of both the eternal and temporal senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the full Arabic and translation. The objector presents two arguments: (1) if 'kalam Allah' is only for the eternal meaning, then calling the composed Quran 'kalam Allah' would be false; (2) the miracle of inimitability is directed at the composed Quran, not the eternal attribute.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said...' introduces the objection.
2
Identify the two arguments in the objection
The objection has two parts: the first concerns the possibility of denial, the second concerns the miracle. Understand each one before moving to the answer.
3
Study the answer and how homonymy resolves it
The answer states that 'kalam Allah' is a homonym (mushtarak) with two distinct literal meanings: eternal internal speech (attribute) and temporal verbal speech (creation). This means both usages are equally literal, so denial is invalid and miracle applies to the verbal speech.
Homonymy avoids both falseness and philosophical problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو كان كلام الله تعالى حقيقة في المعنى القديم، مجازا في النظم المؤلف لصح نفيه عنه بأن يقال : ليس النظم المنزل المعجز المفصل إلى السور والآيات كلام الله تعالى. والإجماع على خلافه. وأيضا : المعجز المتحدى به هو كلام الله تعالى حقيقة مع القطع بأن ذلك كما يتصور في النظم المؤلف المفصل إلى السور إذ لا معنى لمعارضة الصفة القديمة. قلنا : التحقيق أن كلام الله تعالى اسم مشترك بين الكلام النفسي القديم - ومعنى الإضافة: كونه صفة الله تعالى - وبين اللفظي الحادث المؤلف من السور والآيات - ومعنى الإضافة: أنه مخلوق الله تعالى، ليس من تأليفات المخلوقين. فلا يصح النفي أصلا، ولا يكون الإعجاز والتحدي إلا في كلام الله تعالى.
If it is said: If 'kalam Allah' were true (haqiqa) for the eternal meaning and metaphorical (majaz) for the composed wording, then it would be valid to deny it of the latter by saying: 'The miraculous, revealed wording divided into suras and verses is not the speech of Allah.' But there is consensus to the contrary. Also: The miracle with which challenge is made is truly the speech of Allah, with the certainty that this can only be conceived in the composed wording divided into suras, since there is no sense in challenging the eternal attribute. We say: The verification is that 'kalam Allah' is a homonym (mushtarak) between the eternal internal speech (kalam nafsi) - the meaning of the attribution being that it is an attribute of Allah - and the temporal verbal speech (kalam lafzi) composed of suras and verses - the meaning of the attribution being that it is a creation of Allah, not from the compositions of creatures. So the denial is not valid at all, and inimitability and challenge are only in the speech of Allah.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that if 'kalam Allah' is strictly the eternal attribute (haqiqa), then the composed Quran cannot be called kalam Allah truly, which contradicts consensus. Also, the challenge of inimitability (i'jaz) is directed at the composed Quran, not the attribute. The answer: 'kalam Allah' is a homonym (mushtarak) with two distinct meanings: (1) the eternal attribute, attributed to Allah as His attribute, and (2) the created verbal speech, attributed to Allah as His creation. Thus, the composed Quran is truly called kalam Allah in the second sense. Hence, denial is invalid, and miracle (i'jaz) is in the created verbal speech.

Key Terms

مجاز
نظم مؤلف
إعجاز
تحدي
اسم مشترك
كلام نفسي
كلام لفظي
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'mushtarak' means the word is ambiguous or unclear.
Correction: In kalam terminology, 'mushtarak' means the word has two or more distinct literal meanings, each used in different contexts, not that it is vague.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the status of 'kalam Allah' in relation to the composed Quran?
A) It is metaphorical (majaz).
B) It is a homonym (mushtarak) with a created meaning.
C) It is false (batil).
D) It is only for the eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
The composed Quran is truly called the speech of Allah because 'kalam Allah' is a homonym for the eternal attribute and the created verbal speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you recognize that the words you utter are a creation of Allah, yet they convey His eternal attribute of speech? How does this dual reality affect the reverence and focus you bring to every verse?
?
The objector thought that if the composed Quran were only metaphorically God's speech, it could be denied. Have you ever faced doubts about the divine origin of the Quran when encountering scientific or historical challenges? How does the precise answer of homonymy fortify your certainty that every letter is truly God's speech?
?
If 'kalam Allah' is a homonym, then calling the Quran 'God's speech' is not a mere metaphor but a literal truth in a distinct sense. How does this shift your personal accountability when you hear or recite the Quran? Will you now treat the mus'haf with even greater awe, knowing it is literally the creation of Allah that embodies His eternal word?

Investigators' Clarification on 'Kalam Allah'
The author notes that some investigators say 'kalam Allah' means an eternal meaning, not opposed to utterance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view of some investigators that 'kalam Allah' refers to an eternal meaning, distinct from the verbal utterance and its denotation.

Core Idea
'Kalam Allah' is an eternal attribute, not the words themselves.

This lesson clarifies a crucial nuance from the investigators (muhaqqiqun) regarding the term 'kalam Allah'. They explain that when our scholars say 'kalam Allah', they do not mean the uttered words or their meanings; rather, they mean an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah. This helps avoid the misunderstanding that the divine speech is temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its literal translation. Notice that the author is clarifying a specific interpretation from some investigators.
The sentence is compact; try to parse each term.
2
Identify the core claim
The key phrase is 'معنى قديم ليس في مقابلة اللفظ' – an eternal meaning not in opposition to utterance. Understand that the eternal attribute is not something that opposes or contradicts the verbal expression.
The phrase 'مقابلة اللفظ' means being set in opposition to the utterance, i.e., being a rival or alternative.
3
Distinguish from denotation
The passage clarifies that the eternal meaning is not the denotation (madlul) of the utterance. So when the word 'Quran' is used, it does not refer to the meanings of the sentences but to the eternal attribute of speech.
This guards against the misconception that divine speech is merely the meanings of the words.
4
Summarize the theological point
Conclude that the investigators' view emphasizes that kalam Allah is an eternal attribute, distinct from the temporal uttered words. This upholds the doctrine that Allah's speech is eternal.
This is a subtle but important clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعض المحققين إلى أن المعنى في قول مشايخنا كلام الله تعالى معنى قديم ليس في مقابلة اللفظ حتى يراد به مدلول اللفظ
And some investigators held that the meaning in the statement of our shaykhs 'kalam Allah' is an eternal meaning, not in opposition to the utterance (lafz) such that by it the denotation of the utterance is intended.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This final note explains that when our scholars use the term 'kalam Allah', they mean the eternal meaning (ma'na qadim), not something opposed to the verbal expression. So it does not refer to the denotation of the words (e.g., the sentences) but to the attribute itself.

Misconception: Some might think that 'kalam Allah' refers to the denotation of the words or that it is something opposed to the utterance.
Correction: The investigators clarify that it is an eternal meaning not in opposition to utterance, and it does not mean the denotation. It is the eternal attribute itself.

Key Takeaway
