Defending the Eternal and Revealed Speech
Taftazani presents and refutes two objections against the view of kalam as eternal meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defends the doctrine that the eternal attribute of speech and the revealed Qur'an are both truly called 'kalam Allah' through the concept of a shared term.

Core Idea
'Kalam Allah' is a shared term between the eternal attribute and the created Qur'an.

In this passage, Taftazani addresses two key objections that arise if only the eternal attribute is truly 'kalam Allah.' Critics argue that the composed Qur'an could then be denied as Allah's speech, and the miraculous challenge would be meaningless. Taftazani's nuanced answer introduces the term as shared between two realities, preserving both the eternal attribute and the revealed text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two objections
Read the Arabic passage and identify the two objections: the denial of the composed Qur'an as kalam, and the problem of the miraculous challenge being directed at an attribute.
The first objection appeals to consensus, the second to the nature of the challenge.
2
Grasp Taftazani's resolution
Focus on the phrase 'ism mushtarak' (shared term). Notice that Taftazani distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created composition, yet both are truly called 'kalam Allah' due to the shared term.
The term is not purely metaphorical; it applies to both in different respects.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this resolution maintains both the unity of the attribute and the reality of the revealed Qur'an as Allah's speech.
This clarifies the miraculous challenge and the consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو كان كلام الله تعالى حقيقة في المعنى القديم، مجازا فى النظم المؤلف لصح نفيه عنه بأن يقال : ليس النظم المنزل المعجز المفصل إلى السور والآيات كلام الله تعالى. والإجماع على خلافه وأيضا : المعجز المتحدى به هو كلام الله تعالى حقيقة مع القطع بان ذلك كما يتصور فى النظم المؤلف المفصل إلى السور إذ لا معنى لمعارضة الصفة القديمة. قلنا : التحقيق أن كلام الله تعالى اسم مشترك بين الكلام النفسى القديم. ومعنى الإضافة: كونه صفة الله تعالى وبين اللفظي الحادث المؤلف من السور والآيات ومعنى الإضافة: أنه مخلوق الله تعالى، ليس من تأليفات المخلوقين. فلا يصح النفى أصلا، ولا يكون الإعجاز والتحدى إلا في كلام الله تعالى .
If it were said: If the speech of Allah (kalam Allah) is true in the eternal meaning, and metaphorical in the composed wording, then it would be valid to negate it from it by saying: The revealed, miraculous, detailed composition into chapters and verses is not the speech of Allah. And the consensus is contrary to that. Also: The miraculous challenge is with the speech of Allah truly, while it is certain that this is only conceivable in the composed wording detailed into chapters, since there is no meaning to opposing the eternal attribute. We say: The verification is that the speech of Allah is a shared term between the eternal inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi al-qadim), and the meaning of the addition is that it is an attribute of Allah, and between the created verbal speech (al-lafzi al-hadith) composed of chapters and verses, and the meaning of the addition is that it is a creation of Allah, not from the compositions of creatures. So negation is not valid at all, and the miraculous challenge is only in the speech of Allah.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the view that only the eternal attribute is called 'speech of Allah' while the articulated Qur'an is called it metaphorically, followed by the author's response. The first objection: if the term 'speech of Allah' is applied truly only to the eternal meaning and metaphorically to the composed wording, then one could validly deny that the revealed, miraculous, detailed composition into surahs and verses is the speech of Allah, but there is consensus against such a denial. The second objection: the miraculous challenge (tahaddi) is directed at the speech of Allah in a true sense, and it is certain that this challenge is only conceivable in the case of the composed wording detailed into surahs and verses, because challenging the eternal attribute is meaningless. The author responds: the correct position is that the term 'speech of Allah' is a shared term (ism mushtarak) between the eternal inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi al-qadim), which is an attribute of Allah, and the created verbal speech (al-kalam al-lafzi al-hadith) composed of surahs and verses, which is a creation of Allah and not a composition of creatures. Therefore, the denial of calling the articulated Qur'an the speech of Allah is not valid at all, and the miraculous challenge applies only to the speech of Allah, i.e., the revealed wording.

Key Terms
كلام الله
كلام نفسي قديم
كلام لفظي حادث
إعجاز
تحدي
اسم مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an is only metaphorically called 'kalam Allah'.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that 'kalam Allah' is a shared term (ism mushtarak) that applies truly to both the eternal attribute and the revealed text.

Checkpoint

A) He argues that the term 'kalam' is used metaphorically for the Qur'an.
B) He claims that the term 'kalam' is a shared term between the eternal attribute and the created wording.
C) He denies that the Qur'an is composed of surahs and verses.
D) He asserts that the Qur'an is entirely eternal.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani resolves the tension by explaining that 'kalam Allah' is a shared term covering both the eternal attribute and the created Qur'an, thus maintaining the miraculous challenge.

?

?

What did you discover about your own weakness and the Qur'an's divinity? If you never tried, what are you afraid of?

The Muhaqqiqun on Divine Speech: Eternal Meaning vs Created Recitation
Taftazani presents the muhaqqiqun's view on divine speech and the eternal/created distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) distinguish the eternal attribute of divine speech from the created act of recitation and wording.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal attribute; the recited words are created acts of recitation.

Taftazani introduces the nuanced stance of the verifying scholars on divine speech. They clarify that when the earlier masters said 'Allah's speech is an eternal meaning,' the contrast is not with the wording (lafz) but with the substance (al-ayn), meaning that speech is an attribute subsisting through God. This leads to a crucial distinction between the eternal speech itself and the temporal recitation by humans. In this passage, Taftazani explains their reasoning and refutes a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key distinction
Notice that the muhaqqiqun explain 'eternal meaning' contrasts with 'substance' (al-ayn), not 'wording' (lafz). This shift changes how we understand the attribute of speech.
The term 'ayn here refers to something that subsists by itself, unlike attributes.
2
Examine the refutation of the Hanbali view
The Hanbalis claimed the composed wording (the actual letters and sounds) is eternal. The muhaqqiqun argue this is self-evidently impossible because letters must be uttered in sequence, showing temporality.
The example of uttering 'sin' after 'ba' in 'Bismillah' illustrates the necessary order.
3
Grasp the conclusion about recitation
The passage concludes with the formula: 'al-maqru' (the recited) is eternal, al-qira'a (the recitation) is originated.' This captures the distinction between the eternal divine speech and the human act.
This resolves the tension between the eternality of the Qur'an as God's speech and its recitation in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعض المحققين إلى أن المعنى في قول مشايخنا كلام الله تعالى معنى قديم ليس فى مقابلة اللفظ حتى يراد به مدلول اللفظ ومفهومه، بل في مقابلة العين والمراد به ما لا يقوم بذاته كسائر الصفات ومرادهم : أن القرآن اسم للفظ والمعنى شامل لهما وهو قديم ولا كما زعمت الحنابلة من قدم اللفظ المؤلف المرتب الأجزاء، فإنه بديهى الاستحالة، للقطع بأنه لا يمكن التلفظ بالسين من بسم الله ، إلا بعد التلفظ بالباء، بل بمعني أن اللفظ القائم بالنفس، ليس مرتب الأجزاء في نفسه كالقائم بنفس الحافظ، من غير ترتب الأجزاء وتقدم البعض على البعض والترتب إنما يحصل في التلفظ والقراءة، لعدم مساعدة الآلة . وهذا معنى قولهم : المقروء قديم، والقراءة حادثة .
And some verifying scholars went to that the meaning in the statement of our shaykhs 'the speech of Allah is an eternal meaning' is not in contrast to the wording (lafz) such that what is meant is the indication and concept of the wording, but rather in contrast to the substance (al-'ayn), and what is meant by it is that which does not subsist by itself like the other attributes. And their intent is that the Qur'an is a name for the wording and the meaning, inclusive of both, and it is eternal, and not as the Hanbalis claimed of the eternity of the composed wording whose parts are ordered, for its impossibility is self-evident, because it is certain that it is not possible to utter the letter sin from 'Bism Allah' except after uttering the letter ba'. Rather, by the meaning that the wording subsisting in the soul is not ordered in its parts, like that which subsists in the mind of the memorizer, without ordering of parts and precedence of some over others; ordering only occurs in utterance and recitation, due to the lack of compatibility of the instrument. And this is the meaning of their statement: 'The recited (al-maqru') is eternal, and the recitation (al-qira'a) is originated.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the nuanced view of some verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) regarding the attribute of speech. They explain that when our masters (mashayikh) say that 'Allah's speech is an eternal meaning,' the contrast is not between the eternal meaning and the verbal wording (lafz) such that 'meaning' means the denotation of the wording. Rather, the contrast is between the eternal meaning and the 'substance' (al-'ayn) – i.e., the attribute of speech is something that does not subsist by itself, like other divine attributes (which subsist through God). Their intended meaning is that 'the Qur'an' is a name that covers both the wording (lafz) and the meaning (ma'na), inclusive of both, and it is eternal. This is not as the Hanbalis claimed, who asserted the eternity of the composed wording whose parts are ordered – an impossibility that is self-evident. The proof: one cannot utter the letter sin in 'Bismillah' without first uttering the letter ba', which shows temporal order. Rather, the speech that subsists in the soul (al-kalam al-nafsi) is not ordered in its parts; it exists like something in the mind of a memorizer, without ordering or precedence. Ordering only occurs in the act of uttering and reciting, due to the limitations of the vocal apparatus. This is the meaning of their statement: 'The recited (al-maqru') is eternal, and the recitation (al-qira'a) is originated (hadith).'

Correction: The muhaqqiqun clarify that only the meaning (the divine attribute of speech) is eternal; the recited words and recitation are created events in time.

B) The attribute of speech is eternal and not in contrast to wording but to substance.
C) The Qur'an is only the meaning, not the wording.
D) The recitation is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The verifying scholars assert that divine speech is an eternal attribute, distinct from the temporal act of recitation and the composed wording.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The muhaqqiqun insist that divine speech has no order or sequence in itself — yet your own mental experience of understanding a verse unfolds in time. Does that tension unsettle you? How will you train your heart to submit to the reality that God's speech transcends your own cognitive processes?
?
Consider the Hanbalis' claim that the composed wording is eternal. The passage calls it 'an evident impossibility'. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah's attributes that you have not subjected to the same rigorous, self-evident logic? What would it cost you to examine them honestly today and root out any trace of anthropomorphism?

The Non-Composite Nature of Divine Speech
Taftazani discusses the difficulty of conceiving speech that subsists in God without order or composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine inner speech is not composed of ordered parts and why human analogies fall short.

Core Idea
God's speech is pure meaning without letters or sequence.

Taftazani addresses a challenging point in the doctrine of divine speech: how can we conceive of speech that has no order or composition? He explains that while scholars affirm that God's speech subsists in His essence without sequence, our human minds naturally think of speech as composed of sounds and letters. This lesson unpacks Taftazani's argument and the difficulty he acknowledges.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Taftazani presents the scholarly view and then his own reflection.
Pay attention to the contrast between divine and human inner speech.
2
Identify the key claim about divine speech
Find where Taftazani states that there is no order in God's speech and why. How does God's lack of need for an instrument affect this?
3
Examine the human analogy
Note how Taftazani explains human inner speech as stored forms of letters. How does this differ from divine speech?
This reveals the limitation of our conceptualization.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the provided common misunderstanding. How does this passage correct the idea that God's speech consists of letters and sounds?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائم بذات الله تعالى، فلا ترتب فيه حتى من سمع كلامه تعالی، سمعه غير مرتب الأجزاء لعدم احتياجه إلى الآلة. هذا حاصل كلامهم. وهو جيد لمن تعقل لفظا قائما بالنفس غير مؤلف من الحروف المنطوقة أو المخيلة، المشروط وجود بعضها بعدم البعض، ولا من الأشكال المرتبة الدالة عليه . ونحن لا نتعقل من قيام الكلام بنفس الحافظ إلا كــون صـور الحروف مخزونة مرتسمة في خياله، بحيث إذا التفت إليها كان كلاما مؤلفا من ألفاظ مخيلة أو نقوش مرتبة، وإذا تلفظ كان كلاما مسموعا .
As for that which subsists in the essence of Allah, there is no order in it, such that whoever hears His speech hears it without ordering of parts, due to His lack of need for an instrument. This is the gist of their speech. And it is good for one who can understand a word/term (lafz) subsisting in the soul, not composed of spoken or imagined letters, the existence of some of which is conditioned upon the non-existence of others, nor of ordered shapes indicating it. But we do not understand the subsistence of speech in the mind of the memorizer except as the forms of letters being stored and imprinted in his imagination, such that when he turns to them, they become speech composed of imagined words or ordered inscriptions, and when he speaks, it becomes audible speech.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage addresses the speech that subsists in Allah's essence (al-qa'im bi dhat Allah). Taftazani explains that there is no order or sequence in it; whoever hears God's speech would hear it without ordering of parts, because God does not need a vocal instrument that would impose sequential articulation. This is the conclusion of the scholars' discussion. This line of thought is acceptable for someone who can conceive of a 'word' (lafz) that subsists in the soul, which is not composed of spoken or imagined letters (whose existence is conditioned on the absence of some) and not composed of ordered shapes indicating it. However, Taftazani then notes a difficulty: common understanding cannot conceive of speech subsisting in the mind (like that of a memorizer) except as stored forms of letters imprinted in the imagination. When the memorizer turns to these forms, they become speech composed of imagined words or written symbols, and when he articulates them, they become audible speech. This implies that human inner speech is still imagistic and composed in a sense, whereas the divine inner speech is not. This highlights the challenge of understanding the nature of God's attribute of speech as pure meaning without any composition, and the author leaves it as a point for those who can grasp it.

Correction: This passage clarifies that divine speech has no order or composition, unlike human speech which is understood through imagined letters and sounds.

Key Takeaway
Divine inner speech is free from order and composition, unlike human inner speech which relies on imagined letters and forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Your own thoughts are often composed of inner words—imagined sentences. This lesson exposes how limited your understanding of 'speech' is. Are you willing to accept that God's attribute of speech is fundamentally beyond your mental categories, even if it feels uncomfortable? Commit to leaving your imagination behind when contemplating the divine attributes.

The Quran as Eternal Speech in the Soul
The author distinguishes between eternal speech in the soul and created recitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the eternal meaning of the Quran (speech in God's essence) and the temporal recitation, and why the articulated utterance cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
The Quran's eternal reality is meaning in God's mind, not the letters we recite.

The author tackles a crucial theological nuance: how can the Quran be eternal if it consists of words spoken in time? This passage clarifies that the true, eternal Quran is the meaning subsisting in God's essence, while our recitation of it is a created act. This refutes both literalist and rationalist extremes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the author's use of technical terms like 'كلام نفسي'.
2
Identify key distinctions
Look for three distinctions: attribute subsisting in subject vs. accident; eternal vs. temporal; speech in soul vs. articulated utterance.
The author uses the example of the letters bā' and sīn to illustrate sequence.
3
Understand the refutation of Hanbalites
The author argues that articulated utterance cannot be eternal because pronunciation requires sequential order, which implies temporality.
This is a rational proof based on the nature of speech production.
4
Reflect on the human limitation
The author admits we conceive of speech only as stored letters, but God's speech is not limited by our conception.
This shows the tension between human understanding and divine reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And its concept, rather in opposition to the 'ayn (essence), and what is meant by it is that which does not subsist by itself like other attributes. And their intent: that the Qur'an is a name for the utterance and the meaning, inclusive of both, and it is eternal, not as the Hanbalites claimed of the eternity of the articulated utterance composed of ordered parts, for it is intuitively impossible, because it is certain that one cannot pronounce the letter sīn of Bismillah except after pronouncing the bā'. Rather, in the sense that the utterance subsisting in the soul is not composed of ordered parts in itself, like that subsisting in the memory of the memorizer, without ordering of parts and precedence of some over others; rather ordering only occurs in pronunciation and recitation because the instrument does not assist. And this is the meaning of their saying: the recited is eternal, and the recitation is temporal. As for that which subsists by the essence of Allah the Exalted, there is no ordering in it, until [؟] whoever hears His speech the Exalted hears it without ordering of parts because it has no need of an instrument. This is the gist of their words. And it is good for one who conceives an utterance subsisting in the soul not composed of spoken or imagined letters, the existence of some of which is conditioned by the non-existence of others, nor of ordered forms indicating it. But we do not conceive of the subsistence of speech in the soul of the memorizer except [as] the forms of letters being stored and depicted in his imagination, such that when he attends to them, it becomes speech composed of imagined utterances or ordered inscriptions, and when he pronounces, it becomes audible speech.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a theological clarification about the nature of the Quran. The author begins by defining an attribute that subsists in the subject (قيام بذاته) as opposed to an accident. He explains that the intended meaning of 'Quran' encompasses both the utterance (لفظ) and the meaning (معنى), and it is eternal. He refutes the Hanbali view that the articulated utterance composed of ordered parts is eternal, calling it intuitively impossible because pronunciation necessarily involves sequence (e.g., one cannot say 'sin' before 'ba'). Instead, he argues that the eternal speech is speech in the soul (كلام نفسي), which is not composed of ordered parts. The ordering only occurs in recitation due to the limitations of the human instrument. This is the meaning of the phrase 'the recited is eternal, the recitation is temporal.' God's speech, subsisting in His essence, has no ordering; whoever hears it hears it without order. The author then notes that this is good for one who can conceive of speech in the soul that is not composed of letters. However, he admits that we humans do not conceive of speech in the soul except as stored forms of letters in the imagination, which become composed when attended to or pronounced. This highlights the difficulty in fully grasping this concept.

Takwīn as an Eternal Attribute of Allah
The author defines Takwīn and enumerates four proofs for its eternality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Takwīn and the four rational proofs for its eternality as an attribute of Allah.

Core Idea
Takwīn is an eternal attribute, not a temporal act, because temporal events cannot subsist in Allah's essence and infinite regress is impossible.

Takwīn (التكوين) is the attribute by which Allah brings things into existence. The author defines it and then presents four proofs to establish its eternality, refuting the view that creation is a temporal act. These proofs rely on reason and the impossibility of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Define Takwīn
Read the definition of Takwīn carefully. It is the attribute that brings the non-existent into existence, expressed by various terms like 'act', 'creation', etc.
Note that this attribute is distinct from the act itself.
2
First proof: Temporal events cannot subsist in Allah
The first proof argues that temporal events (like creation) cannot subsist in Allah's essence, as established earlier. Therefore, Takwīn cannot be temporal because that would make it an accident in Allah.
This builds on the principle that Allah is not a locus for temporal events.
3
Second proof: Allah's eternal speech describes Him as Creator
Allah described Himself as 'the Creator' in His eternal speech. If He were not Creator in eternity, His speech would be false or require metaphor. Also, if 'Creator' meant merely 'able to create', any ability could be ascribed, which is absurd.
This proof relies on the uncreatedness of the Quran.
4
Third proof: Avoiding infinite regress
If Takwīn were temporal, it would need another takwīn to bring it into being, leading to infinite regress (impossible), or it would come without a cause, nullifying Allah's role as Maker.
Infinite regress is impossible because it would make the world's creation impossible.
5
Fourth proof: Takwīn cannot occur in Allah's essence or in another
If Takwīn were temporal, it would occur either in Allah's essence (making it a locus of temporal events) or in something else (as Abu al-Hudhayl claimed). Both are problematic, as the first is impossible and the second contradicts divine oneness.
The passage cuts off here, but the implication is that Takwīn must be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والتكوين) وهو المعنى الذي يعبر عنه بالفعل والخلق والتخليق والإيجاد والإحداث والاختراع ونحو ذلك . ويفسر بإخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود . (صفة الله تعالى لإطباق العقل والنقل على أنه خالق للعالم مكون له، وامتناع إطلاق اسم المشتق على الشيء من غير أن يكون مأخذ الاشتقاق وصفا له قائما به (أزلية) لوجوه : الأول : أنه يمتنع قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى - لما مر - . - - . الثاني: أنه وصف ذاته في كلامه الأزلي بأنه الخالق . فلو لم يكن فى الأزل خالقا لزم الكذب أو العدول إلى المجاز. أو الخالق فيما يستقبل أو القادر على الخلق من غير تعذر الحقيقة. على أنه لو جاز إطلاق الخالق عليه بمعنى القادر على الخلق لجاز إطلاق كل ما يقدر هو عليه من الأعراض. الثالث : أنه لو كان حادثا . فإما بتكوين ،آخر فيلزم التسلسل وهو محال . ويلزم منه استحالة تكون العام مه أنه مشاهد . وإما بدونه فيستغنى الحادث عن المحدث والأحداث وفيه تعطيل الصانع. الرابع : أنه لو حدث، لحدث إما فى ذاته فيصير محلا للحوادث أو في غيره. كما ذهب إليه أبو الهذيل ) (۱) من أن تكوين كل جسم
(And at-Takwīn) which is the meaning expressed by 'act' (الفعل), 'creation' (الخلق), 'origination' (التخليق), 'bringing into existence' (الإيجاد), 'bringing about' (الإحداث), 'invention' (الاختراع), and the like. And it is interpreted as bringing the non-existent out of non-existence into existence. (It is) an attribute of Allah the Exalted because of the agreement of reason and revelation that He is the Creator of the world, its Maker, and the impossibility of applying a derived name to a thing without the source of derivation being an attribute subsisting in it. (It is eternal) because of several reasons: The first: that it is impossible for temporal events to subsist in His essence the Exalted – as has passed. The second: that He described His essence in His eternal speech as 'the Creator'. So if He were not a Creator in eternity, falsity [would be] necessary, or turning to metaphor, or [He would be] Creator in the future, or powerful over creation without difficulty of actuality. And if it were permissible to apply 'Creator' to Him in the sense of 'powerful over creation', it would be permissible to apply every thing He is capable of, [even] from among the accidents. The third: that if it were temporal, then either by another takwīn, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible, and from it would entail the impossibility of the world's coming into being, even though it is witnessed; or without it, then the temporal event would be independent of the temporalizer and the events, and in that is the nullification of the Maker. The fourth: that if it were to occur, it would occur either in His essence, making it a locus of temporal events, or in other than Him, as Abu al-Hudhayl went to – that the takwīn of every body [is temporal].
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines Takwīn (التكوين) as the attribute by which Allah brings the non-existent into existence. It then provides four proofs for its eternality (أزلية). First, temporal events cannot subsist in Allah's essence, as established earlier. Second, Allah described Himself as 'the Creator' in His eternal speech (the Quran); if He were not Creator in eternity, His speech would be false or require metaphorical interpretation. Also, if 'Creator' meant merely 'able to create', then any ability could be ascribed similarly, which is absurd. Third, if Takwīn were temporal, it would require another act of creation (تكوين) to bring it into being, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible and would make the creation of the world impossible. Or it would come to be without a cause, making the temporal independent of God, nullifying His role as Maker. Fourth, if it were temporal, it would either occur in Allah's essence (making it a locus of temporal events, which is impossible) or in something else (as the Mu'tazilite Abu al-Hudhayl claimed, that Takwīn occurs in the created body). The author rejects this by implication.

Key Terms
التكوين
الخلق
الإيجاد
أزلية
التسلسل
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Takwīn is a temporal act like human creation, occurring in time.
Correction: The author proves Takwīn must be an eternal attribute of Allah, as temporal events cannot subsist in Allah, infinite regress is impossible, and Allah's eternal speech describes Him as Creator.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof for the eternality of Takwīn entail?
A) Takwīn is independent of God
B) If Takwīn were temporal, it would require either infinite regress or a causeless event, both impossible
C) Takwīn is an accident in God's essence
D) Takwīn is the same as creation in time

Key Takeaway
Takwīn is an eternal attribute of Allah, proven by reason and revelation, and it is not a temporal act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Takwīn is an eternal attribute of Allah, then creation is not a one-time event but a continuous act of bringing into existence. How does this change your awareness of Allah's presence and power in every moment of your life? Will you live today more mindful that every breath is being created by Him right now?
?
The third proof says that if Takwīn were temporal, the world could not have come into being. Yet you see the world exists. Does your heart truly submit to the fact that your own existence depends entirely on an eternal Creator, not on any independent power? What steps will you take to make this belief a living reality in your daily reliance upon Allah?
?
Allah describes Himself as 'the Creator' in His eternal speech. If you truly believe His attribute of creation is eternal, then every act of creation—including your own soul's formation—was known and intended by Him before time. How should this certainty affect your trust in His plan for you, especially in moments of hardship or confusion?

The Nature of Takwin: Real Attribute or Relational Consideration?
Taftazani presents two scholarly views on takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main scholarly positions on the nature of takwin (bringing into existence) and how they relate to the broader discussion of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Takwin is either a real attribute or a relational concept — a key debate in kalam.

This passage from Taftazani's commentary introduces a foundational terminological debate in kalām: is God's act of bringing things into existence (takwin) a real, eternal attribute like knowledge and power, or is it merely a relational consideration? The answer determines how we think about God's creative action.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's words set the stage.
Pay attention to the contrast introduced by the word 'و' (and) linking the two views.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that the first sentence states a basis for proofs: takwin is a real attribute. The second sentence presents the view of the verifiers: it is relational and mental.
The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) are respected scholars in kalām.
3
Understand the relational examples
The relational view is illustrated by examples: God being before everything, with everything, after everything, etc. These are not separate attributes but ways of describing God's relationship to creation.
Such relational descriptions are 'i'tibari' (mental considerations).
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for proofs of creation. The passage says the 'basis of proofs' is the real-attribute view, implying that the relational view might lead to different arguments.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومبنى هذه الأدلة على أن التكوين صفة حقيقية، كالعلم والقدرة. والمحققون من المتكلمين على أنه من الإضافات والاعتبارات العقلية، مثل كون الصانع - تعالى وتقدس - قبل كل شيء ومعه وبعده. ومذكورا بالسنتنا، ومعبودا لنا ويميتنا ويحيينا. ونحو ذلك.
And the basis of these proofs is that takwin (bringing into existence) is a real attribute, like knowledge and power. And the verifiers among the mutakallimun (theologians) hold that it is one of the relational and mental considerations, such as the Maker - exalted and sanctified - being before everything, with everything, after everything, mentioned by our tongues, worshipped by us, causing us to die and live, and so forth.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage classifies two opposing views on the nature of takwin (God's act of bringing into existence). The first view, which is the basis of the earlier proofs (presumably proofs for the world's creation), treats takwin as a real, existent attribute like knowledge (ilm) and power (qudra). The second view, held by the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) among the mutakallimun, considers takwin to be a relational (idafi) and mental (i'tibari) concept — it is not an additional entity but a way of describing God's relation to creation. Examples include saying God is 'before everything, with everything, after everything' — these are relational descriptions. The list: being mentioned by our tongues, worshipped, causing death and life, etc. The passage thus sets up the terminological debate that underlies subsequent arguments.

Key Terms
التكوين (takwin)
صفة حقيقية (real attribute)
الإضافات والاعتبارات العقلية (relational and mental considerations)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Takwin is a separate entity or a temporal action.
Correction: The verifiers consider takwin to be a relational and mental consideration, not a separate entity. The real-attribute view also sees it as eternal, not temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers among the mutakallimun, what kind of thing is takwin?
A) A real attribute like knowledge and power
B) A relational and mental consideration
C) A physical action
D) A created entity

Key Takeaway
The kalām debate over takwin shows that even God's creative act can be understood as a real attribute or a relational description, with implications for theological arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture God creating the universe, do you imagine a distinct 'creative attribute' at work? How does the idea that creation might be a mere relation rather than an extra attribute affect your sense of God's closeness to His creation?

The passage says takwin is like God being 'mentioned by our tongues' or 'worshipped by us'. These are relational descriptions. Are you comfortable that some of the most significant acts of God—like creating you—may be relational rather than inherent? What does that say about how you relate to Him?
?
If takwin is merely a mental consideration, does it make God's act of creating less real to you? Or does it press you to rethink how you attribute actions to God? Examine your own heart: do you need God's attributes to be 'real and separate' to feel secure in His power?

Takwin is Not a Separate Attribute
Taftazani argues takwin is not a distinct attribute and answers the objection about its eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why takwin (bringing into existence) is not a distinct attribute but rather a relation of power and will, and how its eternity does not entail the eternity of created things.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute can have temporal relations without being temporal itself.

This passage tackles a subtle point in divine attributes: Is 'bringing into existence' (takwin) a separate eternal attribute, or is it simply the effect of power and will? The objection that an eternal takwin would make creation eternal is answered by distinguishing between the attribute and its relation to created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the claim, then the objection, then the answer.
Pay attention to the key terms: takwin, qudra, irada, ta'alluq, huduth, qidam.
2
Identify the main claim
What does Taftazani assert about takwin? Find the sentence that states it is not a separate attribute.
It is in the first half: 'ولا دليل على كونه صفة أخرى سوى القدرة والإرادة.'
3
Understand the objection
The objectors say if takwin is eternal, then its objects must be eternal. Trace their reasoning from the passage.
They compare it to striking without the struck – impossible.
4
Follow the answer
Taftazani answers by distinguishing between the attribute and its relation. The relation (ta'alluq) is temporal, not the attribute itself.
The analogy with knowledge and power helps clarify.
5
Reflect on the implication
Why does this matter for Islamic theology? How does it preserve both God's eternal attributes and the temporality of creation?
This protects against both anthropomorphism and denying divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل في الأزل: هو مبدأ التخليق والترزيق والإماتة والإحياء، وغير ذلك. ولا دليل على كونه - أي التكوين - صفة أخرى سوى القدرة والإرادة. فإن القدرة وإن كانت نسبتها إلى وجود المكون وعدمه على السواء، لكن مع انضمام الإرادة يتخصص أحد الجانبين. ولما استدل القائلون بحدوث التكوين بأنه لا يتصور بدون المكون كالضرب بدون المضروب، فلو كان قديما لزم قدم المكونات، وهو محال. أشار إلى الجواب بقوله: (وهو) أي التكوين. تكوينه تعالى للعالم ولكل جزء من أجزائه لا في الأزل، بل لوقت وجوده على حسب علمه وإرادته، فالتكوين باق أزلا وأبدا، والمكون حادث بحدوث التعلق، كما في العلم والقدرة وغيرهما من الصفات القديمة التي لا يلزم من قدمها قدم متعلقاتها، لكون تعلقاتها حادثة.
And the conclusion regarding pre-eternity (al-azal): He is the principle of creating, providing, causing death, and giving life, and other than that. And there is no evidence that it (i.e., takwin) is another attribute besides power (qudra) and will (irada). For power, even though its relation to the existence of the made (mukawwan) and its non-existence is equal, yet with the addition of will, one of the two sides becomes specified. And when those who argue for the temporality (huduth) of takwin argued that it cannot be conceived without the made, like striking without the struck, and if it were eternal (qadim), it would necessitate the eternity of the made things (mukawwanat) – which is impossible – he pointed to the answer by saying: 'And it (i.e., takwin) – His – exalted – takwin of the world and of every part of its parts is not in pre-eternity, but at the time of its existence according to His knowledge and will. So takwin remains eternal (baqi azalan wa abadan), and the made is temporal (hadith) by the temporality of the relation (ta'alluq), as in knowledge, power, and other eternal attributes whose eternity does not necessitate the eternity of their objects (muta'allaqat), because their relations are temporal.'
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a complete argument: first, the claim that takwin is not a separate attribute beyond power and will. Power is equally related to existence and non-existence, but will specifies one side. Then the objection: if takwin were eternal, then its objects (the created things) would be eternal, which is false. So takwin must be temporal. The answer: takwin remains eternal, but its relation (ta'alluq) to the made thing is temporal — it brings things into existence at the time they are created. This is analogous to knowledge and power, which are eternal attributes but their objects (what is known or powerful over) are temporal. So the eternity of the attribute does not imply the eternity of its objects.

Key Terms
القدرة (power)
الإرادة (will)
التعلق (relation/attachment)
حدوث (temporality)
قدم (eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute of God is eternal, then whatever it relates to must also be eternal.
Correction: The mutakallimun distinguish between the attribute itself (which is eternal) and its relation (ta'alluq) to the object (which can be temporal). Thus, God's power and takwin are eternal, but the things He creates are temporal.

Checkpoint
What determines which side power is specified for, according to the passage?
A) The attribute of takwin
B) The addition of will
C) The nature of the made
D) The passage does not specify

Key Takeaway
An eternal divine attribute like takwin does not necessitate eternal objects because the attribute's relation to temporal things is itself temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You claim to believe that God's power and will are eternal, yet do you truly live as though His command over your every moment is as real as the moment it happens? Are you patient with His timing, or do you demand instant results?

If you truly understood that God's creative act (takwin) is not bound by time like your actions, would you still feel anxious about the future? What does that say about your trust in His eternal will?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the eternal attribute and its temporal relation. In your own life, where do you confuse the permanence of God's attribute with the temporality of your own circumstances? How can you realign your perspective?

Refutation of the Analogy Between Takwin and Striking
Taftazani refutes the analogy between takwin and striking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between takwin (God's attribute of bringing into existence) and striking is invalid in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Takwin is not a relational attribute; striking is.

This short but dense passage delivers the crucial refutation of a common objection against the eternality of the divine attribute takwin. The objector had claimed that just as striking requires a struck object, takwin requires a made thing to exist alongside it. Taftazani cuts the analogy by showing that striking is a relational attribute, whereas takwin is not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note that it begins with 'والحاصل' indicating a conclusion.
2
Identify the analogy being rejected
The objector compared takwin to striking (ضرب). Understand why this comparison was troubling: if takwin is like striking, it would need its object (the made thing) to exist at the same time.
3
Understand the refutation
Taftazani explains that striking is a relational attribute (صفة إضافية) that requires two relata (striker and struck). Takwin, however, is not relational; it is an eternal attribute of God that does not depend on the created thing's existence.
Relational attributes are those that only make sense in relation to something else, like 'father' needs a child.
4
Reflect on the implication
If takwin were relational, it would require the made thing to exist eternally, which is false. Thus, takwin exists eternally but its effect appears in time.
This preserves the Islamic belief in God's eternal attributes without temporal co-existence with creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنا لا نسلم أنه لا يتصور التكوين بدون وجود المكون، وأن وزائه معه كوزان الضرب مع المضروب. فإن الضرب صفة إضافية لا يتصور بدون المضافين أعني الضارب والمضروب.
And the conclusion is that we do not concede that takwin cannot be conceived without the existence of the made, and that its balancing with it (the made) is like the balancing of striking with the struck. For striking is a relational (idafiyya) attribute that cannot be conceived without the two relata, namely the striker and the struck.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the author's final refutation of the objection that takwin requires the existence of the made thing, just as striking requires a struck object. The author rejects the analogy: striking is a relational attribute (sifa idafiyya) that inherently depends on two relata (the striker and the struck). Takwin, however, is not a relational attribute; it is an eternal attribute of God that brings things into existence at a certain time. Therefore, takwin exists timelessly, while the made thing comes into existence later. Thus the analogy is invalid.

Key Terms
صفة إضافية (relational attribute)
ضرب (striking)
مضروب (struck)
مكون (made thing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that takwin, like striking, must have its object exist simultaneously, so if takwin is eternal then the world must be eternal.
Correction: Striking is a relational attribute dependent on two parties, but takwin is not relational; it is an eternal attribute that produces its effect in time without requiring the effect to be co-eternal.

Checkpoint
Why is the analogy between takwin and striking invalid according to the passage?
A) Because striking is a real attribute while takwin is relational
B) Because striking is a relational attribute while takwin is not
C) Because takwin is a created action
D) Because striking requires a striker but takwin does not require a maker

Key Takeaway
Takwin is not a relational attribute, so it does not require the simultaneous existence of the made thing, preserving God's eternal attribute without necessitating an eternal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage insists that God's attribute of takwin does not depend on the existence of creation. Have you ever, even unconsciously, assumed that God needed a world to be 'Creator'? How does this truth reshape your gratitude for His creative power?
?
When you think of God's attributes like knowledge or power, do you picture them as relational—needing a known object or a powered thing? How does the distinction between relational and non-relational attributes challenge you to purify your concept of God?
?
What would be the practical impact on your worship if you truly believed that God is eternally the Maker, even before any created thing? How does this knowledge change your sense of dependence on Him—not just for what He gives, but for who He is in Himself?

Temporal and Eternal: Definitions of Philosophers and Mutakallimun
The author distinguishes definitions of temporal and eternal between philosophers and mutakallimun regarding takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the philosophers' and mutakallimun's definitions of temporal (hadith) and eternal (qadim), and how this distinction resolves the apparent paradox of a pre-eternal attribute (takwin) attached to a temporal effect.

Core Idea
Temporality means having a beginning, not merely depending on another.

In this passage, the author addresses a key doctrinal dispute: does the fact that a thing's existence depends on Allah's attribute of takwin make that thing temporal? The philosophers say yes, based on their definition; the mutakallimun disagree. This distinction is crucial for preserving the pre-eternity of Allah's attributes while affirming the creation of the world in time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the author begins in the middle of a previous argument about attributes.
2
Identify two definitions of temporal and eternal
The passage presents two definitions: the philosophers define temporal as that whose existence depends on another; the mutakallimun define it as that which has a beginning preceded by non-existence. Locate both.
These definitions lead to different conclusions about takwin.
3
Understand the dilemma about attachment of takwin
The author presents a dilemma: if takwin attaches to the mukawwan, either it implies pre-eternity of the world (false) or not, in which case takwin can be pre-eternal while the mukawwan is temporal. Follow this logic.
The objection from philosophers is then addressed.
4
Note the resolution via creation by choice
The author explains that if we prove the world is created by choice (not necessary emanation), then attachment to takwin does imply temporality. But without that proof, dependence alone does not necessitate temporality.
This ties the doctrine of takwin to the broader proof of God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو صفة من صفاته لزم تعطيل الصانع واستغناء تحقق الحوادث عن الموجد وهو محال . وإن تعلق فإما أن يستلزم ذلك قدم ما يتعلق وجوده به فيلزم قدم العالم. وهو باطل، أو لا، فليكن التكوين أيضا قديما، مع حدوث المكون المتعلق به . وما يقال من أن القول بتعلق وجود المكون بالتكوين قول بحدوثه إذ القديم ما لا يتعلق وجوده بالغير، والحادث ما يتعلق وجوده به ففيه نظر لأن هذا معنى القديم والحادث بالذات، على ما يقول به الفلاسفة. وأما عند المتكلمين، فالحادث ما يكون لوجوده بداية، أي يكون مسبوقا بالعدم والقديم بخلافه و مجرد تعلق وجوده بالغير لا يستلزم الحدوث بهذا المعنى الجواز أن يكون محتاجا إلى الغير صادرا عنه دائما بدوامه. كما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة فيما ادعوا قدمه من الممكنات كالهيولى مثلا . نعم إذا أثبتنا صدور العالم عن الصانع بالاختيار دون الإيجاب بدليل لايتوقف على حدوث العالم، كان القول بتعلق وجوده بتكوين الله تعالى قولا بحدوثه ومن هنا يقال التنصيص على كل جزء من أجزاء العالم، إشارة إلى الرد على من زعم قدم بعض الأجزاء كالهيولى . وإلا فهم إنما يقولون بقدمها بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالعدم، لا معنى عدم تكونه بالغير.
Or an attribute of His attributes—necessitating the incapacitation of the Maker and the independence of the occurrence of events from the Originator, which is impossible. And if it attaches, then either that necessitates the pre-eternity of that whose existence is attached to it, thus necessitating the pre-eternity of the world, which is false; or not, then let takwin also be pre-eternal, with the temporality of the mukawwan attached to it. And what is said that the statement that the attachment of the existence of the mukawwan to takwin is a statement of its temporality, since the pre-eternal is that whose existence does not attach to another, and the temporal is that whose existence attaches to another—there is consideration in this, because this is the meaning of pre-eternal and temporal in essence, according to what the philosophers say. As for according to the mutakallimun, the temporal is that which has a beginning for its existence, i.e., it is preceded by non-existence, and the pre-eternal is contrary to that. And mere attachment of its existence to another does not necessitate temporality in this sense, [allowing] the possibility that it be in need of another, emanating from it perpetually with its perpetuity. As the philosophers held regarding what they claimed pre-eternal of possible things, like hyle for example. Yes, if we prove the emanation of the world from the Maker by choice and not by necessity, by a proof that does not depend on the temporality of the world, then the statement that its existence is attached to the takwin of God Almighty is a statement of its temporality. And from here it is said that specifying every part of the parts of the world is an indication of refutation against those who claimed the pre-eternity of some parts like hyle. Otherwise, they only say about their pre-eternity in the sense of not being preceded by non-existence, not in the sense of not being brought into existence by another.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the implications of the attachment of the divine attribute takwin (bringing into existence) to the mukawwan (thing brought into existence). The author begins mid-argument, stating that denying an attribute leads to incapacitating the Maker and making events independent—both impossible. He then presents a dilemma: if takwin attaches to the mukawwan, either it implies the pre-eternity of the mukawwan (which is false, as the world is temporal), or it does not, in which case takwin can be pre-eternal while the mukawwan is temporal. He addresses an objection: the philosophers define the temporal as that whose existence depends on another, implying that if the mukawwan's existence depends on takwin, it must be temporal. The author counters that this is the philosophers' definition (temporal in essence), but the mutakallimun define the temporal as that which has a beginning preceded by non-existence. Mere dependence does not entail having a beginning; as the philosophers themselves claim that possible entities like hyle can be pre-eternal yet dependent. However, if one proves that the world emanates from God by choice (not necessity), then attachment to takwin does imply temporality. The passage concludes by noting that the Qur'anic specification of every part of the world refutes those claiming pre-eternity of some parts, clarifying that by 'pre-eternal' they only mean not preceded by non-existence, not independence from an originator.

Key Terms
تكوين (takwin)
مكون (mukawwan)
قديم (qadim)
حادث (hadith)
فلاسفة (philosophers)
متكلمون (mutakallimun)
اختيار (choice)
إيجاب (necessary emanation)
هيولى (hyle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any ontological dependence implies temporality.
Correction: The mutakallimun define temporality specifically as having a beginning preceded by non-existence; dependence alone does not make something temporal. A thing can be dependent on another yet pre-eternal, as philosophers claim for hyle.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun as stated in this passage, what is the defining characteristic of a temporal thing (hadith)?
A) It has a beginning to its existence and is preceded by non-existence.
B) Its existence depends on another.
C) It is subject to change.
D) It is created from nothing.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun define temporal as having a beginning preceded by non-existence, so dependence on Allah's takwin does not make the world temporal unless creation is by choice, not necessary emanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your existence is dependent on Allah every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change in your daily life if you internalized that you are constantly being brought into existence by Him?
?
The author distinguishes between necessary emanation (philosophers' view) and creation by choice (mutakallimun). Do you relate to God as One who chooses to create and sustain you, or do you sometimes feel like a mere emanation, distant and automatic? How does knowing that He chose to bring you into existence change your relationship with Him?
?
If you accept that your existence depends on Allah but you were created with a beginning, how should that affect your gratitude and worship? Are you living as if you have always existed in His mind, or are you grateful for each moment He brings you into being?

Takwin: A Real Attribute, Not an Additive Relation
The author defines takwin as a real attribute and refutes the analogy with accidental additive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why takwin (bringing into existence) is a real attribute of Allah distinct from additive attributes like striking, and how it can be pre-eternal while its effect is temporal.

Core Idea
Takwin is a real attribute, not a relation dependent on its object.

The author now addresses a common objection: how can takwin be eternal if its effect (created things) are temporal? Some argue it's like striking, which requires the object to exist simultaneously. But the author draws a crucial distinction between additive and real attributes, showing that takwin belongs to the latter category, allowing it to be eternal while its effect appears later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: sifah haqiqiyyah and sifah idafiyyah.
2
Understand the opponent's analogy
The opponent compares takwin to darb (striking). Identify why darb requires simultaneous existence of the object.
Darb is an accident that cannot persist.
3
Grasp the author's distinction
The author says takwin is a real attribute, the source of the addition, not the addition itself. This allows it to be eternal.
Note the wording: 'مبدأ الإضافة' not 'عينها'.
4
Consider the concession
Even if takwin were identical to the addition (as some shaykhs said), the author argues it would still be distinct because God's act is pre-eternal and enduring.
This counters the objection by showing the analogy fails.
5
Apply the insight
Reflect on why this matters for the doctrine of divine attributes: takwin is not a contingent relation but a real, eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنا لا نسلم أنه لا يتصور التكوين بدون وجود المكون وأن وزائه معه كوزان الضرب مع المضروب . فإن الضرب صفة إضافية لا يتصور بدون المضافين أغنى الضارب والمضروب . والتكوين صفة حقيقية هي مبدأ الإضافة التي هي إخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود، لا عينها. حتى لو كانت عينها على ما وقع في عبارة المشايخ، لكان القول بتحققها بدون المكون مكابرة وإنكارا للضرورى فلا يندفع بما يقال من أن الضرب عرض مستحيل البقاء، فلا بد لتعلقه بالمفعول ووصول الألم إليه من وجود المفعول. معه إذ لو تأخر لانعدم، وهو بخلاف فعل الباري، فإنه أزلى واجب الدوام، يبقى إلى وقت وجود المفعول.
The conclusion is that we do not concede that takwin cannot be conceived without the existence of the mukawwan and that its situation with it is like the situation of darb (striking) with the madrub (thing struck). For darb is an additive attribute (sifah idafiyyah) that cannot be conceived without the two relata, namely the striker and the struck. And takwin is a real attribute (sifah haqiqiyyah) that is the source of the addition which is bringing the non-existent from non-existence into existence, not the addition itself. Even if it were the addition itself, as occurred in the expression of the shaykhs, then the statement that it is realized without the mukawwan would be obstinacy and denial of the necessary, so it cannot be repelled by what is said that darb is an accident that cannot persist, so its attachment to the object and the arrival of pain to it requires the existence of the object with it, because if it were delayed, it would cease to exist. And this is contrary to the act of the Creator, for it is pre-eternal, necessary of duration, remaining until the time of the existence of the object.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the nature of takwin. The author begins by rejecting the argument that takwin cannot be conceived without the mukawwan existing simultaneously, comparing it to darb (striking) which requires the struck object. He explains that darb is an additive attribute (sifah idafiyyah) depending on two relata; but takwin is a real attribute (sifah haqiqiyyah) that is the source of the addition (bringing into existence), not the addition itself. Even if some shaykhs considered it identical to the addition, claiming it exists without the object would be absurd. The analogy with darb fails because darb is a transient accident that requires simultaneous existence of its object to persist, whereas God's act (takwin) is pre-eternal and enduring, remaining until the object exists. Thus, takwin can be eternal while its effect is temporal, unlike accidental relations that demand contemporaneity.

Key Terms
صفة حقيقية (sifah haqiqiyyah)
صفة إضافية (sifah idafiyyah)
ضرب (darb)
مضروب (madrub)
تكوين (takwin)
مكون (mukawwan)
أزلى (pre-eternal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Takwin is an additive attribute that requires its object to exist simultaneously.
Correction: Takwin is a real attribute, the source of the addition, and can be eternal while its effect is temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does takwin differ from accidental relations like darb?
A) Takwin is a real attribute while darb is an additive attribute.
B) Takwin is temporal while darb is eternal.
C) Takwin requires the object's simultaneous existence.
D) Takwin is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Takwin is a real, eternal attribute of Allah that brings about creation without requiring the simultaneous existence of created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at an area of your life where you are impatient for results—perhaps in a relationship, a financial need, or a personal goal. How does knowing that Allah's takwin is an eternal attribute, not dependent on immediate existence, challenge you to trust His timing? What would it look like for you today to surrender that schedule to His eternal decree?
?
The author distinguishes between attributes that are additive (depending on others) and real (self-sufficient). In your worship, do you ever treat Allah's attributes as if they depend on your actions—like thinking your prayers must be perfect for Him to hear? How can you realign your heart to see His attributes as inherently perfect, independent of your performance?
?
Human acts of creation (like making a product) are transient and require the object to exist simultaneously. Allah's takwin is eternal and brings about existence without needing the object present. How does this difference stir your gratitude for being created? Can you honestly say you value the very act of your existence as a gift from an eternal attribute of your Creator?

The Act and Effect are Necessarily Distinct
Sanusi presents the first two arguments for the distinction of act and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why takwin (the act of bringing into existence) must be distinct from the mukawwan (the thing brought into existence), and why identifying them leads to self-creation and the denial of God's creative role.

Core Idea
The act is always other than its effect, or else the creature would create itself.

Sanusi begins by asserting the fundamental principle that an act is necessarily distinct from its effect. He provides intuitive examples and then shows the disastrous consequences of denying this distinction. This lesson unpacks the first part of his argument, establishing why takwin and mukawwan must be separate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi begins with a clear assertion and then gives examples.
2
Identify the core assertion
The passage states that takwin is other than the mukawwan. Find the first reason given: the act is necessarily other than the effect, like striking and the struck.
This is an a priori argument from the nature of act and effect.
3
Examine the first reductio
The second argument is a reductio: if takwin were identical to the mukawwan, the mukawwan would be self-created, pre-eternal, and independent of God. See how this is impossible.
The absurdity shows the distinction is necessary.
4
Consider the second consequence
The passage also argues that if identical, God's relation to the world would be mere priority and ability, not actual creation. That does not make Him a creator.
This contradicts the fact that the world is created by God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو غير المكون (عندنا لأن الفعل يغاير المفعول بالضرورة كالضرب مع المضروب والأكل مع المأكول، ولأنه لو كان نفس المكون لزم أن يكون المكون مكونا مخلوقا بنفسه، ضرورة أنه مكون بالتكوين، الذي هو عينه فيكون قديما مستغنيا عن الصانع، وهو محال . وأن لا يكون للخالق تعلق بالعالم، سوى أنه أقدم منه وقادر عليه من غير صنع وتأثير في ضرورة تكونه بنفسه. وهذا لا يوجب كونه خالقا . والعالم مخلوقات (له فلا يصح القول بأنه ليس خالق العالم وصانعه . هذا خلف. وأن لا يكون الله تعالى مكونا للأشياء، ضرورة أنه لا معنى للمكون إلا من قام به التكوين والتكوين إذا كان غير المكون لا يكون قائما بذات الله تعالى، وأن يصح القول بأن خالق سواد هذا الحجر أسود. وهذا الحجر خالق السواد. إذ لا معنى للخالق والأسود إلا من قام به الخلق ،والسواد وهما واحد، ومحلهما واحد وهذا كله تنبيه على كون الحكم بتغاير الفعل والمفعول ضروريا. لكنه ينبغي للعاقل أن يتأمل في أمثال هذه المباحث، ولا ينسب إلى الراسخين من علماء الأصول ما يكون استحالته بديهية ظاهرة على من له أدنى تمييز بل يطلب لكلامهم محملا صحيحا، يصلح
And it (takwin) is other than the mukawwan according to us, because the act (fi'l) is necessarily other than the effect (maf'ul), like striking and the struck, and eating and the eaten. And because if it were identical to the mukawwan, then it would necessitate that the mukawwan be brought into existence by itself, since it is brought into existence by takwin which is identical to it, so it would be pre-eternal, independent of the Maker, which is impossible. And that the Creator would have no relationship with the world except that He is prior to it and able over it, without making (s'un) or affecting [it], in the necessity of its coming into existence by itself. And this does not necessitate that He be a creator. And the world is His creation, so it is not correct that He is not the creator and maker of the world. This is a contradiction. And that God Almighty would not be a bringer-into-existence of things, because there is no meaning of 'bringer-into-existence' (mukawwin) except the one in whom takwin subsists, and if takwin is other than the mukawwan, it would not subsist in the essence of God Almighty. And that the statement would be valid that the creator of the blackness of this stone is black, and this stone is the creator of blackness, because there is no meaning of 'creator' and 'black' except the one in whom creation and blackness subsist, and they are one, and their locus is one. All of this is a notification that the judgment that the act and effect are distinct is necessary. However, it is befitting for a rational person to reflect on such investigations, and not attribute to the deeply rooted scholars of usul that whose impossibility is obvious to anyone with minimal discernment, but rather to seek a correct interpretation for their words that is sound.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a proof that takwin (the divine act of bringing into existence) is distinct from the mukawwan (the thing brought into existence). The author presents several arguments. First, an a priori argument: the act is necessarily other than the effect, as in all examples like striking and the struck. Second, a reductio: if they were identical, the mukawwan would be self-created (since it is brought into existence by takwin, which would be itself), making it pre-eternal and independent of God, which is impossible. Third, if takwin were identical to the mukawwan, God's relation to the world would be merely priority and ability, not actual creation, which contradicts that the world is His creation. Fourth, then God would not be the 'bringer-into-existence' (mukawwin), because that name requires takwin to subsist in Him; but if takwin is identical to the effect, it would not subsist in God. Fifth, it would lead to absurdities like the creator of blackness being black and the stone being the creator of blackness, since the attribute would be identical to the subject. The author concludes that the distinction is necessary. He then advises that one should not attribute obviously impossible views to the deeply rooted scholars but seek proper interpretation.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l)
مفعول (maf'ul)
مكون (mukawwan)
تكوين (takwin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine attribute takwin is the same as the created thing (mukawwan).
Correction: If that were true, the created thing would be self-creating and independent of God, which is impossible. The act must be distinct from its effect.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument (reductio) show would happen if takwin were identical to the mukawwan?
A) The Creator would be eternal and independent.
B) The mukawwan would be self-created and pre-eternal.
C) The act of creation would become temporal.
D) The world would have no creator.

Key Takeaway
Takwin must be distinct from the mukawwan, because otherwise the creature would be self-creating and God would not be a true creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever lived as if your own existence were self-sufficient, forgetting that God's distinct act of creation is what brings you into being each moment? How can you train yourself to acknowledge your utter dependence on His creative act?
?
If God's creative act were not distinct from the universe, your very existence would be independent of His will. Do you truly believe that God is the sole creator of every effect in your life, or do you sometimes credit your own efforts as if they were self-generated? What would it look like to attribute every outcome to His distinct act?
?
The passage argues that if act and effect were identical, God would not be a true creator. When you pray or seek help, do you act as if God is the real creator of the answer to your prayer, or do you rely on your own plans? How can you deepen your conviction that every good effect comes directly from His creative act?

Further Consequences and the Principle of Scholarly Interpretation
Sanusi presents three more reductio arguments and a methodological conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand additional absurdities that follow from identifying takwin with the mukawwan, and why one must seek valid interpretations for scholars' statements rather than dismissing them.

Core Idea
Identifying act and effect destroys creation, makes God's attributes vacuous, and leads to logical chaos.

Sanusi continues his reductio arguments, showing that if takwin were identical to the mukawwan, the logical consequences would not only be self-creation but also that God would not be the creator of the world, the term 'mukawwin' would become meaningless, and even absurd statements like 'the creator of blackness is black' would follow. He concludes by urging readers to interpret the words of senior scholars charitably.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation
Start with the Arabic: 'والعالم مخلوقات له' and go to the end. Read the translation carefully.
2
Analyze the third argument
The first part states that if takwin is identical to the mukawwan, then the world is not actually created by God; it would just come into existence by itself. This contradicts the fact that the world is His creation.
This is another reductio leading to a contradiction.
3
Examine the fourth argument
It argues that then God would not be a 'mukawwin' (bringer-into-existence) because that term requires takwin to subsist in Him. If takwin is identical to the effect, it cannot subsist in God.
This shows the divine attribute of takwin would be lost.
4
Consider the fifth absurdity
The blackness example: if the act and effect are identical, then the subject of the act would have the same attribute as the effect. So the creator of blackness would be black, and the stone would be its own creator. This is absurd.
This illustrates the confusion from merging act and effect.
5
Reflect on the concluding advice
Sanusi reminds that the distinction is necessary, but one should not hastily attribute impossible views to scholars; instead, seek proper interpretation (ta'wil) for their statements.
This is a methodological principle for understanding kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Have you ever dismissed a theological position or a scholar's statement simply because it seemed contradictory at first glance? Will you commit, from now on, to seeking a charitable ta'wil before passing judgment?
?
The absurd example of the stone being the creator of its own blackness might seem abstract, but it reveals a danger: confusing the Creator with creation. In your own life, is there any created thing—a person, a possession, a desire—that you treat as if it has the power to give you what only God can give? Confess this to yourself now and recommit to keeping Creator and creation distinct in your heart.
