All Actions Are by His Will and Decree: The Distinction Between Qada and Maqdi
The author explains divine will, judgment, and decree, and answers an objection about being pleased with kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine decree (qada) and the thing decreed (maqdi), and why being pleased with the decree does not imply approving of kufr.

Core Idea
We must be pleased with the decree, not with the decreed evil.

In this passage, the author clarifies that all actions of servants occur by Allah's will, judgment, and decree. He then addresses a common objection: if kufr is by Allah's decree, does that require us to be pleased with it? By distinguishing between the decree itself and the thing decreed, the author resolves the apparent conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation once to understand the flow.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the terms: إرادة (will), مشيئة (wish), حكم (judgment), قضية/قضاء (decree), مقضى (decreed thing).
These are central to the argument.
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues: if kufr is by Allah's decree, then we must be pleased with it, but being pleased with kufr is kufr — contradiction.
4
Follow the author's response
The author says kufr is maqdi (decreed), not qada (decree). Being pleased is required for the decree, not the decreed.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهى) أى أفعال العباد. كلها بإرادته ومشيئته (وحكمه) لا يبعد أن يكون ذلك إشارة إلى خطاب التكوين. (وقضيته) أي قضائه. وهو عبارة عن الفعل مع زيادة أحكام. ولا يقال: لو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى، لوجب الرضا به، لأن الرضا بالقضاء واجب، واللازم باطل، لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر. لأنا نقول: الكفر مقضى لا قضاء، والرضا إنما يجب بالقضاء دون المقضى.
And they (i.e., actions of servants) are all by His will and wish. And His judgment: it is not far that that points to the speech of formation (khitab al-takwin). And His decree: i.e., His qada. It is an expression of the action with an increase of rulings. And it is not said: If kufr were by the decree of Allah, then it would be obligatory to be pleased with it, because being pleased with the decree is obligatory, and the consequent is false because being pleased with kufr is kufr. For we say: Kufr is decreed (maqdi), not decree (qada). Being pleased is only obligatory with the decree, not the decreed.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines key terms related to divine will and decree, and then addresses an objection about kufr being by God's decree. The author explains that 'hukm' likely refers to the creative command (khitab al-takwin), and 'qadiyya' means qada, which is the action with additional rulings. The objector argues that if kufr is by God's decree, then one must be pleased with it, but being pleased with kufr is kufr, so it would be contradictory. The answer distinguishes between the decree (qada) itself and the thing decreed (maqdi). Kufr is the thing decreed, not the decree. One must be pleased with the decree (i.e., God's judgment and wisdom), but not with the specific act of kufr. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms

مشيئة
حكم
قضية
قضاء
مقضى
رضا
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must approve of any evil act that Allah has decreed, otherwise one is displeased with the decree.
Correction: Allah decrees both good and evil, but we are obliged to be pleased with His decree as a divine act, not with the evil act itself. Kufr is the thing decreed, not the decree; thus being pleased with the decree does not mean approving of kufr.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the proper object of being pleased?
A) The thing decreed.
B) The decree itself.
C) Both.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The decree of Allah is always good; we are pleased with His decree, but we are not required to approve of the sinful acts that are decreed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship that includes someone else's sin (e.g., oppression or betrayal), do you find yourself resenting Allah's decree because of the evil act? How can you train your heart to accept the decree while rejecting the sin?
?
Are there sins in your own life that you have made peace with, thinking 'Allah decreed it, so it's okay'? What would it mean for you to be pleased with Allah's decree regarding your past mistakes without being pleased with the mistakes themselves?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's decree is always good, what one area of your life (a specific event or condition) are you holding back from accepting? What would change if you surrendered that to Allah tonight?

The Meaning of 'Khalaq' in the Quran: Creation as Determination
Answering the objection that God creates attributes but is not described by them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the word 'khalaq' in certain Quranic verses means 'determination' (taqdir), not bringing into existence in a way that would imply God's possession of created attributes.

Core Idea
God creates attributes in bodies by determination, not by acquiring them.

When we read verses like 'the best of creators' or 'when you created from clay', we might wonder if God is being described with created forms. The objection raised here is precisely that: if God creates blackness and whiteness, why is He not described by them? The answer lies in understanding the linguistic meaning of 'khalaq' in these contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage and identify the objection: that if God creates attributes like blackness and whiteness, the attribute should subsist in Him. Ask yourself why this might be a problem for orthodox theology.
The objector uses a logical premise: the one described by something is the one in whom that thing subsists.
2
Examine the Quranic citations
Notice that the objector quotes two verses: 'So blessed be Allah, the best of creators' (23:14) and 'when you created from clay the likeness of a bird' (5:110). Consider why these verses are used to support the objection.
The verses use the word 'khalaq' in contexts of shaping and forming.
3
Analyze the answer
Read the answer carefully: 'that creation here means determination'. Understand that 'khalaq' in these verses refers to determining or shaping, not bringing into existence from nothing in a way that would make the attribute subsist in the creator.
This semantic distinction solves the theological problem.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about what this teaches us about divine attributes. How does distinguishing 'khalaq' as taqdir protect God from being described by created qualities?
The key is that God creates attributes in others but is not Himself described by them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عظيم لأن المتصف بالشيء من قام به ذلك الشيء لا من أوجده أولا يرون أن الله تعالى هو الخالق للسواد والبياض وسائر الصفات في الأجسام ولا يتصف بذلك؟ وربما يتمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَالِقِينَ ﴾ [المؤمنون: ١٤] - ﴿ وإذ تخلق من الطين كهيئةِ الطَّيْرِ ﴾ [المائدة: ١١٠] من واحد. والجواب: أن الخلق ههنا بمعنى التقدير.
Great, because the one described by something is the one in whom that thing subsists, not the one who first brings it into existence—do they not see that Allah is the Creator of blackness and whiteness and all attributes in bodies, and He is not described by that? And perhaps they cling to His saying: 'So blessed be Allah, the best of creators' [23:14] and 'when you created from clay the likeness of a bird' [5:110] from one? And the answer: that 'creation' here means 'determination' (taqdir).
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector raises a problem: if God creates attributes like blackness and whiteness in bodies, why is He not described by those attributes? The objector then cites two Qur'anic verses: 'Blessed be Allah, the best of creators' and 'when you created from clay the likeness of a bird' to support the idea that God creates forms and thus should be described by them. The author answers by clarifying that the word 'khalaq' (creation) in these contexts means 'taqdir' (determination, measurement, shaping), not bringing into existence from nothing in a way that would entail the attribute's subsistence in the creator. So God is not described by created attributes.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates attributes like blackness and whiteness, He must be described by those attributes.
Correction: The word 'khalaq' in these contexts means 'determination' (taqdir), not causing the attribute to subsist in the Creator. Thus, God determines these qualities in others without possessing them.

Checkpoint

B) As determination (taqdir)
C) As an attribute of God
D) As a synonym for 'bara'a'

Key Takeaway
In the Quran, 'khalaq' can mean determination (taqdir), not necessarily bringing into existence in a way that would attribute created qualities to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can the understanding that 'khalaq' here means 'determination' free you from such anthropomorphic images? Are you willing to retrain your mind to think of God's action as pure decree rather than physical making?
?
This lesson teaches that God creates blackness and whiteness in bodies but is not black or white. Is there any attribute you have been falsely assuming God possesses because He created it? What would it cost you to let go of that assumption and affirm that God is beyond all created attributes? Will you make a firm commitment to only describe God as He describes Himself, not as you perceive His effects?

Distinguishing Qada' from Maqdi: A Key to Predestination
Defining divine terms and resolving a theological objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's decree (qada') and the decreed thing (maqdi), and why being pleased with the decree does not entail being pleased with evil acts like disbelief.

Core Idea
We are pleased with the decree, not the decreed evil.

This passage tackles a common theological objection: if everything, including disbelief, is by God's decree, then being pleased with the decree would require being pleased with disbelief—which is itself disbelief. The author resolves this by carefully distinguishing between the decree itself (qada') and the thing decreed (maqdi). Understanding this distinction is essential for a correct grasp of divine predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the flow: first definitions, then an objection, then the answer.
The passage is short but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the three key terms
Look for 'hukm', 'qadiyya' (qada'), and 'maqdi'. Notice how each is defined: hukm as creative command, qada' as act plus rulings, and maqdi as the decreed thing.
These terms are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection claims that if disbelief is by qada', then we must be pleased with it. The answer distinguishes qada' from maqdi. Work through the logic step by step.
The answer hinges on the fact that only qada' is the object of obligatory pleasure, not maqdi.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction protects Islamic theology from the charge that God's decree forces us to approve of evil. Why is this distinction important?
This resolves a seeming contradiction between divine predestination and moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهى) أى أفعال العباد. كلها بإرادته ومشيئته) قد سبق أنهما عندنا عبارة عن معنى (وحكمه) لا يبعد أن يكون ذلك إشارة إلى خطاب التكوين. (وقضيته) أي قضائه. وهو عبارة عن الفعل مع زيادة أحكام. ولا يقال: لو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى، لوجب الرضا به، لأن الرضا بالقضاء واجب، واللازم باطل، لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر. لأنا نقول: الكفر مقضى لا قضاء، والرضا إنما يجب بالقضاء دون المقضى.
(And they are) i.e., the acts of servants. All of them are by His will and volition. It has preceded that both are, according to us, expressions for a meaning. (And His decree (hukm)) it is not unlikely that this is an allusion to the creative command. (And His ordinance (qadiyya)) i.e., His judgment (qada'). And it is an expression for the act along with additional rulings. And it is not said: If disbelief were by the judgment (qada') of Allah, then being pleased with it would be obligatory, because being pleased with the decree is obligatory, but the necessary consequence is false, because being pleased with disbelief is disbelief. For we say: Disbelief is decreed (maqdi), not the decree (qada'), and being pleased is only obligatory with the decree, not with the decreed thing.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a definition and objection-answer unit. It begins by defining key terms: 'af'al al-ibad' (acts of servants) are all by God's will and volition. Then 'hukm' is explained as a reference to the creative command ('khitab al-takwin'). 'Qadiyya' (ordinance) is God's judgment (qada'), which is the act plus additional rulings. Then an objection is raised: if disbelief is by God's qada', then one must be pleased with it, but being pleased with disbelief is itself disbelief—so a contradiction arises. The author answers by distinguishing between 'qada'' (the decree itself) and 'maqdi' (the decreed thing). Disbelief is 'maqdi', not 'qada''. It is the decree we must be pleased with, not the decreed act of disbelief. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
حكم
قضاء
قضية
مقضى
رضا بالقضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that being pleased with God's decree requires being pleased with every specific event, including evil acts.
Correction: The passage clarifies that pleasure is directed at the decree (qada') as a divine attribute and action, not at the content of the decreed thing (maqdi) when it is sinful.

Checkpoint

A) Because disbelief is not part of God's decree
B) Because disbelief is maqdi, not qada'
C) Because being pleased with disbelief is obligatory
D) Because qada' is only the act itself

Key Takeaway
The distinction between qada' and maqdi resolves the apparent conflict between divine decree and the prohibition of approving sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Name one recent event where you can practice being pleased with the decree without approving of the evil.

Reconciling Divine Will and Human Choice
Proving that all acts are by God's will yet humans have choice, rejecting compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition affirms that all acts are willed by God while maintaining human responsibility through choice.

Core Idea
God's will works through human choice, not against it.

A common challenge to the doctrine of divine predestination is that if God wills everything, humans are merely puppets. This passage from the Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyyah addresses that challenge directly. The author defines divine determination (taqdir) and then tackles the objection head-on, showing how God's will and human choice are compatible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition, purpose, objection, answer.
The passage is concise but dense; read carefully.
2
Understand the definition of taqdir
The passage begins by defining taqdir as the delimitation of each creature's limits. This sets the stage for the discussion of divine will.
This connects to broader discussions of God's attributes.
3
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if God wills and creates all acts, then people are compelled (mujbar) in disbelief and sin, making religious obligation invalid.
4
Analyze the answer
The author responds that God wills those acts by the person's own choice, not by compulsion. This is parallel to God's knowledge: He knows their choice without forcing it.
The answer preserves both divine will and human responsibility.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this answer resolves the tension: God's will is universal, but human choices are real and accountable.
This is a key doctrine in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وتقديره) وهو تحديد كل مخلوق بحده الذي يوجد من حسن وقبح ونفع وضر وما يحويه من زمان ومكان، وما يترتب عليه من ثواب وعقاب. والمقصود: تعميم إرادة الله وقدرته لما مر من أن الكل بخلق تعالى. وهو يستدعى القدرة والإرادة لعدم الإكراه والإجبار. فإن قيل: فيكون الكافر مجبورا في كفره والفاسق في فسقه فلا يصح تكليفهما بالإيمان والطاعة قلنا: إنه تعالى أراد منهما الكفر والفسق باختيارهما، فلا جبر كما أنه تعالى علم منهما الكفر والفسق بالاختيار، ولم يلزم تكليف المحال.
(And His determining (taqdir)) and it is the delimitation of every created thing by its limit in which it exists, of good and evil, benefit and harm, and what encompasses it of time and place, and what results from it of reward and punishment. And the purpose: the generalization of God's will and power as has passed, that all is by His creation, may He be exalted. And that requires power and will due to the absence of coercion and compulsion. For if it is said: Then the disbeliever would be compelled in his disbelief and the sinner in his sin, so their being obliged to faith and obedience would not be valid. We say: He, exalted, willed from them disbelief and sin by their choice, so there is no compulsion, just as He, exalted, knew from them disbelief and sin by choice, and the obligation of the impossible does not follow.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines 'taqdir' (divine determination) as the setting of limits for each creature—good/evil, benefit/harm, time/place, reward/punishment. The author then states the purpose: to affirm that God's will and power encompass all things, which requires that God has power and will, not coercion or compulsion. An objection follows: if God wills all acts, then people are compelled in their disbelief and sin, making religious obligation invalid. The author answers that God willed those acts through the person's own choice, so there is no compulsion. This is parallel to God's knowledge: He knows their disbelief by choice, and it is not impossible to obligate them to what they freely choose.

Key Terms
تقدير
إرادة
قدرة
إكراه
إجبار
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the passage reconcile God's will for all acts with human responsibility?
A) By denying human choice
B) By saying God only wills good acts
C) By stating that God wills acts through human choice
D) By limiting God's will to acts of obedience

Key Takeaway
God's universal will is realized through human choice, preserving both divine sovereignty and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Mu'tazilite Claim and Initial Refutation
Initial refutation of Mu'tazilite denial of God's will for evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that God does not will evil and the author's refutation that evil lies in acquisition, not in divine will.

Core Idea
Evil is in the act of acquiring, not in God's will.

The author sets the stage by describing the Mu'tazilite denial of God's will for evil. They argue that willing evil would be evil itself, like creating evil. The author responds clearly: evil is in the acquisition of evil and being described by it, not in the divine will. This distinction preserves God's transcendence while accounting for human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear its rhythm, then the translation. Focus on the Mu'tazilite claim and the author's 'But we prevent that' response.
The Arabic is concise; the translation clarifies the logical steps.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Contrast the Mu'tazilite statement 'He willed faith, not disbelief' with the author's rebuttal 'The ugly is the acquisition of the ugly.'
Notice the author does not deny that God wills all things, but redefines what 'ugly' means.
3
Understand the consequence for the Mu'tazilites
The author points out that if the Mu'tazilites were correct, most human actions would be contrary to God's will, which is 'very shameful.' Reflect on why this is an unacceptable theological outcome.
This shows the logical absurdity of denying God's will for evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما أنه تعالى علم منهما الكفر والفسق بالاختيار، ولم يلزم تكليف المحال. والمعتزلة أنكروا إرادة الله تعالى للشرور والقبائح، حتى قالوا: إنه تعالى أراد من الكافر والفاسق إيمانه وطاعته لا كفره ومعصيته، زعما منهم أن إرادة القبيح قبيحة كخلقه وإيجاده. ونحن نمنع ذلك بل القبيح كسب القبيح والاتصاف به فعندهم يكون أكثر ما يقع من أفعال العباد على خلاف إرادة الله تعالى. وهذا شنيع جدا. حكى عن عمرو بن عبيد أنه قال: ما ألزمني أحد مثل ما ألزمني مجوسي كان معي في السفينة. فقلت له: لم لا تسلم؟ فقال: لأن الله لم يرد إسلامي، فإذا أراد الله إسلامي أسلمت. فقلت للمجوسي: إن الله تعالى يريد إسلامك، ولكن الشياطين لا يتركونك. فقال المجوسي: فأنا أكون مع الشريك الأغلب. وحكى أن القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني دخل على الصاحب بن عباد وعنده الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني. فلما رأى الأستاذ قال: سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء. فقال الأستاذ على الفور: سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء. والمعتزلة اعتقدوا أن الأمر يستلزم الإرادة والنهى عدم الإرادة فجعلوا إيمان الكافر مرادا وكفره غير مراد. ونحن نعلم أن الشيء قد لا يكون مرادا ويأمر به، وقد يكون مرادا وينهى عنه، للحكمة والمصالح التي يحيط بها علم الله تعالى، أو لأنه لا يسأل عما يفعل [الأنبياء: ٢٣]. ألا ترى أن السيد إذا أراد أن يظهر على الحاضرين عصيان عبده يأمره بالشيء ولا يريده منه. وقد يتمسك من الجانبين بالآيات وباب التأويل مفتوح على الفريقين.
Just as He, exalted, knows from them disbelief and sin by choice, and it does not necessitate imposing the impossible. The Mu'tazilites denied the will of God for evils and ugly acts, to the point that they said: He, exalted, willed from the disbeliever and sinner his faith and obedience, not his disbelief and sin, claiming from them that willing the ugly is ugly like creating it and bringing it into existence. And we prevent that; rather the ugly is the acquisition of the ugly and being described by it. According to them, most of what occurs from the actions of servants would be contrary to God's will. And this is very shameful. It is narrated from 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd that he said: No one has forced me like a Magian who was with me on the ship forced me. I said to him: Why don't you become Muslim? He said: Because God has not willed my Islam. If God wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to the Magian: God wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you. The Magian said: Then I will be with the stronger partner. And it is narrated that al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadani entered upon al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad while al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini was with him. When he saw the master, he said: Glory be to Him who is transcendent above indecency. So the master immediately said: Glory be to Him who only what He wills occurs in His dominion. And the Mu'tazilites believed that the command entails the will and the prohibition entails non-will, so they made the faith of the disbeliever willed and his disbelief non-willed. And we know that a thing may not be willed yet He commands it, and it may be willed yet He prohibits it, for wisdom and interests that God's knowledge encompasses, or because He is not questioned about what He does (Quran 21:23). Do you not see that when the master wants to show the disobedience of his slave to those present, he commands him with something but does not will it from him? And both sides may resort to verses, and the door of interpretation is open for the two groups.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that God does not will evil. The author begins by stating that God knows both belief and disbelief by choice, and that does not entail commanding the impossible. The Mu'tazilites denied God's will for evil, claiming He wills faith from the disbeliever, because they think willing evil is evil. The author rejects this, explaining that evil is the acquisition of evil and being described by it. He then points out that according to the Mu'tazilites, most human actions would be against God's will, which is abhorrent. Two anecdotes illustrate the absurdity: the story of 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and the Magian shows that if God's will is limited, the Magian can claim to follow the stronger partner (Satan). The debate between al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and al-Isfara'ini demonstrates the correct view: nothing occurs in God's dominion except what He wills. The author then explains the Mu'tazilite belief that command implies will and prohibition implies non-will, making the disbeliever's faith willed and his disbelief unwilled. The author refutes this with examples: a thing may be commanded but not willed, or prohibited but willed, for divine wisdom. He cites Quran 21:23—God is not questioned about what He does. He gives the example of a master who commands his slave to do something in order to show the slave's disobedience, not willing that act from him. Finally, he notes that both sides use scriptural texts and the door of interpretation is open.

Key Terms
إرادة الله
القبيح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if God wills evil, then He is evil or that willing evil is itself evil.
Correction: The author explains that evil is in the human acquisition of the act, not in God's will. God's will is not evil even if the willed act is.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite reason for denying that God wills evil?
A) Because God is merciful
B) Because willing evil would be ugly like creating it
C) Because evil does not exist
D) Because humans have free will

Key Takeaway
God's will for evil does not make Him evil; evil is in the human acquisition of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you ever find yourself questioning God's goodness? How does this lesson's distinction between God's will and human acquisition challenge that doubt?
?
Have you ever blamed God for a sin you committed, thinking He willed it so it's not your fault? If you really believe evil lies in your acquisition, how must your repentance change?
?
If God wills everything, including your moments of weakness, does that make you passive? Or does it call you to deeper responsibility? What will you do differently tomorrow when you face a temptation you know God has willed to test you?

Two Anecdotes on the Absurdity of Limiting God's Will
Two anecdotes illustrating the absurdity of denying God's will for evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two historical anecdotes illustrate the theological absurdity of denying that God wills all events, including evil.

Core Idea
If God's will is limited, the unbeliever can claim a stronger partner.

The author strengthens his refutation with two famous anecdotes. The first involves the early Mu'tazilite 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and a Magian, showing that limiting divine will gives the unbeliever a logical loophole. The second recounts a verbal duel between al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, where the correct doctrine is succinctly stated: nothing occurs in God's dominion except what He wills.

Guided Path
1
Read both anecdotes carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Visualize the scenarios: a ship, a debate, and a court. Notice the key dialogue in each.
The stories are short but packed with meaning.
2
Analyze the Magian's argument
The Magian says: if God does not will my Islam, then I follow the stronger partner (the devils). Identify the flaw in the Mu'tazilite logic that this exposes.
The Magian turns the limited-will doctrine against the Mu'tazilite.
3
Understand the second anecdote's core statement
Al-Isfara'ini's reply 'Subhan man la yajri fi mulkihi illa ma yasha' means 'Glory be to Him who only what He wills occurs in His dominion.' This is the Sunni position.
This directly refutes al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar's claim that God is free from indecency.

Checkpoint
What does the Magian's response 'I will be with the stronger partner' imply?
A) That God is weak
B) That if God's will is limited, then the devils can be seen as a competing power
C) That the Magian wants to become Muslim
D) That 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd is correct

Key Takeaway
Limiting God's will leads to the absurd consequence that evil forces can be seen as independent powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe that God wills it for a purpose, or do you secretly blame other forces? How does the Magian's argument expose any hidden shirk in your heart?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life—including suffering, loss, and even your own failures—occurs by God's will? Or is there a part of you that resents God for allowing evil? The second anecdote teaches that God is above indecency. How does that truth free you from blaming Him while still acknowledging His absolute sovereignty?
?
If you were challenged by a non-believer like the Magian, would your response be as quick and theologically sound as Abu Ishaq's? What does your hesitation reveal about your own conviction in God's absolute will?

The Command-Will Distinction and Scriptural Interpretation
Rational argument that command does not entail will, with scriptural openness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine command does not necessarily imply divine will, and that both sides use scriptural interpretation in this debate.

Core Idea
God may command what He does not will, and prohibit what He wills.

The author now tackles the Mu'tazilite assumption that command (amr) entails will (irada) and prohibition (nahy) entails non-will. He gives counterexamples: a master commands his slave to show his disobedience, and God commands things He does not will for wisdom. He also cites Quran 21:23 to emphasize that God is not questioned. Finally, he notes that both sides resort to scripture, leaving room for interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite inference
The Mu'tazilites believe that if God commands something, He must will it, and if He prohibits something, He must not will it. This leads them to say God wills faith from the disbeliever.
Read the sentence: 'wa al-mu'tazilatu i'taqadu anna al-amra yastalzim al-irada...'
2
Grasp the author's counterexample
The author gives the example of a master commanding his slave in order to reveal the slave's disobedience. The master does not will the act from the slave; he wills to expose disobedience.
This shows that command does not always indicate will.
3
Reflect on the scriptural dimension
The author mentions that both sides use verses and that 'the door of interpretation is open.' Recognize that theological debates often involve interpreting the same texts differently.
This is a reminder that the issue is not simply scriptural but also rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's command always reflects what He wills from the commanded person.
Correction: The passage shows that command may be given for reasons other than willing the act, such as exposing disobedience or fulfilling divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
What is the analogy used by the author to show that command does not always imply will?
A) A king commanding his army
B) A master commanding his slave to expose his disobedience
C) A father telling his son to study
D) A teacher instructing a student

Key Takeaway
Command and will are distinct; God may command what He does not will for wise purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read God's commands in the Quran, do you ever feel discouraged because you fail to fulfill them? How does the master analogy—where a command is given not to be fulfilled but to expose a slave's disobedience—change your outlook on your own failures and God's wisdom in commanding you?
?
If God is not questioned about what He does (21:23), that means His commands and prohibitions are rooted in a wisdom you may never fully grasp. How does that truth challenge your tendency to judge God's ways in your life based on your own understanding?
?
The Mu'tazilites assumed they could deduce God's will from His commands. Have you assumed that a difficult command means God wants you to suffer or that an easy path means His pleasure? How can you learn to trust His wisdom even when His ways are opaque?

Determinism Objection and Its Answer
The author refutes the claim that God's universal knowledge and will entail compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that God's universal knowledge and will necessitate determinism and negate human free will.

Core Idea
God's will includes human choice, not cancels it.

This passage presents a classic objection against free will: if God knows and wills everything, then human actions are either necessary or impossible, leaving no room for choice. The author provides a concise answer that reframes divine will as encompassing human choice, thus preserving both sovereignty and responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (the objection) to understand the logical argument: if God's knowledge and will cover everything, then each act is either necessitated or impossible.
The objection is based on two exclusive possibilities.
2
Identify the two options
Notice the two cases the objection posits: either God's will relates to the act's existence (necessitating it) or to its non-existence (making it impossible). Both eliminate choice.
3
Analyze the answer
Read the author's answer. He clarifies that God knows and wills the act as being performed by the servant's own choice. This reconciles divine will with human freedom.
The answer does not deny God's universal will but specifies its modality.

فإن قيل : بعد تعميم علم الله تعالى وإرادته : الجبر لازم قطعا . لأنهما إما أن يتعلقا بوجود الفعل، فيجب أو بعدمه فيمتنع ولا اختیار مع الوجوب والامتناع . قلنا يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركه باختياره فلا إشكال .

We say: He knows and wills that the servant does it or leaves it by his choice, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding divine knowledge and will. The objection argues that if God's knowledge and will encompass all things, then any particular act is either necessitated (if God wills its existence) or impossible (if He wills its non-existence), leaving no room for human choice. The answer explains that God's knowledge and will are not simply about the act's existence or non-existence in a brute sense; rather, He knows and wills that the servant performs the act or refrains from it by his own choice. Thus, the divine will includes the modality of human choice, so there is no conflict.

Correction: God's will can be for an act to occur specifically through human choice, as the author states: 'He knows and wills that the servant does it or leaves it by his choice.'

Necessity by Choice Confirms Freedom
The author refutes the claim that necessity by choice negates human freedom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity arising from one's own choice does not negate human freedom, and how the acts of God serve as an analogy.

Core Idea
Necessity from choice confirms freedom, not contradict it.

Some argue that if God's knowledge and will determine an action, then the act becomes necessary, which destroys free will. In this passage, the author responds directly, asserting that necessity by choice actually affirms freedom. He also supports his argument by pointing to God's own acts, which are known and willed but not compelled.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part: 'If it is said: Then his voluntary act would be either necessary or impossible. And this contradicts choice.' Understand the opponent's claim.
The opponent equates necessity with compulsion.
2
Examine the author's response
Notice the author says 'It is denied' and then gives a positive statement: 'for necessity by choice is a confirmation of choice, not contradictory to it.' Reflect on how necessity can arise from choice yet still be free.
The key is that the necessity originates from the agent's own choice.
3
Analyze the analogy from God's acts
The author points to God's own actions: His knowledge and will relate to His acts, yet that does not make His acts necessary in a way that negates freedom. Consider how this analogy strengthens the argument.
If God's acts are free despite being known and willed, human acts can be free despite being necessary due to choice.
4
Distinguish types of necessity
Differentiate between external compulsion (which negates freedom) and necessity that arises from one's own choice (which confirms freedom). This distinction is crucial.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Summarize: The objection fails because it confuses two kinds of necessity. The author's response and analogy show that necessity by choice does not contradict freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيكون فعله الاختيارى واجبا أو ممتنعا . وهذا ينافي الاختيار. قلنا ممنوع فإن الوجوب بالاختيار محقق للاختيار لا مناف له. وأيضا منقوض بأفعال الباري جل ذكره - لأن علمه وإرادته متعلقان بأفعاله، فيلزم أن يكون فعله واجبا عليه .
If it is said: Then his voluntary act would be either necessary or impossible. And this contradicts choice. We say: It is denied, for necessity by choice is a confirmation of choice, not contradictory to it. Also, it is refuted by the acts of the Creator, Glorious is His mention – because His knowledge and will relate to His acts, so it would follow that His act is necessary upon Him.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection which argues that if God's knowledge and will determine the act, the voluntary act becomes either necessary (if God wills its occurrence) or impossible (if He wills its non-occurrence), which contradicts human choice. The author responds by denying the premise, asserting that necessity arising from choice actually confirms choice; it is not a contradiction. He supports this with an argument from analogy: God's own acts are known and willed by Him, yet that does not make them necessary upon Him in a way that negates His freedom. Thus, the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Necessity that arises from one's own choice does not negate freedom; it confirms it, as exemplified by God's own acts.

Why Acquired Evil is Blameworthy but Created Evil is Not
Answers why kasb of evil is blameworthy but divine creation of evil is not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between acquired evil (kasb) and created evil, and why only human acquisition incurs blame while divine creation does not.

Core Idea
Blame attaches to human acquisition, not divine creation.

This passage addresses a key objection to the Ash'ari position on evil: How can the same action be evil when acquired by humans but not when created by Allah? The answer hinges on the attribute of divine wisdom and the role of prohibition. Let's explore the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection presented in the passage: how can kasb of evil be blameworthy while creation of evil is not? Notice the contrast being drawn.
The objector assumes that if creation is not blameworthy, then acquisition should also not be, since both involve the same act.
2
Analyze the answer's first part
The answer begins by affirming that the Creator is wise and that every creation has a praiseworthy consequence, even if hidden. This removes blame from divine acts.
Think of examples like toxic plants that have medicinal benefits – we may not see the good immediately.
3
Analyze the answer's second part
The answer contrasts the human acquirer, who may do good or evil and acts against prohibition. This makes his acquisition of evil blameworthy.
The presence of a divine command and prohibition is crucial for blame.
4
Summarize the distinction
Now that you've seen both parts, articulate why the same act differs in blame based on the agent. This reinforces the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فكيف كان كسب القبيح قبيحا سفها، موجبا لاستحقاق الذم والعقاب، بخلاف خلقه . قلنا : لأنه قد ثبت أن الخالق حكيم لا يخلق شيئا إلا وله عاقبة حميدة، وإن لم نطلع عليها، فجزمنا بأن ما نستقبحه من الأفعال قد يكون له فيها حكم ومصالح ، كما في خلق الأجسام الخبيثة الضارة المؤلمة، بخلاف الكاسب، فإنه قد يفعل الحسن، وقد يفعل القبيح فجعلنا كسبه للقبيح مع ورود النهى عنه قبيحا سفها موجبا لاستحقاق الذم والعقاب.
If it is said: 'How is it that the acquisition (kasb) of evil is evil and foolish, necessitating deserving blame and punishment, contrary to its creation?' We say: Because it has been established that the Creator is wise; He does not create anything except that it has a praiseworthy consequence, even if we do not discover it. So we are certain that what we consider evil of actions may have in them wisdom and benefits, as in the creation of harmful, painful, and impure bodies. This is contrary to the acquirer, for he may do good and may do evil. So we consider his acquisition of evil, with the prohibition against it, evil, foolish, necessitating desert of blame and punishment.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This is another objection-answer pair. The objector questions how the same action can be evil when acquired by man but not evil when created by God. The answer has two parts: (1) God is wise and creates nothing without a good purpose, even if we do not see it; thus, what appears evil in creation may have hidden benefits. (2) Man, on the other hand, acts without such wisdom, and when he acts against the divine prohibition, his act is evil and blameworthy. So the difference lies in the agent's nature and context.

Key Terms
القبيح
السفه
الحكيم
العاقبة الحميدة
المصلحة
النهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that if God creates evil actions, then He must be unjust or blameworthy for them.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God creates everything with a wise purpose and praiseworthy outcome, even if unseen. Therefore, divine creation is never evil or blameworthy; blame arises only from human acquisition against divine commands.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the creation of evil not blameworthy?
A) Because God is not subject to any law.
B) Because the Creator is wise and creates nothing without a praiseworthy consequence.
C) Because creation does not involve choice.
D) Because the acquirer might also create.

Key Takeaway
The blameworthiness of an act depends on the agent (God or human) and the presence of prohibition, not on the act itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Divine Pleasure and the Classification of Actions
Defines hasan and qabih in terms of divine pleasure and cites Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine will (irada) and divine pleasure (rida) and how it classifies actions into good (hasan) and evil (qabih).

Core Idea
God wills all things but only approves the good.

In Islamic theology, a crucial distinction is made between what God wills (irada) and what God approves (rida). This passage from the creed clarifies that while evil actions occur by God's decree, they are not objects of His pleasure. The Quranic verse 'He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Zumar:7) anchors this distinction in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the parenthetical phrasing reminiscent of commentary style.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines 'hasan' (good) as that which is done by God's pleasure, and 'qabih' (evil) as that which attracts blame and punishment. Locate these definitions.
3
Recognize the key terms
Pay attention to the terms rida, irada, mashiah, taqdir, husn, and qubh. They are the building blocks of this teaching.
These terms appear in the concluding summary.
4
Understand the distinction
The author explains that God's will, desire, and decree encompass everything, but His pleasure, love, and command only apply to good. This is the core insight.
Meditate on the Quranic proof: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( برضاء الله تعالى) أى بإرادته من غير اعتراض . (والقبيح منها ) وهو ما يكون متعلق الذم في العاجل والعقاب في الآجل . (ليس برضائه لما عليه من الاعتراض . قال الله تعالى ﴿ وَلَا يَرْضَى لعباده الكفر ﴾ [الزمر: ٧] يعنى أن الإرادة والمشيئة والتقدير يتعلق بالكل والرضا والمحبة والأمر لا يتعلق إلا بالحسن دون القبيح.
(With the pleasure of Allah Most High) meaning by His will without objection. (And the evil of them) that which is subject to blame in this world and punishment in the Hereafter. (Not by His pleasure due to the objection to it. Allah Most High said: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' [Zumar: 7] meaning that will, desire, and decree encompass everything, while pleasure, love, and command do not encompass except the good without the evil.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines good and evil actions in relation to divine pleasure. The author explains that 'by the pleasure of Allah' means by His will, but that does not imply approval. Evil actions are blameworthy and punishable. The Quranic verse confirms that Allah does not approve (yarda) disbelief for His servants. The author distinguishes between divine will (irada, mashiah, taqdir) which encompasses all things, and divine pleasure (rida), love (mahabba), and command (amr) which apply only to good actions.

It Is Not Obligatory Upon Allah to Do What Is Best
Proves that Allah is not obligated to do the most beneficial for servants, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is not obligatory upon Allah to do what is most beneficial for His servants, and how the Mu'tazila's position leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
Allah is not obligated to do the best for every creature; that would undermine His grace and power.

The Mu'tazila argued that Allah must always do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. In this passage, the author refutes this by showing the absurd consequences of such a view. By examining each reductio ad absurdum, you will see why Islamic theology holds that Allah's actions are not constrained by human notions of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated pattern of 'otherwise...' (وإلا) in the reductio arguments.
2
Identify the main thesis and the reductio arguments
The passage begins with the thesis that it is not obligatory upon Allah to do what is more beneficial. Then it lists several consequences that would follow if it were obligatory. List these consequences.
There are five distinct absurd consequences.
3
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazila
After listing consequences, the author criticizes the Mu'tazila for their weak reasoning and relying on analogy between Creator and creatures. Then he answers their main objection that leaving the more beneficial would be miserliness.
The answer is that Allah has the right to withhold, and His actions are just and wise.
4
Reflect on the question of obligation
The author ends by questioning what 'obligation' can mean for Allah, since there is no superior over Him. Think about why this question is important for rejecting the Mu'tazilite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس يجب على الله تعالى ما هو أصلح للعبد
( وما هو الأصلح للعبد ، فليس ذلك بواجب على الله تعالى وإلا لما خلق الكافر الفقير المعذب في الدنيا والآخرة، ولما كان له منه على العباد، واستحقاق شكر في الهداية. وإفاضة أنواع الخيرات لكونها أداء للواجب .
ولما كان امتنان الله على النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام فوق امتنانه على أبي جهل لعنه الله. إن فعل بكل منهما غاية مقدوره من الأصلح له، ولما كان لسؤال العصمة والتوفيق وكشف الضراء والبسط في الخصب والرخاء معنى، لأن ما لم يفعله في حق كل واحد فهو مفسدة له، يجب على الله تعالى تركها، ولما بقى في قدرة الله تعالى بالنسبة إلى مصالح العباد شيء، إذ قد أتى بالواجب .
ولعمرى إن مفاسد هذا الأصل - أعنى وجوب الأصلح (۱)، بل أكثر أصول المعتزلة - أظهر من أن يخفى وأكثر من أن يحصى. وذلك لقصور نظرهم فى المعارف الإلهية، ورسوخ قياس الغائب على الشاهد في طباعهم. وغاية تشبثهم في ذلك : أن ترك الأصلح يكون بخلا وسفها.
وجوابه : أن منع ما يكون : حق المانع. وقد ثبتت بالأدلة القاطعة كرمه وحكمته ولطفه، وعلمه بالعواقب يكون محض عدل وحكمة . ثم ليت شعرى ما معنى وجوب الشيء على الله تعالى ؟ إذ ليس
It is not obligatory upon Allah Almighty what is more beneficial for the servant. And what is the most beneficial for the servant is not obligatory upon Allah Almighty; otherwise, He would not have created the disbelieving poor person tormented in this world and the Hereafter, and He would not have had any grace upon the servants, nor deserving of gratitude for guidance, nor the bestowal of various kinds of good things, because they would be payment of an obligation. And the grace of Allah upon the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, would not be greater than His grace upon Abu Jahl, may Allah curse him, if He had done for each the utmost of His power of what is most beneficial for him. And the asking for protection, success, removal of hardship, and expansion in abundance and ease would have no meaning, because what He has not done for each person would be harmful for him, so it would be obligatory upon Allah to leave it. And nothing would remain in Allah's power with respect to the interests of the servants, since He would have already done the obligatory. By my life, indeed the corruptions of this principle – I mean the obligation of the most beneficial, nay, most of the principles of the Mu'tazila – are more apparent than to be hidden and more numerous than to be counted. That is because of the deficiency of their insight in divine sciences and the rootedness of analogizing the unseen upon the witnessed in their natures. Their utmost clinging in that is that leaving the most beneficial would be miserliness and foolishness. The answer to it is that withholding what is one's right is the right of the one who withholds. And it has been established by decisive proofs that His generosity, wisdom, kindness, and knowledge of consequences are pure justice and wisdom. Then I wish I knew what is the meaning of obligation of something upon Allah Almighty? Since there is no superior over Him.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage proves that Allah is not obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants, a doctrine held by the Mu'tazila. The author begins with the thesis: it is not obligatory upon Allah to do what is more beneficial for the servant. He then argues by reductio ad absurdum: if it were obligatory, then many impossible consequences would follow. First, Allah would not have created the disbelieving poor person who suffers in both worlds, because that would contradict His doing the most beneficial. Second, Allah would have no grace upon servants, because His acts would be mere payment of a debt, not generosity. Third, the grace upon the Prophet would not be greater than upon Abu Jahl, if each received maximum benefit. Fourth, asking for divine protection and relief would be meaningless, because whatever Allah does not do for each person would be harmful and must be avoided. Fifth, nothing would remain in Allah's power concerning the interests of servants, since He would have already done all that is obligatory. The author then declares that the consequences of this Mu'tazilite principle are manifestly absurd. He diagnoses the cause: their deficient understanding of divine matters and their habit of analogizing Allah to creatures. The Mu'tazila cling to the idea that leaving the more beneficial would be stingy and foolish. The author answers that Allah has the right to withhold what He wills; His generosity, wisdom, and knowledge of outcomes are proven by decisive proofs, and His actions are pure justice and wisdom. Finally, he questions whether 'obligation' can even be conceived regarding Allah, since there is no authority above Him to impose it.

Key Terms
الأصلح
وجوب
اللطف
الحكمة
المعتزلة
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah must always do what is best for each individual in every situation.
Correction: Allah is not obligated to do the most beneficial for each person; His actions are based on wisdom and justice, not human notions of obligation.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence the author mentions if doing the most beneficial were obligatory upon Allah?
A) Allah would not have created the disbelieving poor person tormented in both worlds.
B) Allah would have more grace upon Abu Jahl than the Prophet.
C) The Prophet would not have been guided.
D) The Mu'tazila would be correct.

Key Takeaway
Allah is not obligated to provide the best outcome for each person; His actions are based on wisdom and justice, not obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience hardship or see others suffering, do you ever question Allah's wisdom, thinking He should have done something better for you? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? Will you commit to trusting Allah's wisdom even when you cannot see the benefit?
?
Do you sometimes feel that Allah 'owes' you something because of your good deeds or prayers? How does the truth that Allah is not obligated to do what is best for you change your attitude towards gratitude and reliance on Him? What would it mean to live with that awareness today?
?
The lesson shows that even asking for protection and success would be meaningless if Allah were obligated to give you what is best. Reflect on your own du'as: Do you sometimes pray as if you are reminding Allah of His duty? How can you transform your du'a into a humble request from His grace, not a demand? Will you check your heart in your next prayer?

The Absurd Consequences of Obligating the Best for the Servant
Taftazani enumerates five absurd consequences of the Mu'tazilite principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite principle that God must do what is best for every servant leads to absurd conclusions that contradict clear theological truths.

Core Idea
If God were obligated to give the best, no one would be disbeliever, poor, or tormented.

Taftazani begins his refutation by taking the Mu'tazilite principle at face value and showing where it leads. If God is truly obligated to do what is best for each servant, then many undeniable realities—like the existence of disbelievers, the need for supplication, and the distinction between Prophet and Abu Jahl—become impossible. This lesson walks through each consequence to see why the principle collapses under its own weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Notice the repeated 'otherwise' clauses that introduce each consequence.
The passage uses conditional reasoning: if X (obligation), then Y (absurd result).
2
List the five consequences
Identify each of the five consequences that would follow if God were obligated to give the best. Write them down in your own words.
The consequences are: no disbelievers/poor, no favor from God, Prophet and Abu Jahl equal, supplication meaningless, God's power exhausted.
3
Reflect on why each is impossible
For each consequence, ask yourself: Is this actually true? Why can't it be? This reveals the principle's falsity.
For example, disbelievers exist, so the principle cannot hold.

ليس يجب على الله تعالى ما هو أصلح للعبد
( وما هو الأصلح للعبد ، فليس ذلك بواجب على الله تعالى وإلا لما خلق الكافر الفقير المعذب في الدنيا والآخرة، ولما كان له منه على العباد، واستحقاق شكر في الهداية. وإفاضة أنواع الخيرات لكونها أداء للواجب .
ولما كان امتنان الله على النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام فوق امتنانه على أبي جهل لعنه الله. إن فعل بكل منهما غاية مقدوره من الأصلح له، ولما كان لسؤال العصمة والتوفيق وكشف الضراء والبسط في الخصب والرخاء معنى، لأن ما لم يفعله في حق كل واحد فهو مفسدة له، يجب على الله تعالى تركها، ولما بقى في قدرة الله تعالى بالنسبة إلى مصالح العباد شيء، إذ قد أتى بالواجب .
ولعمرى إن مفاسد هذا الأصل - أعنى وجوب الأصلح (۱)، بل أكثر أصول المعتزلة - أظهر من أن يخفى وأكثر من أن يحصى. وذلك لقصور نظرهم فى المعارف الإلهية، ورسوخ قياس الغائب على الشاهد في طباعهم. وغاية تشبثهم في ذلك : أن ترك الأصلح يكون بخلا

معناه استحقاق تاركه الذم والعقاب. وهو ظاهر ، لا لزوم صدوره عنه بحيث لا يتمكن من الترك، بناء على استلزامه محالا من سفه أو جهل أو عبث أو بخل، أو نحو ذلك، لأنه رفض لقاعدة الاختيار وميل إلى الفلسفة الظاهرة العوار.
Commentary on the Nasafī Creed. It is not obligatory upon God Most High to do what is best for the servant. (And what is best for the servant, that is not obligatory upon God Most High; otherwise He would not have created the disbeliever, the poor, the tormented in this world and the Hereafter, and He would not have any favor upon the servants, nor deserve gratitude for guidance and bestowal of kinds of good things because they are performance of the obligatory. And the favor of God upon the Prophet (peace and salutation be upon him) would not be above His favor upon Abu Jahl (may God curse him) if He did for each the utmost of His power in what is best for him, and the supplication for protection and success and removal of hardship and expansion in fertility and plenty would have no meaning, because what He did not do for each one is harmful to him, and God Most High must abandon it, and nothing would remain in the power of God Most High regarding the interests of the servants, since He has done the obligatory. By my life, the corruptions of this principle — I mean the obligation of the best (1), rather most principles of the Mu'tazila — are more evident than to be hidden and more than to be counted. That is due to the deficiency of their consideration in divine doctrines and the rootedness of analogy of the unseen to the witnessed in their natures. The utmost of their clinging in that is that leaving the best would be stinginess and folly. Its answer: that prevention is the right of the preventer. His generosity, wisdom, kindness, and knowledge of consequences have been established by decisive proofs and are pure justice and wisdom. Then would that I knew what is the meaning of obligation of a thing upon God Most High? For it is not (1) it is one of the most important principles of the Mu'tazila – refer to their principles in the book al-Murshid al-Amin to the beliefs of the Muslims and polytheists, by our authorship. Commentary on the Nasafī Creed. its meaning: that the one who leaves it deserves blame and punishment. That is apparent, not the necessity of its occurrence from Him such that He cannot abandon it, based on its entailing an impossibility like folly, ignorance, frivolity, stinginess, or the like, because it is a rejection of the principle of choice and a leaning toward philosophy whose defect is apparent.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Muʿtazilite principle that God is obligated to do what is best (al-aṣlaḥ) for His servants. The author begins by stating the position: it is not obligatory upon God. He then demonstrates the absurd consequences if it were obligatory: God would not have created the disbelieving poor who are tormented, there would be no favor from God, supplication would be meaningless, and God’s power would be exhausted by duty. Next, he criticizes the principle itself, noting its many corruptions and attributing the Muʿtazilite error to limited insight and excessive analogy of the unseen to the witnessed. Their main argument is that leaving the best would be stinginess or folly. The author answers that God’s prevention of something is His right, and His attributes of generosity, wisdom, and kindness are proven, making His actions pure justice. Finally, he questions the very meaning of obligation on God: it does not imply blame for leaving it, nor does it imply inability to omit, because that would negate free will and resemble defective philosophy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a consequence of the principle that God must do what is best for every servant?
A) Disbelievers would not exist.
B) The Prophet's favor above Abu Jahl would be meaningless.
C) Supplication would be meaningless.
D) God's power would be infinite.

Key Takeaway
If God were obligated to give the best, the realities of disbelief, poverty, supplication, and gradation of favors would be impossible, proving the principle false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or see a disbeliever prospering, do you secretly question God's justice? How does this lesson's refutation of the 'obligation of the best' confront that doubt, and what will you do when that thought arises?
?
You have supplicated for something that never came. Did you think God was obligated to answer? After this lesson, how will you view the supplications that went unanswered?
?
Consider the favor of guidance in your own life. Are you grateful to God as a gift, or do you feel entitled because of your own efforts? How does the lesson's point about God having no favor if He were obligated change your sense of gratitude?
